[٣] قوله : ما ذكر لكليهما ؛ أي ما ذكر لكل من المشتري والبائع، وهو أن يقال للمشتري : إما أن ترضى بالثمن الذي ادعاه البائع، وإلا فسخنا البيع، وللبائع : إما أن تُسَلّم ما ادعاه المشتري وإلا فسخنا البيع. [٤] قوله : وحُلف ... الخ ؛ يعني بدأ القاضي بيمين المشتري في الصور الثلاث، لو بيع عين بدين، وهذا قول محمد وأبي يوسف آخراً ، ورواية عن أبي حنيفة
He rules for the one who presents proof. If both present proof, he rules for the one who proves the increase. If they differ on both (price and goods), then the seller's proof is regarding the price, and the buyer's proof is regarding the goods. He rules for the one who presents proof. If both present proof, he rules for the one who proves the increase. That is the seller if the dispute is over the amount of the price, and the buyer if the dispute is over the amount of the goods. (If they differ on both, as when the seller says, 'I sold this single slave for two thousand,' and the buyer says, 'No, rather you sold the two slaves for one thousand,' then the seller's proof is regarding the price, and the buyer's proof is regarding the goods. If you owe a hundred dinars and he presents evidence, the buyer is bound by the sale of the slave, and the seller's evidence is accepted over the buyer's, because the buyer's right to the slave girl is established by their agreement, and the dispute is only about the seller's right. The seller's evidence establishes right for himself in the slave, while the buyer's evidence negates that, and evidence is for establishing, not negating. Al-Samani mentioned this in 'Kamil al-Diraya'.) [1] His statement: 'He rules...' etc. means the judge rules for the one who presents evidence, because he illuminates his claim with proof, while the other's claim remains bare. Evidence is stronger because it obligates the judge to rule, unlike the bare claim, which does not obligate him. [2] His statement: 'barhana' – it is said: 'barhana alayhi' i.e., he presented the proof. Thus in 'al-Sirah'. Al-Burhan: proof and its clarification. It is said the nun is extra, and it is said it is original. Al-Azhari narrated both opinions, saying in the chapter on triliterals: the nun is extra, and their term 'barhana' is a coining. The correct thing is to say 'abraha' if he brings proof, as Ibn al-A'rabi said. And he said in the chapter on quadriliterals: 'barhana' if he brings his proof. Al-Jawhari restricted it to being original, and al-Zamakhshari restricted it to what was narrated from Ibn al-A'rabi, saying: al-burhan is proof derived from 'barahrahah', which is the white among slave girls, just as 'sultan' is derived from 'sulayt'. Thus in al-Misbah al-Munir. [3] His statement: 'He rules for the one who proves the increase' because there is no opposition regarding the increase; the evidence that establishes the lesser does not address the increase, so the evidence that establishes the increase is free from opposition. (1) Kamil al-Diraya (q 596). (2) Al-Misbah al-Munir (p. 46). And if both are unable, each is pleased with the increase claimed by the other, otherwise they take oaths, and the buyer swears first. And if both are unable, each is pleased with the increase claimed by the other, otherwise they take oaths. So his statement: 'And if both are unable' refers to the three scenarios: i.e., whether the dispute is about the price, or the goods, or about both. If the dispute is about the price, it is said to the buyer: 'Either you agree to the price claimed by the seller, or we nullify the sale.' If the dispute is about the goods, it is said to the seller: 'Either you deliver what the buyer claims, or we nullify the sale.' If the dispute is about both, what was mentioned is said to both. If each agrees to the other's statement, it is clear; otherwise they take oaths. And the buyer swears first in the three scenarios. [1] His statement: 'And if both are unable...' etc. means that if the seller and buyer are unable in the three scenarios to present evidence, then if each agrees to the counterpart of the other in it, and if neither agrees to the other's claim, the judge administers an oath to each one of them regarding the other's claim. If it is before delivery, it is based on analogy (qiyas) because each is a denier. After delivery, it is based on juristic preference (istihsan) because the buyer does not claim anything, since the goods are in his possession. Only the seller's claim for an increase in price remains, and the buyer denies it, so his oath suffices. But we know it by the text (nas), which is his statement: 'The two parties to a sale, if they differ and the goods are still in existence, and they have no evidence, then the statement is that of the seller, or they rescind.' Narrated by al-Darimi and Ibn Majah. [2] His statement: 'It is said...' etc. It is said to them only because the aim is to end the dispute, and this is a means to it; perhaps they will desire the sale rather than rescission, so they consent when they know that. [3] His statement: 'What was mentioned to both' i.e., what was mentioned to each of the buyer and seller: it is said to the buyer, 'Either you agree to the price claimed by the seller, or we nullify the sale,' and to the seller, 'Either you deliver what the buyer claims, or we nullify the sale.' [4] His statement: 'And swears...' etc. means the judge begins with the buyer's oath in the three scenarios, in the case of a sale of a specific item for a debt. This is the statement of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf in his later opinion, and a narration from Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah deals with the rules of evidence and oaths when a buyer and seller dispute the terms of a sale. The commentary is expanding on the base text (matn). The passage is structured around three scenarios: dispute over the price, dispute over the goods, and dispute over both. The ruling states that if only one party brings evidence (beyyina), the judge rules for that party. If both bring evidence, the judge rules for the one who proves an increase (i.e., the party claiming more than the other acknowledges). For a dispute over price, the seller is claiming an increase; for goods, the buyer is claiming an increase. If the dispute is over both price and goods, the seller's evidence is preferred for price, and the buyer's evidence is preferred for goods. The commentary explains why: the buyer's right to the goods is already established by mutual agreement (since both agree the sale occurred), so the dispute is only about the seller's right to the price increase. Evidence is for establishing a right, not negating it; thus the seller's evidence is accepted. If neither party has evidence, the judge first asks each if they will accept the other's claim. If not, they both take oaths (tahāluf). The buyer swears first. This is the relied-upon position according to Muhammad and Abu Yusuf in his later opinion, and also a narration from Abu Hanifa. The commentary cites a hadith from al-Darimi and Ibn Majah as primary proof: 'The two parties to a sale, if they differ and the goods are still in existence and they have no evidence, then the statement is that of the seller, or they rescind.' The commentator notes that before delivery, the ruling is based on qiyas (analogy) because each is a denier; after delivery, it is based on istihsan because the buyer is in possession and does not claim anything, but the hadith overrides analogy. This passage is a clear example of how Hanafi jurists integrate textual proof (naql) with rational reasoning (aql) while always giving priority to the text.

Key Terms
برهان (burhān): Proof or evidence; synonymous with bayyinah in this context.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence; typically the testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females in property cases.
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oath-taking by both parties when neither has evidence and they cannot agree on the other's claim.
زيادة (ziyādah): The increase claimed by one party over what the other acknowledges.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When neither party has evidence in a dispute over the price of a sale, what is the first step the judge should take?
A) Immediately administer oaths to both parties.
B) Rule for the seller because the goods are with the buyer.
C) Ask each party if they accept the other's claim.
D) Rescind the sale automatically.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales dispute resolution, evidence for an increase prevails over evidence for a lesser amount; when evidence is absent, mutual oaths with the buyer swearing first lead to rescission.

The Order of Oaths in Sales Disputes: Intra-Hanafi Analysis
Explains the procedure of mutual oaths and resolves intra-Hanafi disagreement on who swears first

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi procedure for mutual oaths in sales disputes and identify which party swears first according to the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
In a dispute without evidence, the buyer swears first because he is first demanded for payment, expediting the benefit of refusal.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya explains the procedure for mutual oaths (tahalluf) when buyer and seller disagree over price, goods, or both, and neither has evidence. The Hanafi school has an internal ikhtilaf regarding who should swear first—the buyer or the seller. The relied-upon position is that the buyer swears first, supported by textual and rational arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Recognize the three possibilities: dispute over price (ثمن), over goods (مبيع), or over both. Each triggers a specific initial offer from the judge.
The judge first offers the parties to accept the other's claim or rescind, before administering oaths.
2
Understand the preliminary option
The judge informs the parties that they may accept the other's claim to avoid oaths. If they refuse, oaths are administered.
This is meant to encourage settlement.
3
State the Hanafi ruling on order
The relied-upon position is that the buyer swears first in all three scenarios for a sale of a specific item for debt.
In barter and currency exchange, the judge may begin with either party.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf initially held that the seller swears first, citing the hadith 'If two traders disagree, the statement is the seller's statement.' The later view (Muhammad, Abu Yusuf's later view, and a riwaya from Abu Hanifa) holds the buyer swears first.
The hadith is interpreted as applying to cases where the goods are still in hand, and the buyer's oath is already established by the principle 'the oath is upon the denier.'
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi position
The buyer swears first because he is first demanded for payment, so his denial is logically prior, and it expedites the benefit of nukul (obligation of price). This is the position adopted in al-Hidayah and later works.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if buyer refuses to swear, sale rescinded and price obligated; if seller refuses, sale rescinded but buyer keeps goods without immediate payment.

Book of Claims, Chapter of Mutual Oath: If they are unable (to provide evidence), each may accept what the other claims by way of an increase, otherwise they take mutual oaths, and the buyer swears first. His statement: 'If they are unable' refers to three scenarios: disagreement over the price, over the goods, or over both. If the disagreement is over the price, the buyer is told: either accept the price the seller claims, otherwise we rescind the sale. If the disagreement is over the goods, the seller is told: either deliver what the buyer claims, otherwise we rescind. If the disagreement is over both, each is told as mentioned. If each accepts the other's claim, it is clear; otherwise they take mutual oaths. The buyer swears first in all three scenarios. This means that if the buyer and seller are unable to provide evidence in the three scenarios, and if each does not accept the other's claim, the judge administers an oath to each upon the other's claim. If this is before delivery, it is by analogy (qiyas) because each is a denier. After delivery, it is by istihsan because the buyer does not claim anything; the goods are in his possession, only the seller claims an increase in price and the buyer denies it, so his oath suffices. But we know this by the text: 'If two traders disagree and the goods are still in their original state, and there is no evidence between them, then the statement is the seller's statement, or they cancel the transaction.' Narrated by al-Darimi and Ibn Majah. They are told this because the purpose is to end the dispute, and this is a means to that end; perhaps they will prefer the sale to cancellation if they know this. The judge begins with the buyer's oath in the three scenarios, for a sale of a specific item for debt. This is the statement of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf in his later view, and a narration from Abu Hanifa. Because he is first demanded for the price, so his denial comes first, and also the benefit of nukul (refusal to swear) is expedited, which is the obligation of the price. In a barter sale (goods for goods) and in currency exchange (sarf), the judge may begin with whichever he wishes. Each swears to negate what the other claims, and there is no need to prove what he claims; this is the correct view. Thus in al-Hidayah and others. Abu Yusuf initially said: begin with the seller's oath because of what the authors of the four Sunan narrated: 'If two traders disagree, the statement is the seller's statement.' The Messenger of Allah ﷺ made the statement the seller's, which implies his oath suffices, but since it does not suffice, at least begin with it to achieve the benefit of specific mention. Al-Aqta' said in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri in response to this hadith: The seller is specifically mentioned because the buyer's oath is known and not problematic, due to his statement: 'The oath is upon the denier.' He remained silent about what was previously explained and what was problematic and not previously explained. And he swears each to negate what the other claims; for example, the seller swears: 'By Allah, I did not sell it for a thousand,' and the buyer swears: 'By Allah, I did not buy it for two thousand.' It is stated in al-Ziyadat: he swears: 'By Allah...'
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on mutual oaths (tahalluf) in sales disputes explains the procedure when buyer and seller disagree over the price, the goods, or both, and neither has evidence. The text is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya, and it clarifies the ruling and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding who should swear first. First, the judge gives both parties an option: to accept the other's claim or else the sale is rescinded. If they still disagree, each must swear an oath to negate the other's claim. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the buyer swears first. The text gives two reasons: (1) the buyer is first demanded for payment, so his denial comes first; (2) expediting the benefit of nukul (refusal to swear) which is the obligation to pay the price. The hadith 'the statement is the seller's statement' is cited by Abu Yusuf (in his earlier view) to argue the seller should swear first, but the commentator refutes this by noting that the hadith is interpreted differently: the seller is mentioned because the buyer's oath is already known from the principle 'the oath is upon the denier'. In barter and currency exchange, the judge may begin with either. Each party swears to negate the other's claim, not to prove their own. This is the correct view as stated in al-Hidayah and others.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahalluf): Mutual oath-taking between buyer and seller when they disagree and lack evidence.
نكول (nukul): Refusal to take an oath, which results in the opponent's claim being upheld.
الصور الثلاث (al-suwar al-thalath): The three scenarios of dispute: over price (ثمن), over goods (مبيع), or over both.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The seller swears first
B) The buyer swears first
C) The judge swears both simultaneously
D) No oath is required

Key Takeaway
The buyer swears first in sales disputes without evidence, as the later Hanafi position holds, to expedite the resolution and based on the principle that the oath is upon the denier.

Priority of Oaths: Buyer First in Price Disputes
Commentary on order of tahalif: buyer first

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for the order of oaths in price disputes and explain the two reasons given in the text.

Core Idea
In a price dispute, the buyer's oath comes first because he is the first demanded and to hasten the nukul penalty.

When two parties to a sale dispute the price and have no evidence, the judge orders mutual oaths. But whose oath comes first? This lesson examines the Hanafi position that the buyer should swear first, supported by two legal reasons, and how this view withstands an objection based on a hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The judge demands oaths when both parties lack evidence. The question is: which party swears first?
2
State the Hanafi position
The buyer swears first, not the seller. This is the ruling in al-Hidayah and other core texts.
The text says 'he begins with the oath of the buyer' and marks it as correct (al-sahih).
3
Analyze the two reasons
First: the buyer is first demanded the price, so his denial is prior. Second: swearing the buyer first yields immediate benefit – if he refuses (nukul), the price becomes due. These justify priority.
The text uses 'تعجل فائدة النكول' – hastening the benefit of nukul.
4
Examine the objection and answer
Abu Yusuf (and Shafi‘i) cite a hadith: 'If the two differ, the statement is that of the seller.' The Hanafi response: that hadith applies when no oath is taken; it does not dictate priority in oath-taking. The general principle 'the oath is on the one who denies' (buyer denies the higher price) prevails.
The response quotes al-Aqta‘: the hadith specifies the seller because the buyer's oath is already clear from the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
لأنَّه يطالبُ أَوَّلاً بالثَّمن، فإنكاره أسبق، وأيضاً : يتعجَّلُ فائدة النُّكول، وهو وجوبُ الثَّمن، وفي بيع السلعة بالسلعة، وفي الصَّرف يبدأ القاضي بأيهما شاءَ،
ويَحْلِفُ كل على نفي ما يدعيه الآخر ولا احتياج إلى إثبات ما يدعيه ، هو
الصحيح .
وهو الصحيح. كذا في «الهداية»(۱)، وغيرها. وقال أبو يوسف : أولاً يبدأ بيمين البائع لما أخرجه أصحاب السنن الأربعة من قوله : «إذا اختلف المتبايعان، فالقولُ ما قاله البائع (۳) ، فرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جعل القول ما قاله البائع، وذلك يقتضي الإكتفاء بيمينه، لكن لا يكتفي بها فلا أقل من البداية بها ؛ لتحصل فائدة التخصيص بالذكر. والعلامةُ الأَقْطَعُ قال في «شرح مختصر القدوري» جواباً عن هذا الحديث : إنما خص البائع بالذكر ؛ لأنَّ يمين المشتري معلومة لا تشكل ؛ لقوله : واليمين على من أنكر»، وسكت عمّا تقدم بيانه وبين ما يشكل ولم يتقدم بيانه. انتهى بقدر الضرورة.
[١]قوله : لأنه... الخ ؛ يعني إنما يبدأُ بيمين المشتري لوجهين : الأول : إنه يطالب أولاً بالثمن، فيكون بادئاً في الإنكار، فَلَمَّا تقدَّمَ في الإنكارِ تقدم في الذي يترتب عليه. والثاني : إنّه يتعجل فائدة، وهو وجوب الثمن، واليمين إنما شرعت لفائدة النكول، وتقديم ما يتعجَّلُ فائدته أولى، ولو حلف البائع أوّلاً فحين نكول البائع تتأخر المطالبة بتسليم المبيع إلى زمان استيفائه الثمن ؛ لضرورة تأخر تسليم المبيع إلى زمانِ استيفاء الثّمن ؛ لأنه يقال له : أمسك المبيع إلى أن تستوفي الثمن.
[٢] قوله : ويحلف كلِّ على نفي ما يدعيه الآخر؛ بأن يحلف البائع : بالله ما باعه
بألف، ويحلف المشتري : بالله ما اشتراه بألفين، وقال في «الزيادات»: يحلف بالله ما
باعه بألف ، ولقد باعه بألفين، ويحلف المشتري : بالله ما اشتراه بألفين، ولقد اشتراه
بألف، يضم الإثبات إلى النفي تأكيداً. كذا في «الهداية».
وبيانُه : إنه لو حَلَفَ المشتري : بالله ما اشتراه بألفين، رُبَّما يحلف ويكون باراً في يمينه ، فلعله اشتراه بألف وتسعمئةٍ فيبطل حق البائع في الزيادة، وكذا البائع لو حلف : بالله ما باعه بألف رُبَّما يحلف الجواز أنه باعه بألف درهم، ويكون صادقاً في يمينه أنه لم
يبع بألف درهم. والأصح الاقتصار على النفي، ولا عبرة بذلك الوهم ؛ لأن البائع لو كان باعه بألف وتسعمئة لا يدعي البيع بألفين؛ لأنه يعلم أنَّ المشتري متى حلف على دعواه لا يُبالي بالحلف ؛ لأنه لا يحنث بيمينه، وكذا المشتري لو كان اشتراه بألف درهم لا يدعي الشراء بألف ؛ لأنه يعلمُ أنَّ البائع لا يبالي بالحلف على ألف ؛ لأنه لا يحنث في يمينه. كذا في «الكفاية»(٢). ولذا قال في «الهداية)) (۳) : والأصح الاقتصار على النفي؛ لأن الأيمان على ذلك وضعت ، دل عليه حديث القسامة : بالله ما قتلتُم، ولا علمتم له قاتلاً. انتهى. وهذا ما اختاره الفقهاء، وعليه العمل ؛ ولذا قال الشارح البارع : ولا احتياج إلى إثباتِ ما
يدعيه، هو الصحيح.
Cream of the Final for the Pinnacle of Care: Because he is first demanded the price, his denial is prior, and also he hastens the benefit of nukul (refusal to swear), which is obligation of the price. And in sale of goods for goods and in sarf, the judge begins with whomever he wishes. And each takes an oath on negating what the other claims, and there is no need to affirm what he claims; that is the correct [view]. And that is the correct [view]. Thus in al-Hidayah and others. Abu Yusuf said: First he begins with the oath of the seller because of what the Sunan al-Arba'ah related from his statement: 'If the two parties to a sale differ, the statement is that of the seller.' So the Messenger of Allah made the statement that of the seller, and that requires sufficing with his oath, but he does not suffice with it, so at least begin with it to achieve the benefit of specification by mention. And al-Allamah al-Aqta' said in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri in reply to this hadith: He specified the seller only because the oath of the buyer is known and not problematic, due to his statement: 'The oath is on the one who denies,' and he remained silent about what was previously explained and what is problematic and not previously explained. End to the extent of necessity. [1] His statement: 'Because... etc.' means he begins with the oath of the buyer for two reasons: first, he is demanded the price first, so he is foremost in denial, so when he precedes in denial, he precedes in what follows. Second, he hastens a benefit, which is obligation of the price, and oaths are legislated for the benefit of nukul, and presenting what hastens its benefit is more appropriate. And if the seller swore first, then upon the seller's nukul, the demand for delivery of the sold item is delayed to the time of collecting the price, due to the necessity of delaying delivery of the sold item to the time of collecting the price, because he is told: 'Hold the sold item until you collect the price.' [2] His statement: 'And each takes an oath on negating what the other claims,' meaning the seller swears: 'By Allah, I did not sell it for one thousand,' and the buyer swears: 'By Allah, I did not buy it for two thousand.' And it is stated in al-Ziyadat: He swears: 'By Allah, I did not sell it for one thousand, but I sold it for two thousand,' and the buyer swears: 'By Allah, I did not buy it for two thousand, but I bought it for one thousand,' adding affirmation to the negation as emphasis. Thus in al-Hidayah. And its explanation: If the buyer swears: 'By Allah, I did not buy it for two thousand,' he might swear and be truthful in his oath, for perhaps he bought it for one thousand nine hundred, thus invalidating the seller's right to the increase. Similarly, if the seller swears: 'By Allah, I did not sell it for one thousand,' he might swear that he did not sell it for one thousand dirhams and be truthful in his oath that he did not sell for one thousand dirhams. But the sound position is to restrict to negation, and no attention is paid to that delusion, because if the seller sold for one thousand nine hundred, he would not claim the sale for two thousand, since he knows that if the buyer swears on his claim, he will not care about swearing, because he will not be sinful in his oath. Similarly, if the buyer bought for one thousand dirhams, he would not claim the purchase for one thousand, because he knows that the seller will not care about swearing on one thousand, because he will not be sinful in his oath. Thus in al-Kifayah. Hence it is said in al-Hidayah: The sound position is to restrict to negation, because oaths are established for that; the hadith of qasamah indicates it: 'By Allah, you did not kill, nor do you know a killer for him.' End. This is what the jurists chose, and upon it is the practice; hence the brilliant commentator said: 'And there is no need to affirm what he claims; that is the correct [view].'
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the tahalif (mutual oath-taking) procedure when two parties dispute over the terms of a sale. The base text (matn) states that in a dispute over price, the judge begins with the buyer's oath. The commentary provides two reasons: first, the buyer is initially demanded to pay the price, so his denial comes first; second, swearing the buyer first yields the immediate benefit of the nukul penalty — if he refuses, the price becomes due. In contrast, for barter sales (goods for goods) and currency exchange (sarf), the judge may start with either party. Then the commentary addresses the proper wording of the oath: each party must take an oath negating the other's claim, without needing to affirm their own claim. This is the correct Hanafi position. A minority view from Abu Yusuf holds that the seller should give the first oath, citing the hadith 'If the two parties differ, the statement is that of the seller.' The commentary rebuts this by explaining that the hadith refers to the seller's statement being accepted when there is no oath, but when oaths are taken, the buyer's priority is based on the general principle 'the oath is on the one who denies', which is more general and not problematic. Regarding the content of the oath, some early authorities recommended adding an affirmative statement (e.g., 'I did not sell for X but for Y') for emphasis. However, the commentary dismisses this as unnecessary and potentially harmful: the added affirmation could allow a party to take an oath that is technically true but misleading. The relied-upon practice is to restrict the oath to negation only, based on the hadith of qasamah where the companions swore only negation. Thus the correct view is that no affirmation is needed — this is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
التحالف (al-tahalif): Mutual oath-taking between parties in a dispute when both have no evidence.
النكول (al-nukul): Refusal to take an oath after being required to do so by the judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The seller
B) The buyer
C) The judge may choose
D) Neither, the judge decides based on evidence

Key Takeaway
In price disputes, the buyer swears first to hasten resolution; the hadith cited for seller priority applies only when no oath is taken.

Content of the Oath: Negation Only, Not Affirmation
Commentary on content of tahalif: negation only

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for the wording of the oath in tahalif and explain why negation alone suffices.

Core Idea
Each party swears only to negate the other's claim; adding affirmation is unnecessary and may lead to false oaths.

Once the order of oaths is settled, the next question is: what should each party say in their oath? Some early jurists suggested adding an affirmation for emphasis, but the Hanafi school restricts the oath to simple negation. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this view and the evidence from the hadith of qasamah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What wording is required when each party swears? Should they only deny the other's claim, or also affirm their own?
2
State the Hanafi position
The correct view is to restrict the oath to negation only. Each party swears: 'By Allah, I did not sell/buy for that amount.' No affirmation is needed.
The text says 'ولا احتياج إلى إثبات ما يدعيه، هو الصحيح'.
3
Analyze the alternative view
Some authorities (like al-Ziyadat) recommend adding affirmation: e.g., 'I did not sell for X, but I sold for Y.' This is considered an emphasis.
The text quotes al-Ziyadat: 'يضم الإثبات إلى النفي تأكيدا'.
4
Evaluate the objection and counter
The problem with adding affirmation: a party could be technically truthful in his negation but still deceive. For example, if the buyer swears 'I did not buy for 2000' but actually bought for 1900, he is truthful but the seller's right is lost. The response: such hypothetical delusions are ignored; the parties would not lie in their own favor when they know the other can swear truthfully.
The text says 'لا عبرة بذلك الوهم' and explains why the scenario is unlikely.
5
Confirm the evidence
The relied-upon evidence is the hadith of qasamah: the companions swore only negation ('By Allah, you did not kill, nor do you know a killer'). This shows that oaths are established for negation only.
The text cites al-Hidayah: 'الأيمان على ذلك وضعت، دل عليه حديث القسامة'.

Cream of the Final for the Pinnacle of Care: Because he is first demanded the price, his denial is prior, and also he hastens the benefit of nukul (refusal to swear), which is obligation of the price. And in sale of goods for goods and in sarf, the judge begins with whomever he wishes. And each takes an oath on negating what the other claims, and there is no need to affirm what he claims; that is the correct [view]. And that is the correct [view]. Thus in al-Hidayah and others. Abu Yusuf said: First he begins with the oath of the seller because of what the Sunan al-Arba'ah related from his statement: 'If the two parties to a sale differ, the statement is that of the seller.' So the Messenger of Allah made the statement that of the seller, and that requires sufficing with his oath, but he does not suffice with it, so at least begin with it to achieve the benefit of specification by mention. And al-Allamah al-Aqta' said in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri in reply to this hadith: He specified the seller only because the oath of the buyer is known and not problematic, due to his statement: 'The oath is on the one who denies,' and he remained silent about what was previously explained and what is problematic and not previously explained. End to the extent of necessity. [1] His statement: 'Because... etc.' means he begins with the oath of the buyer for two reasons: first, he is demanded the price first, so he is foremost in denial, so when he precedes in denial, he precedes in what follows. Second, he hastens a benefit, which is obligation of the price, and oaths are legislated for the benefit of nukul, and presenting what hastens its benefit is more appropriate. And if the seller swore first, then upon the seller's nukul, the demand for delivery of the sold item is delayed to the time of collecting the price, due to the necessity of delaying delivery of the sold item to the time of collecting the price, because he is told: 'Hold the sold item until you collect the price.' [2] His statement: 'And each takes an oath on negating what the other claims,' meaning the seller swears: 'By Allah, I did not sell it for one thousand,' and the buyer swears: 'By Allah, I did not buy it for two thousand.' And it is stated in al-Ziyadat: He swears: 'By Allah, I did not sell it for one thousand, but I sold it for two thousand,' and the buyer swears: 'By Allah, I did not buy it for two thousand, but I bought it for one thousand,' adding affirmation to the negation as emphasis. Thus in al-Hidayah. And its explanation: If the buyer swears: 'By Allah, I did not buy it for two thousand,' he might swear and be truthful in his oath, for perhaps he bought it for one thousand nine hundred, thus invalidating the seller's right to the increase. Similarly, if the seller swears: 'By Allah, I did not sell it for one thousand,' he might swear that he did not sell it for one thousand dirhams and be truthful in his oath that he did not sell for one thousand dirhams. But the sound position is to restrict to negation, and no attention is paid to that delusion, because if the seller sold for one thousand nine hundred, he would not claim the sale for two thousand, since he knows that if the buyer swears on his claim, he will not care about swearing, because he will not be sinful in his oath. Similarly, if the buyer bought for one thousand dirhams, he would not claim the purchase for one thousand, because he knows that the seller will not care about swearing on one thousand, because he will not be sinful in his oath. Thus in al-Kifayah. Hence it is said in al-Hidayah: The sound position is to restrict to negation, because oaths are established for that; the hadith of qasamah indicates it: 'By Allah, you did not kill, nor do you know a killer for him.' End. This is what the jurists chose, and upon it is the practice; hence the brilliant commentator said: 'And there is no need to affirm what he claims; that is the correct [view].'

Dissolution of Sale by Judge After Tahalif
Ruling on dissolution of sale after mutual oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires the judge to dissolve a sale after tahalif, and distinguish it from the view that it dissolves automatically.

Core Idea
After mutual oaths, the judge must dissolve the sale; it does not dissolve by itself.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the legal procedure when a dispute over a sale remains unresolved after both parties take oaths (tahalif). The central ruling is that the judge actively dissolves the contract. Understanding why the Hanafi school insists on this judicial intervention, rather than automatic dissolution, reveals key principles about judicial authority and the nature of a corrupt (fasid) contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act? Dissolution of sale after tahalif. The ruling: it is wajib upon the judge to dissolve.
2
Locate the dispute
Some say it dissolves automatically; the first view is correct. Note: this is intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the proof
Why is judicial dissolution required? Because claims are unproven, sale becomes ambiguous (majhul), and a corrupt sale must be dissolved.
4
Consider the consequence
If automatic dissolution were accepted, the buyer's rights over a slave girl (if she was the item) would be lost; under judicial dissolution, the contract remains until the judge acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب التحالف
وفسخ القاضي البيع
۱۳۹
وفسخ القاضي البيع) : أي بعد التحالف
[١]قوله : وفسخ القاضي البيع ؛ أي بطلب أحدهما، أو بطلب كليهما، وقيل: ينفسخ، والأول هو الصحيح؛ لأنه لم يثبت ما ادعاه كلُّ واحدٍ منهما، فيبقى بيع مجهول، فيفسحه القاضي قطعاً للمنازعة، أو يقال : إنه إذا لم يثبت البدل بقي بيعاً بلا فاسد، ولا بُدَّ من الفسخ في البيع الفاسد ، فلو كان المبيع جارية وطئها، ولو
بدل،
وهو
فسد بنفس التحالف لم تحل له.
Book of Claims, Chapter of Mutual Oaths and Dissolution of Sale by the Judge. [Page 139] And the dissolution of the sale by the judge: i.e., after tahalif. [1] His statement: 'And the dissolution of the sale by the judge' means at the request of one of them or both; and it is said it dissolves automatically. The first is the correct [view] because the claim of each has not been proven, so the sale remains ambiguous; the judge dissolves it decisively to end the dispute. Or it is said: when the counter-value is not proven, the sale remains without a proper counter-value, and dissolution is necessary in a corrupt sale. If the sold item was a slave girl and he had intercourse with her, and if it is dissolved by the tahalif itself, she would not be permissible for him.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal effect after the tahalif (mutual oath-taking) procedure fails to resolve the dispute. The ruling is that the judge dissolves the sale. There is a minor ikhtilaf: whether the dissolution occurs automatically by the tahalif itself or requires a judicial decree. The relied-upon position (al-awwal huwa al-sahih) is that the judge must explicitly dissolve the sale. The reason is that after both parties take contradictory oaths, neither claim is proven; the contract becomes ambiguous regarding the price and delivery terms. A contract that lacks certainty of these essential elements is considered corrupt (fasid), and a fasid contract must be dissolved. The judge's dissolution is necessary to prevent ongoing dispute and confusion. A potential consequence is illustrated: if the sold item was a slave girl (jariyah) and the buyer had intercourse with her, if the sale were deemed automatically dissolved by the tahalif, the buyer would have no legal claim to her. However, since the correct view requires judicial dissolution, the sale remains valid until the judge intervenes, thus protecting the parties' rights during the interim.

Key Terms
الفسخ (al-faskh): Judicial revocation of a contract by the judge due to unresolved dispute after tahalif.
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is intrinsically valid but defective in an external condition, requiring dissolution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The judge dissolves the sale
C) The contract stands as agreed

Key Takeaway
After tahalif, the judge must dissolve the sale; it does not dissolve automatically.

Ruling on Refusing the Oath (Nukul) in Sale Disputes
Explains legal consequence when a party refuses the oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nukul and apply the legal basis of iqrar or badhl to determine the binding consequence.

Core Idea
Refusing the oath makes the opponent's claim binding by judicial decree, whether through tender or admission.

In a sale dispute, when one party refuses the oath after it is presented, the Hanafi school holds that the opponent's claim becomes binding. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the procedure and the underlying legal reasoning. The refuser is considered either to have admitted the claim or to have tendered the oath, but the binding effect requires a judicial decree.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is nukul? What is the consequence according to the main text? The text states: 'Whoever refuses the oath, the claim of the other is binding upon him.'
Nukul is refusal to take the oath after it is offered.
2
Understand the procedural order
The oath is first presented to the buyer. If he refuses, the seller's claim is binding. If the buyer swears, the oath is presented to the seller; if the seller swears, the sale is rescinded; if he refuses, the buyer's claim binds.
The procedure ensures both parties have a chance to swear.
3
Analyze the legal basis
The refuser is considered either an admitter (iqrar) or a tenderer (badhl). On tender, the effect is clear. On admission, it is an admission with a semblance of tender, so it requires the judge's decree to become binding.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on analogy.
4
Recognize the role of the judge
The binding effect is not automatic; it is connected to the judicial decree (qada). The text says: 'it becomes binding when the decree is connected to it.'
The judge's ruling gives the nukul its legal force.
5
Note the scope
This ruling applies when the dispute is specifically about the tender (i.e., the subject of the oath).
If the dispute is about something else, different rules may apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
ومَن نكلَ لَرْمَهُ دعوى الآخر
ومَن نكل لَزِمَهُ دعوى الآخر) : أي إذا عرض اليمينُ أَوَّلاً على المشتري ، فإن نكلَ لَزِمَهُ دعوى البائع ، فإن حَلَفَ يعرض اليمين على البائع ، فإن حَلَفَ يفسخُ البيع، وإن نكل لزمه دعوى المشتري.
(1) قوله : من نكل ... الخ ؛ يعني أنَّ مَن نكَلّ عن اليمين من البائع والمشتري لزمه دعوى صاحبه بالقضاء ؛ لأنه صار مُقراً أو باذلاً ، فَلَزِمه إذا اتصل به القضاء، وهو المراد بقوله : دعوى الآخر، أما على اعتبار البذل فالأمر بين.
وأما على اعتبار أنه إقرار ؛ فلأنه إقرارٌ فيه شبهة البذل، فلا يكون موجباً بانفراده بل بتقوية القضاء يكون حجَّةً ملزمة، وهذا كله إذا كان اختلافهما في البذل مقصوداً.
Summary of al-Nihaya for 'Umdat al-Ri'aya. And whoever refuses the oath, the claim of the other is binding upon him. And whoever refuses the oath, the claim of the other is binding upon him: meaning if the oath is first presented to the buyer, then if he refuses, the seller's claim becomes binding upon him. If he swears, the oath is presented to the seller; if he swears, the sale is rescinded; if he refuses, the buyer's claim becomes binding upon him. (1) His saying: 'Whoever refuses... etc.' means that whoever among the seller and buyer refuses the oath, the claim of his opponent becomes binding upon him by judicial decree, because he becomes either an admitter or a tenderer. So it becomes binding when the decree is connected to it, and that is meant by his saying 'the claim of the other'. As for considering it a tender, the matter is clear. As for considering it an admission, it is an admission with a semblance of tender, so it does not become a binding cause by itself, but with the strengthening of the judicial decree it becomes a binding proof. All this applies when their difference is specifically about the tender.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on nukul (refusing the oath) in a sale dispute. The main text states that whoever refuses the oath, the claim of the other party becomes binding upon him. The commentary clarifies the process: first the oath is presented to the buyer; if he refuses, the seller's claim is binding. If the buyer swears, the oath is presented to the seller; if the seller swears, the sale is rescinded; if he refuses, the buyer's claim is binding. The commentator then analyzes the legal basis: the refuser is considered either to have admitted the claim (iqrar) or to have tendered the oath (badhl). On the basis of tender, the effect is clear. On the basis of admission, it is an admission with a semblance of tender, so it does not become binding by itself but only through the judge's ruling. This applies when the dispute is specifically about the tender (i.e., the subject of the oath).

Key Terms
نكل (nukul): Refusal to take the oath in a judicial proceeding after it has been offered.
بذل (badhl): Tendering of the oath to the opposing party; the refuser is considered to have tendered the oath.
إقرار (iqrar): Admission; nukul is analogous to admission but with a semblance of tender.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is rescinded immediately.
B) The buyer's claim becomes binding.
C) The seller's claim becomes binding.
D) The judge must decide without further action.

Key Takeaway
Nukul (refusing the oath) makes the opponent's claim binding, but only after a judicial decree, based on the legal theories of tender and admission.

When Mutual Oath-Taking Applies and the Exception for Delivered Items
Ruling with proof and exception case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of tahlif, identify when it does not apply, and explain the hadith evidence with its authentication issues.

Core Idea
Tahlif applies when the commodity exists, but not when the dispute is about the delivered item itself.

This lesson continues the Hanafi discussion on mutual oath-taking (tahlif) when buyer and seller disagree. The text clarifies an important exception: if the dispute is about the identity or quantity of the delivered item (maqbud), the buyer's statement is accepted without tahlif. Then it provides the hadith proof and addresses concerns about its authenticity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and its ruling
Read the example of the ghee and skin. Recognize that the dispute is about the delivered item (the skin), not the price. The ruling is that the buyer's statement is accepted, and mutual oath-taking is not required.
This shows that tahlif is not for every dispute.
2
Understand the rationale
The text explains: the dispute about the price is secondary because it stems from the primary dispute about the skin. Since the skin is in the buyer's possession, his statement is trusted.
Hanafis rule that in disputes about the delivered item, the receiver's word is taken.
3
Locate the hadith proof
Find the prophetic hadith: 'If the two traders differ and the commodity is still in existence, and there is no evidence, the statement is the seller's, or they annul the sale.' This establishes tahlif when the commodity exists.
The hadith is cited from Ibn Mas'ud and narrated by Ibn Majah and Malik.
4
Evaluate the authenticity discussion
Ibn al-Jawzi criticizes the hadith as weak. The author of al-Tanqih defends it as hasan due to multiple chains. Hanafis accept it as proof.
This illustrates how Hanafis engage with hadith criticism.
5
Apply the usul principle of explicit wording
The commentary notes that this hadith's explicit wording indicates swearing the claimant, which is preferred over the famous hadith that only implies oaths for deniers.
This strengthens the Hanafi position that both parties take oaths.

Source Text

But if it is within another thing, like a man buying ghee from another man in a skin, and it is one hundred ratls for example, then he comes with the skin to return it to its owner and it weighs twenty ratls for example. The seller says: This is not my skin, and the buyer says: This is your skin. The statement is the buyer's regarding it being the skin, because it is a dispute about the delivered item, and the statement therein is that of the receiver regarding the delivery and the delivered item. Similarly, regarding the amount of the delivered item, even if it implies a difference in the price, because the price increases with the decrease of the skin and decreases with its increase. So the seller claims an increase in the price and the buyer denies it, and this difference is not considered for obligating mutual oaths, because the difference occurred as a result of their difference about the skin. Abu 'Ubaida did not hear from his father, nor 'Abd al-Rahman and al-Qasim heard from Ibn Mas'ud, nor 'Awn ibn 'Abdullah. Al-Daraqutni narrated it with different wordings and weak chains including Ibn 'Ayyash, Muhammad ibn Abi Layla, al-Hasan ibn 'Umara, and Ibn al-Marzaban, all weak. The author of al-Tanqih said: What appears is that the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud by all its chains has a basis; rather it is a hasan hadith that can be used as proof, but there is difference in its wording. This is supported by Malik's narration in al-Muwatta' as a balagh: Abu Mus'ab from Malik: It reached me that Ibn Mas'ud used to narrate that the Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'Any two traders who trade, the statement is what the seller says, or they annul the sale.' Thus in Nasb al-Raya. And in the narration of Ibn Majah: 'The two traders if they differ and the commodity is still in existence, and there is no evidence between them, the statement is what the seller says, or they annul the sale.' [2] His saying: 'If they differ...etc.'; It is said in Nata'ij al-Afkar: It has been established in books of usul that the explicit wording of the text is given preference over the indication of the text. So this hadith is preferable over the famous hadith, because this hadith by its explicit wording indicates swearing the claimant as well in our case. As for the famous hadith, it does not indicate by its explicit wording the non-swearing of the claimant absolutely; rather it indicates it by its indication, where it is understood from dividing the two proofs between the two opponents or from making the category of oaths upon the deniers.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage continues the topic of tahlif (mutual oath-taking). First, it presents a specific case to illustrate when tahlif is not applied: a buyer purchases ghee in a skin (ziqq) weighing 100 ratls, then returns the skin weighing only 20 ratls. The seller says 'This is not my skin' and the buyer insists it is. The ruling is that the buyer's statement is accepted regarding the skin because it is a dispute about the delivered item, and in such disputes the receiver's statement is taken. Even though this difference implies a difference in price (since the skin's weight affects the price calculation), this price difference is not considered for invoking tahlif because the primary dispute is about the skin itself. The text then provides the hadith evidence for tahlif, quoting the Prophet's ﷺ saying: 'If the two traders differ and the commodity is still in existence, the statement is the seller's, or they annul the sale.' The commentary addresses the authenticity of this hadith: Ibn al-Jawzi criticized it, but the author of al-Tanqih considers it hasan due to multiple chains. Finally, a point from Nata'ij al-Afkar is cited: the explicit wording of this hadith indicates that the claimant (here the seller) is also sworn, which is preferred over the famous hadith that only implies oaths are for deniers.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahlif): Mutual oath-taking by both buyer and seller when they dispute and the commodity exists.
زق (ziqq): A skin container used for storing liquids like ghee.
المقبوض (al-maqbud): The delivered item; in disputes about the delivered item, the receiver's statement is accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the buyer already admitted the seller's claim.
B) Because the dispute is about the delivered item, not the price.
C) Because the commodity was not in existence.
D) Because both parties had evidence.

Key Takeaway
Tahlif is wajib when the commodity exists and the dispute is about the price, but not when the dispute is about the delivered item; the receiver's word is taken instead.

Scope of Mutual Swearing in Sale Disputes: When Not Required
Commentary on the ruling that no mutual swearing occurs in disputes over term, option, or partial payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of disputes that do not trigger mutual swearing (tahāluf) in a sale and apply the Hanafi criterion of 'what establishes the sale' to distinguish them from disputes that do trigger it.

Core Idea
Mutual swearing applies only to the price and merchandise, not to ancillary terms like the payment term or option.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains when parties to a sale dispute do not engage in mutual oaths. The key distinction is whether the disagreement touches the very essence of the contract (the price and merchandise) or merely auxiliary conditions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the procedural rule of tahāluf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith basis
The ruling on mutual swearing is based on the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, which conditions it on the merchandise being still in existence and the parties differing. Understand the textual support and its strength.
The commentator notes some weakness in the chain but considers the hadith hasan due to multiple routes.
2
Distinguish the scope of the hadith
The explicit wording of the hadith mentions only when the merchandise is 'standing' and the parties differ. By implication, disputes not involving the merchandise or price do not trigger mutual swearing.
3
Analyze the analogy (qiyas)
The commentator analogizes disputes over term, option, and partial payment to a dispute over a reduction in price (al-hatt min al-thaman). In those parallel cases, mutual swearing is not required because the dispute does not affect the core of the contract.
The illa (effective cause) is that the dispute is about something other than the price and merchandise.
4
Apply the rule to concrete scenarios
Learn that disputes over the existence or amount of a deferred term, the existence or duration of an option, and whether part of the price was paid all fall outside the scope of mutual swearing. In each case the defendant's statement with oath is taken.
Contrast with disputes over the description or type of the price, which are treated as disputes over the price itself and thus require mutual swearing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التحالف
لكن التحالف هاهنا ثبت بقوله : «إذا اختلف المتبايعان والسِّلْعَةُ قائمة» قال ابن الجوزي في «التحقيق» : أحاديث هذا الباب فيها مقال، فإنّها مراسيل وضعاف، أبو عبيدة لم يسمع من أبيه ولا عبد الرحمن والقاسم لم يسمع من ابن مسعود له ولا عون بن عبد الله، وقد رواه الدارقطني بألفاظ مختلفة، وبأسانيد ضعيفة فيها ابن عيّاش ومحمد بن أبي ليلى، والحسـن بـن عمارة، وابن المرزبان له وكلهم ضعاف. انتهى.
وقال صاحب «التنقيح» : والذي يظهرُ أنَّ حديث ابن مسعود له بمجموع طرقه له أصل، بل هو حديث حسن يحتج به، لكن في لفظه اختلاف. انتهى. ويدلُّ على ذلك أنَّ مالكاً له أخرجه في «الموطأ» بلاغاً : قال أبو مصعب عن مالك ، بلغني أن بن مسعود له كان يحدث أنَّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : «أيما بيعين تبايعا، فالقول ما قال البائع ، أو يترادان». كذا في نصب الراية.
وفي رواية ابن ماجة : «البيعان إذا اختلفا والمبيعُ قائم بعينه، وليس بينهما بينة ، فالقول ما قال البائع أو يترادان». قال في «نتائج الأفكار» : قد تقرر في كتب الأصول : إن عبارة النص تترجّح على إشارة النص فحينئذ يكون هذا الحديث راجحاً على الحديث المشهور ؛ لأنَّ هذا الحديث يدلُّ بعبارته على استحلاف المدعي أيضاً فيما نحن فيه.
وأما الحديث المشهور فلا يدلُّ بعبارته على عدم استحلاف المدعي مطلقاً، بل إنما يدلُّ عليه بإشارته، حيث يفهم من تقسيم الحجتين للخصمين أو من جعل جنس الأيمان على المنكرين. انتهى.
(ولا تحالف) قوله : ولا تحالف ... الخ ؛ يعني أنهما لو اختلفا في الأجل ؛ سواء كان في نفس الأجل أو قدره، أو اختلفا في شرط الخيار سواءً كان في وجوده، بأن قال أحدهما : البيع بالخيار، والآخر ينكره، أو في مدته، أو اختلفا في قبض بعض الثمن أو كله ، بأن قال المشتري : أديت بعضه أو كله ، والبائع ينكرُ، فلم يتحالفا، والقولُ يكون للمنكير مع يمينه ؛ لأنَّ الشرعَ عَلَّقَ وجوب التحالف باختلاف المتبايعين.
وهو اسم مشتق من البيع، فيتعلق وجوب التحالف باختلافهما فيما يثبت به البيع، والبيع يثبت بالمبيع والثمن، وهذا اختلاف في غير المبيع والثمن ؛ لأن بانعدام ما نحن فيه من الأجل، وشرط الخيار، واستيفاء بعض الثمن أو كله لا يختل ما به قوام العقد، فأشبه الاختلاف في الحط من الثمن، وفيهما لا يجب التحالف، فكذا هاهنا. بخلاف الاختلاف في وصف الثمن ؛ مثل الجودة والرداءة، أو جنس الثمن ؛ مثل الدراهم والدنانير، فإنّ هذا الاختلاف بمنزلة الاختلاف في القدر، حتى يجري فيه التحالف ؛ لأنَّ ذلك يرجع إلى الاختلاف في نفس الثمن، فإنَّ الثمن دين في الذمة، وهو يعرفُ بالوصف بأنه جيّد أو وسط أو رديء، والأجل ليس كذلك ؛ لأنه ليس بوصف له ، ألا ترى أنَّ الثمن موجود بعد مضي الأجل، ولو كان وصفاً لتبعه.
Chapter of mutual swearing. But mutual swearing here is established by his statement: 'If the two parties to a sale differ and the commodity is still in existence...' Ibn al-Jawzi said in al-Tahqiq: The hadiths of this chapter have criticism; they are mursal and weak. Abu Ubayda did not hear from his father, nor did Abd al-Rahman and al-Qasim hear from Ibn Mas'ud, nor did Awn ibn Abd Allah. Al-Daraqutni narrated it with different wordings and weak chains including Ibn Ayyash, Muhammad ibn Abi Layla, al-Hasan ibn Umara, and Ibn al-Marzban, all weak. End. The author of al-Tanqih said: It appears that the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, through all its chains, has a basis; indeed it is hasan and can be used as proof, but there is variation in its wording. End. This is supported by Malik's narration in al-Muwatta as a balagh: Abu Mus'ab said from Malik, it reached me that Ibn Mas'ud used to narrate that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Any two sellers who have made a transaction, the statement is what the seller says, or they rescind.' As in Nasb al-Raya. And in the narration of Ibn Majah: 'The two sellers if they differ and the merchandise is still in existence, and there is no evidence between them, then the statement is what the seller says or they rescind.' He said in Nata'ij al-Afkar: It is established in books of usul that the explicit wording of a text outweighs its indication. So this hadith outweighs the well-known hadith because this hadith explicitly indicates that the claimant is also sworn in this matter. As for the well-known hadith, it does not explicitly indicate the absolute absence of swearing the claimant; rather it indicates it by implication, from the division of proofs between the two litigants or from placing all oaths on the deniers. End. (And no mutual swearing). His statement: 'and no mutual swearing' etc.; meaning if they differ regarding the term—whether regarding the existence of the term or its amount—or if they differ regarding a condition of option—whether regarding its existence, such as one says the sale is with option and the other denies it, or regarding its duration—or if they differ regarding the receipt of part or all of the price, such as the buyer says I paid part or all and the seller denies, then they do not engage in mutual swearing, and the statement is that of the denier with his oath. Because the Law has made mutual swearing obligatory upon the difference of the two parties to a sale. And it (mutual swearing) is a noun derived from sale, so the obligation of mutual swearing is attached to their difference regarding that by which the sale is established. The sale is established by the merchandise and the price. This difference is regarding something other than the merchandise and the price, because the absence of what we are discussing—the term, condition of option, and receipt of part or all of the price—does not disrupt the essence of the contract. So it resembles the difference regarding a reduction in price, in which no mutual swearing is required. Hence here as well. Contrary to the difference regarding the description of the price—like quality and poorness—or the type of price—like dirhams and dinars—for this difference is like the difference in amount, so mutual swearing occurs. Because that goes back to the difference in the price itself, for the price is a debt in the liability and is defined by description as good, medium, or poor. The term is not like that because it is not a description of it. Do you not see that the price exists after the passage of the term? If it were a description, it would follow it.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement "ولا تحالف" (and no mutual swearing) from the book of claims. The author first addresses the authenticity of the hadith that establishes mutual swearing when the commodity is still in existence after a dispute. He cites Ibn al-Jawzi's criticism of the hadith as weak, then counters with the view of the author of al-Tanqih who considers it hasan due to multiple chains. He then provides the hadith text from Malik and Ibn Majah. The commentator then cites Nata'ij al-Afkar to argue that this hadith, with its explicit wording, takes precedence over the more general principle that oaths are on the deniers, because explicit wording outweighs implied indication. 

Then the commentary explains the main ruling: If the buyer and seller differ on matters other than the price and merchandise themselves—such as the term (whether a term exists or its length), a condition of option (whether it exists or its duration), or whether the buyer paid part or all of the price—then there is no mutual swearing. Instead, the defendant (the one denying the claim) takes an oath and his statement is accepted. The reasoning is that the obligation of mutual swearing is derived from the word "sale," and sale is established by the merchandise and price. Disputes over these ancillary matters do not affect the core of the contract, so they are analogous to a dispute over a reduction in price (al-hatt min al-thaman), where no mutual swearing is required. In contrast, disputes over the description or type of the price (e.g., quality or coin type) are considered disputes over the price itself, so mutual swearing does apply. This distinction is based on the fact that a price in debt (dhimmah) is defined by description, while a term is not a description of the price.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual swearing between two parties in a dispute, where each takes an oath to support their claim.
الأجل (al-ajal): The deferred term or duration for payment in a sale contract.
شرط الخيار (sharṭ al-khiyār): A condition in a sale giving one or both parties the right to rescind the contract within a specified period.
الحط من الثمن (al-ḥaṭṭ min al-thaman): A reduction or discount from the agreed price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following disputes would NOT result in mutual swearing (tahāluf) according to this passage?
A) Dispute over whether the price is in dirhams or dinars
B) Dispute over the quality (jūdah/radā'ah) of the price
C) Dispute over whether the buyer paid part of the price
D) Dispute over the existence of the merchandise

Key Takeaway
Mutual swearing (tahāluf) in sale disputes is confined to the core elements of price and merchandise; ancillary matters like term, option, and partial payment are handled by accepting the defendant's oath alone.

Dispute over Term and Option: Hanafi vs. Zufar/Shafi'i on Mutual Oaths
Hanafi response to Zufar and Shafi'i on mutual oaths for term/option disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on mutual oaths in disputes over the payment term or option duration and distinguish it from the Shafi'i/Zufar position by identifying the key difference in legal reasoning.

Core Idea
The term is not a description of the price, so disputes about it require only one oath, not mutual oaths.

In sale contracts, when buyer and seller disagree about a payment term or an option condition, do they both swear? The Hanafi school says only the party denying the increased term or duration takes an oath, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i require mutual oaths. This passage explains why the difference matters and how the Hanafi reasoning hinges on the nature of the term as an ancillary condition, not a description of the price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Read the passage and identify the two types of disputes: (1) disagreement about the existence or length of the payment term (ajal), and (2) disagreement about the existence or length of an option period (khiyar).
The passage specifically mentions examples: buyer says 'deferred for a year,' seller says 'six months'; or one says 'option for three days,' the other says 'two days.'
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi ruling is that only the denier of the increase (munkir al-ziyada) takes an oath. In term disputes, the party claiming the longer term claims an increase; the other denies that increase and thus swears.
This applies whether the dispute is about the existence of the term or its duration.
3
Identify the opposing view
Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold that both parties must take mutual oaths (tahāluf) in such disputes, leading to rescission of the contract if their oaths contradict.
Their position is based on the text (nass) that orders mutual oaths when buyers and sellers differ, without specifying any exceptions.
4
Analyze the opposing proof
The opposition argues that a deferred price is less in monetary value than a spot price, so the dispute effectively concerns the price's value. They also invoke the general command for mutual oaths without differentiation.
This reasoning treats the term as part of the price's description (wasf).
5
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response denies that the term is a description of the price. It argues: if the term were a description, it would cease to exist when the price ceases, but a thing does not remain without its description. Since the term is temporary and separate, it does not affect the price's identity. The obligation of mutual oaths (tahāluf) is derived from the word 'sale' (al-bay') and applies only to disagreements about the sold item or the price itself, not to ancillary conditions like the term or option.
Thus, only the party denying the extra (whether term or option duration) swears.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الأجل، وشرط الخيار، وقبض بعض الثَّمن"، وحلف المنكر)، سواء اختلفا في أصل الأجل، أو في قدره، فقال المشتري : الثَّمنُ مؤجل، وأنكر البائع ، أو قال المشتري : الثَّمنُ مؤجل إلى سنة ، وقال البائع : بل نصف سنة، حلف منكر الزيادة، أو قال أحدهما : البيع بشرط الخيار، وأنكر الآخر، وقال أحدهما : لي الخيار إلى ثلاثة أيام، وقال الآخر : بل إلى يومين وقال زفره والشافعي له : يتحالفان في الأجل إذا اختلفا في أصله أو قدره ؛ لأن هذا في معنى الاختلاف في مقدار مالية الثمن، فإنّ المؤجل أنقص من الحال في المالية ؛ ولأنَّ النص أوجب التحالف عند اختلاف المتبايعين، ولم يفصل. والجواب : إنَّ الأجل ليس بوصف الثمن، إذ لو كان وصفاً له فذهب عند ذهابها، إذ الشيء لا يبقى بدون وصفه، ووجوب التحالف للمتبايعين، وهو اسم مشتق من البيع إلى آخر ما قرَّرناه آنفا، فصار كأنه قال : إذا اختلف المتبايعان في المبيع أو الثمن تحالفا.
In the term, and the condition of option, and receipt of some of the price, and the oath of the denier: whether they differ in the existence of the term or its amount, so the buyer says 'the price is deferred' and the seller denies, or the buyer says 'the price is deferred for a year' and the seller says 'rather half a year', the denier of the increase takes an oath; or one says 'the sale is with a condition of option' and the other denies, and one says 'I have the option for three days' and the other says 'rather two days'. And Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: they mutually take oaths regarding the term when they differ in its existence or amount, because this is in the meaning of a difference in the amount of the monetary value of the price, for the deferred is less in monetary value than the spot; and because the text (nass) required mutual oaths when the two sellers differ, and did not differentiate. The answer: the term is not a description of the price, for if it were a description of it, it would disappear when it disappears, for a thing does not remain without its description, and the obligation of mutual oaths is for the two sellers (mutabayi'an), and it is a name derived from sale, up to the end of what we explained above. So it is as if he said: if the two sellers differ about the sold item or the price, they take mutual oaths.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage addresses disputes between buyer and seller over the payment term (ajal) and the condition of option (khiyar al-shart). The Hanafi ruling, as stated, is that when they disagree about the existence or duration of a term (e.g., whether the price is deferred or cash, or for one year vs. six months), the denier of the increase (munkir al-ziyada) takes an oath. Similarly, for disputes about the duration of an option. This is because the term and option are temporary conditions, not part of the price itself. The text then presents the opposing view of Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i: they hold that the parties must perform mutual oaths (tahaluf) in such disputes. Their reasoning is that a deferred payment has a lower monetary value than a spot payment, so the dispute effectively concerns the value of the price; moreover, the general textual evidence (nass) about tahaluf when buyers and sellers differ does not distinguish between types of disagreement. The Hanafi response (الجواب) refutes this by arguing that the term is not a description (wasf) of the price. If it were, it would cease when the price ceases, but a thing cannot exist without its description. The term for mutual oaths (tahāluf) is derived from sale (bay'), so it applies only to disputes about the sold item or the price itself, not to ancillary conditions. Hence, the Hanafi position is that only the party denying the extra (whether the term or option duration) swears, not both.

Key Terms
الأجل (al-ajal): The payment term or period of deferment in a sale contract.
التحالف (al-tahāluf): The mutual oaths taken by buyer and seller when they disagree about the sold item or price.
حلف منكر الزيادة (halafa munkir al-ziyāda): The oath taken by the party who denies the increase in term or option duration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi sale dispute, if the buyer claims the price is deferred for one year and the seller claims it is for six months, who takes the oath?
A) Both parties take oaths.
B) The buyer takes an oath because he is claiming the longer term.
C) The seller takes an oath because he is denying the increase.
D) No oath is taken because the contract is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Only the denier of the increase swears in disputes over a payment term or option duration, because the term is not a description of the price.

Exception for Term Disputes in Salam
Clarifying that in salam, the claimant of the term takes oath unlike ordinary sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exceptional rule for term disputes in salam and distinguish it from ordinary sales.

Core Idea
In salam, the term is a condition of validity; thus the claimant takes the oath, but in ordinary sales the denier does.

We have already learned that when parties dispute a term in an ordinary sale, the denier of the increase takes the oath. But salam contracts have a special rule: the claimant of the term swears instead. This lesson unpacks that exception and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed element
The dispute is about the term (ajal) in a salam contract: whether it exists or its duration, not about whether it has passed.
Disagreement about the term's passing follows the general rule: the buyer's word is accepted.
2
Recall the general rule for ordinary sales
In a regular sale, if parties differ about an additional condition or term, the one who denies the increase takes the oath (al-yamin 'ala man yunkir).
That is because the seller is denying a claim that would impose an extra burden.
3
State the salam exception
In salam, if they dispute the term, the claimant (the one who asserts the term exists) takes the oath, according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
This is an exception; the rule flips.
4
Understand the rationale
The term is a condition (shart) of salam's validity. Omitting it invalidates the contract. When both parties proceeded, the default presumption is validity (zahir al-hal), which supports the claimant's assertion that a term was agreed.
If the term were not specified, the contract would be fasid, so they must have intended it.
5
Apply the distinction
In a salam dispute, always ask: is the term being claimed or denied? The one who claims it (buyer or seller) takes the oath. In an ordinary sale, the denier takes the oath.
This is a key nuance to avoid confusing the two types of sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : في الأجل ؛ أطلقه ليشمل الاختلاف في أصل الأجل وقدره، كما نبه عليه الشارح الله بقوله : سواء اختلفا في أصل الأجل وقدره. انتهى. وخرج الاختلاف في مضيه، فإنَّ القول فيه للمشتري ؛ لأنَّ الأجل حقه، وهو منكر استيفاء حقه. ويستثنى من الاختلاف في الأجل ما لو اختلفا في أجل السلم، بأن ادّعاه أحدهما ونفاه الآخر، فإنَّ القول فيه لمدَّعيه عند الإمام ؛ لأنه شرط ، وتركه مفسد، وإقدامهما يدل على الصحة، فشهد الظاهرُ لمدَّعيه بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإنّ القول لما فيه. ذكره العلامة الحموي.
His saying: 'regarding the term' – he made it absolute to include disagreement in the existence of the term and its amount, as the commentator indicated by his saying: 'whether they differ in the existence of the term and its amount.' End. And excluded is the disagreement about its passing (mady), for the word in that is for the buyer, because the term is his right, and he is denying the fulfillment of his right. And from the disagreement in the term, an exception is made for when they differ about the term in salam – one claims it and the other denies it – for the word in that is for the claimant according to Imam, because it is a condition, and leaving it invalidates (the contract), and their proceeding indicates validity, so the apparent supports the claimant, contrary to our case, where the word is as it is. Mentioned by 'Allama al-Hamawi.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This note clarifies the scope of the phrase 'في الأجل' (in the term). First, it is absolute, covering both disagreement about the existence of a term and its duration. Second, the note excludes the case where they disagree about whether the term has already passed (mady). In that case, the statement of the buyer is accepted because the term is his right and he is denying that his right has been fulfilled. Third, a special exception is made for salam contracts: if the parties differ about the existence or duration of the term in salam, the word (al-qawl) is for the claimant (the one who asserts the term), according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The reason is that in salam, the term is a condition (shart) of validity; omitting it would invalidate the contract. The fact that both proceeded with the contract indicates their intention for a valid contract, so the apparent circumstance (zahir al-hal) supports the claimant. This is opposite to the general rule in ordinary sales where the denier of the increase takes the oath. This exception is cited by al-Hamawi.

Key Terms
السلم (al-salam): A sale contract where the price is paid in advance for deferred delivery of goods; a forward contract.
القول لمدعيه (al-qawl li-mudda'īhi): The statement of the claimant is accepted (with an oath); the claimant here is the one who asserts the term exists or has a certain duration.
الأجل (al-ajal): The term or time period agreed for delivery in a salam contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer, because he is the claimant.
B) The seller, because he is the denier.
C) Both take oaths.
D) No oath needed; the contract is void.

Key Takeaway
In salam, the claimant of the term takes the oath because the term is a condition of validity, unlike ordinary sales where the denier swears.
