No Mutual Oaths After Destruction: The Hanafi Intra-School Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mutual oaths after destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on mutual oaths after destruction of sold item and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf from Muhammad al-Shaybani, identifying the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf) for each type of increase.

Core Idea
After the sold item is destroyed, the buyer's oath alone suffices for Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Muhammad demands mutual oaths, but both agree for disconnected non-produced increases.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute about procedural fairness when a sold item has been destroyed and the parties disagree on the price. The ruling hinges on whether the item has increased in value through connected or disconnected increments. Understanding the classification of increases (ziyāda) and the scholars' differing positions is essential for correctly applying the fiqh of sales disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disagreement
The sold item is destroyed in the buyer's possession, and the parties differ about the price. Determine whether the price is a debt (dayn) or a specific item ('ayn). The dispute concerns whether mutual oaths (tahāluf) should be taken.
If the price is a specific item, there is consensus on mutual oaths.
2
Classify the type of increase (ziyāda)
Analyze whether the destroyed item had an increase that is connected (muttasila) or disconnected (munfasila), and whether it is produced (mutawallida) or non-produced. This classification governs the ruling.
Connected increases: fattening (produced), dye (non-produced). Disconnected: dates (produced), earnings (non-produced).
3
Apply the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
For each increase type, state that no mutual oaths are taken; the contract is rescinded on the actual object or value. The buyer's oath alone is accepted.
Exception: for disconnected non-produced increases (earnings), mutual oaths are taken by consensus.
4
Contrast with Muhammad al-Shaybani's position
For the same increase types (except the consensus case), Muhammad holds that mutual oaths are taken. If both swear, the contract is rescinded.
The practical difference: under the two, the buyer's oath decides; under Muhammad, both swear and rescission occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولا بعد هلاك المبيع ؛ يعني لا تحالف لو اختلفا في قدر الثمن بعد هلاك المبيع في يد المشتري ، وقيَّدنا هلاك المبيع بقولنا : في يد المشتري ؛ لأنه إذا هلك عند البائع قبل قبضه انفسخ البيع. ذكره شيخنا الطحطاوي نقلاً عن الحلبي. وهلاك المبيع شامل لخروجه عن ملك المشتري، وزيادته زيادة متصلةٌ مُتولّدة أو غير متولّدة، أو منفصلة متولّدة، فإنّه لا يتحالفان عندهما، ويتحالفان عنده ، فيفسخُ على العين في المتصلة المتولّدة من الأصل: كالسمن ، وعلى العين أو القيمة في متّصلة غير متولدة منه : كالصبغ ، وعلى القيمة في المنفصلة المتولدة : كالتمر. وأما في منفصلة غير متولدة منه كالكسب، فيتحالفان، ويفسخ على العين بالإجماع. ذكره القهستاني نقلاً عن «المبسوط». قوله : وحلف المشتري ؛ لأنه ينكر زيادة الثمن هذا إذا كان الثمن ديناً، وأمّا إذا كان عيناً يتحالفان بالاتفاق ؛ لأنَّ المبيع في أحد الجانبين قائم، ثم يردُّ الهالك إن كان مثليّاً، وقيمته إن لم يكن ، وهذا إذا هلك بعد القبض ، وإن هلك قبله وكان الثمن مقبوضاً يتحالفان اتفاقاً. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر»، وغيره. قوله : عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف الله ؛ وبه قال مالك له في رواية، وأحمد ولا ه في رواية. ذكره العلامة الشُّمُنِّي (في «كمال الدراية»).
His saying: 'and not after destruction of the sold item' means that there is no mutual oath if they differ about the amount of the price after the sold item is destroyed in the possession of the buyer. And we restricted 'destruction of the sold item' by our saying 'in the possession of the buyer' because if it is destroyed with the seller before delivery, the sale is rescinded. Mentioned by our teacher al-Tahtāwī from al-Halabī. And destruction of the sold item includes its leaving the possession of the buyer, and its increase, whether a connected increase produced or not produced, or a disconnected produced increase, so they do not take mutual oaths according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), and they take mutual oaths according to him (Muhammad). Then the contract is rescinded on the actual object in the case of a connected increase produced from the origin, like fatness; and on the actual object or value in a connected increase not produced from it, like dye; and on the value in a disconnected produced increase, like dates. As for a disconnected increase not produced from it, like earnings, they take mutual oaths and rescind on the actual object by consensus. Mentioned by al-Qahistānī from al-Mabsūṭ. His saying: 'and the buyer takes an oath' because he is denying an increase of the price. This is if the price is a debt. But if it is a specific item, they take mutual oaths by agreement, because the sold item is existing on one side, then they return the destroyed item if it is of a standard type or its value if not. And this is if it is destroyed after delivery. But if it is destroyed before delivery and the price was taken, they take mutual oaths by agreement. This is in Majma' al-Anhur and others. His saying: 'according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf' – and it is also the saying of Malik in one narration, and Ahmad in one narration. Mentioned by 'Allama al-Shomunni in Kamal al-Diraya.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This section deals with the procedure when the sold item has been destroyed while in the buyer's possession and then the parties disagree about the price. The first note clarifies that if the item is destroyed in the buyer's hands, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, there are no mutual oaths (tahāluf) if they differ about the amount of the price. Instead, the buyer's statement is taken with an oath because he is denying an increase. The text goes on to list various types of increases (ziyāda) that may have occurred to the destroyed item: connected increases produced from the original (like fattening), connected increases not produced (like dye), disconnected produced increases (like dates from a tree), and disconnected non-produced increases (like earnings from rent). For each, the ruling differs between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. According to the two (Abū Hanifa and Abū Yūsuf), if the item has increased, no mutual oaths are taken; the contract is rescinded on the actual object or value depending on the type. According to Muhammad, mutual oaths are taken (he dissents). For a disconnected non-produced increase (e.g., earnings), all agree that mutual oaths occur and the rescission is on the actual object. The second note specifies that the buyer's oath applies when the price is a debt (dayn). If the price is a specific item ('ayn), then according to all, they take mutual oaths, because the sold item is considered to exist on one side. The third note names the imams: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the one position, while Muhammad al-Shaybani opposes. Interestingly, the text also notes that Malik (in one narration) and Ahmad (in one narration) agree with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute with cross-madhhab support.

Key Terms
هلاك المبيع (halāk al-mabī'): Destruction of the sold item; includes leaving the possession of the buyer and any increase that may have occurred.
زيادة متصلة (ziyāda muttasila): An increase that is physically connected to the sold item, like fattening or dye.
زيادة منفصلة (ziyāda munfasila): An increase that is not physically connected, like dates from a tree or earnings from rent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if the sold item has been destroyed and there is a connected increase like fattening, do the parties take mutual oaths?
A) Yes, they take mutual oaths.
B) No, they do not take mutual oaths.
C) It depends on the value of the increase.
D) Only if the price is a specific item.

Key Takeaway
The presence of a connected increase (especially produced) changes the procedure: under the dominant Hanafi view, mutual oaths are not required; the buyer's oath suffices.

Tahaluf After Destruction: A Hanafi Internal Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mutual oath after destruction of sold item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on mutual oath when the sold item perishes, and explain the Hanafi majority's response to the analogy for perished goods.

Core Idea
Mutual oath after delivery is an exception to qiyas; the Hanafi majority restricts it to cases where the commodity is present.

When a sold item perishes and the parties dispute the price, the Hanafi school internally disagrees on whether mutual oath (tahāluf) applies. This lesson unpacks the reasoning of each imam, focusing on the scope of a known hadith and the role of qiyas. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply the principles of istihsan and the limits of textual analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They hold that there is no mutual oath after the destruction of the sold item; instead the buyer's statement (denying the higher price) is taken as the condition.
This is the majority Hanafi position.
2
Identify the ruling of Muhammad al-Shaybani
He holds that mutual oath applies and the sale is rescinded against the value of the perished item, because each party claims a different contract.
This is also the view of al-Shafi'i and some narrations from Malik and Ahmad.
3
Analyze Muhammad's proof
He argues by analogy to other contract disputes: each party claims a different price (e.g., 1000 vs 2000), so they should take oaths. The destruction does not terminate the contract entirely, as benefit remains in determining the price.
He treats the dispute as one over the existence of a different contract, similar to claim and counterclaim.
4
Analyze the majority's response
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf reply that mutual oath after delivery (qabd) is already an exception to qiyas (based on a specific hadith). It cannot be extended beyond the plain text, which conditions mutual oath on the commodity being present. Once the item perishes, the contract is terminated, and no benefit from rescission remains.
The hadith explicitly states: 'when the commodity is present.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice: according to the majority, the buyer's statement is binding (he pays the lower price he admitted, or swears). According to Muhammad, both swear, the sale is rescinded, and the buyer returns the value of the perished item.
The difference affects which party bears the loss if the price is uncertain.

[٢] قوله : لأن كلا منهما... الخ ؛ تقريره : إنّ كلاً من المتبايعين يدعى عقداً غير العقد الذي يدعيه صاحبه، فإنَّ من الظاهر أنَّ البيع بألف غير البيع بألفين. ألا ترى أنَّ الشاهدين إذا اختلفا في قدرِ الثمن لا تقبل شهادتهما ؛ لعدم كمال النصاب في كل واحدٍ من البيعين، فصار كما لو ادَّعى أحدهما البيع والآخرُ الهبة، أو البيع مقايضة، فهلك أحد البدلين، أو اختلفا في جنس الثمن بعد هلاك السلعة ، بأن قال أحدهما : دراهم، والآخر: دنانير. فإن قيل : القياس فاسد ؛ لأنه حال قيام السلعة يفيد التراد، ولا فائدة له بعد هلاكها. أجيب : بأنَّ في التحالف هاهنا أيضاً ،فائدة، وهو تسليم ما يدعيه البائع له على تقدير نكول المشتري ، أو سقوط الثمن كله عن المشتري على تقديـر عـدم نـــولـه فيتحالفان. [٣] قوله : ولهما أن التحالف ... الخ ؛ تحريره : إنَّ التحالف بعد القبض على خلاف القياس ، لِمَا أَنه سَلّم للمشتري ما يدعيه، ولا يدعي المشتري على البائع شيئاً ينكره ؛ لأن المبيع مملولاً له، سلّم إليه، وقد ورد النص، وهو قوله : «إذا اختلف المتبايعان، والسلعة قائمة بعينها ، تحالفا وترادًا بالتحالف» (۲) في حال قيام السلعة، والتحالف فيه يفضي إلى الفسخ. ولا كذلك بعد هلاكها ؛ لارتفاع العقدِ بهلاكها ، فلم يكن في معناه ؛ لأنَّ عند قيام السلعة يندفع الضرر عن كل واحد منهما بالتحالف، فإنّه ينفسخ العقد ويعود كل (1) ينظر: «كمال الدراية (ق٥٩٧). (۲) سبق تخريجه .
And not after the destruction of the sold item, and the oath of the buyer. [1] Or the buyer said: 'I paid part of the price,' and the seller denied. (And not after the destruction of the sold item, and the oath of the buyer): Meaning, if the sold item perishes, then they differ over the amount of the price, there is no mutual oath according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and the statement is that of the buyer as a condition, and the statement is for the denier of incidental matters. Such is mentioned by our teacher al-Tahtawi in his gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. [1] His statement: 'And not after the destruction of the sold item'; means there is no mutual oath if they differ over the amount of the price after the sold item perishes in the possession of the buyer. We restricted the destruction of the sold item to 'in the buyer's possession' because if it perishes with the seller before delivery, the sale is rescinded. Our teacher al-Tahtawi mentioned this, quoting from al-Halabi. The destruction of the sold item includes its leaving the buyer's ownership, and its increase as a connected increase, whether original or not, or a separate original increase. For they do not take mutual oaths according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), but they do take mutual oaths according to him (Muhammad). Then he rescinds regarding the physical asset in the case of a connected original increase like fatness, and regarding the physical asset or value in a connected non-original increase like dye, and regarding the value in a separate original increase like dates. As for a separate non-original increase like earnings, they take mutual oaths and rescind regarding the physical asset by consensus. al-Qahistani mentioned this quoting from al-Mabsut. [2] His statement: 'And the oath of the buyer'; because he denies the increase of the price. This is when the price is a debt. But if it is a physical asset, they take mutual oaths by agreement, because the sold item is present on one side, then the perished item is returned if it is fungible, or its value if not. This is when it perishes after delivery. If it perishes before delivery and the price has been paid, they take mutual oaths by agreement. So is it in Majma' al-Anhur and others. [3] His statement: 'According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf'; and Malik said similarly in one narration, and Ahmad in another. al-Shunnī mentioned it. (1) Tahtawi's gloss (3:303). (2) In his gloss (3:304). (3) In Jami' al-Rumuz (2:269). (4) Majma' al-Anhur (2:264). (5) In Kamal al-Dirayah (q597). [Page 145] Book of Claims, Chapter of Mutual Oath 145 [1] And according to Muhammad, they take mutual oaths, and the sale is rescinded against the value of the perished item, because each of them claims a contract and the other denies it, so they take mutual oaths. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is that mutual oath after delivery of the sale is contrary to analogy; so it does not extend to the case of destruction of the commodity. [1] His statement: 'And according to Muhammad'; it is the statement of al-Shafi'i, and Malik said similarly in one narration, and Ahmad in one narration. [2] His statement: 'Because each of them...' etc. Its elaboration: Each of the two contracting parties claims a contract other than the contract that the other claims. For it is apparent that a sale for one thousand is not the same as a sale for two thousand. Do you not see that if two witnesses differ over the amount of the price, their testimony is not accepted, because the required number is not complete for either of the two sales. So it becomes as if one claimed a sale and the other a gift, or a sale for exchange. Then one of the two countervalues perishes, or they differ over the type of price after the destruction of the commodity, such as one saying: dirhams, and the other: dinars. If it is said: The analogy is faulty, because when the commodity is present, it results in restoration, and there is no benefit after its destruction. The answer is: That mutual oath here also has a benefit, which is the delivery of what the seller claims to him in case the buyer refuses to take the oath, or the entire price drops from the buyer in case he refuses to take the oath. So they take mutual oaths. [3] His statement: 'And for them that mutual oath...' etc. Its analysis: Mutual oath after delivery is contrary to analogy, because he (the seller) has handed over to the buyer what he claims, and the buyer does not claim anything against the seller that he denies, because the sold item is owned by him and has been handed over to him. The text has been narrated, which is his statement: 'If the two parties to a sale differ and the commodity is still in its physical form, they take mutual oaths and return through mutual oath.' (2) This is when the commodity is present, and mutual oath therein leads to rescission. Not so after its destruction, because the contract is terminated by its destruction, so it is not in its meaning. Because when the commodity is present, harm is repelled from each of them by mutual oath, for the contract is rescinded and each returns... (1) See: Kamal al-Dirayah (q597). (2) Its source has been cited earlier.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on mutual oath (tahāluf) when the sold item has perished and the parties dispute the price. The base text (matn) from Al-Wiqayah states: 'And not after the destruction of the sold item, and the oath of the buyer.' The commentator explains that if the sold item perishes in the buyer's possession and they differ over the price, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that there is no mutual oath; instead the buyer's statement is accepted as the condition, and the statement is given to the denier of incidental matters. Muhammad al-Shaybani, on the other hand, holds that they do take mutual oaths and the sale is rescinded against the value of the perished item. The passage then details the various types of increments (connected/original, etc.) and the rulings according to each position. The argument for Muhammad is that each party claims a different contract (e.g., sale for 1000 vs sale for 2000), so they should take oaths like any other contract dispute. The argument for Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is that mutual oath after delivery is an exception to analogy and therefore cannot be extended beyond the case where the commodity is still present. The hadith cited supports the ruling only when the commodity is present. The passage also notes the positions of other imams: al-Shafi'i agrees with Muhammad, while Malik and Ahmad have two narrations.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oath-taking between two disputing parties each swearing on their claim.
هلاك المبيع (halāk al-mabī'): Destruction or perishing of the sold item, including loss of ownership or certain increments.
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning; here, the analogy of mutual oath after delivery to other cases is considered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf when the sold item perishes and the parties dispute the price?
A) They take mutual oaths and the sale is rescinded against the value.
B) There is no mutual oath; the buyer's statement is taken.
C) They take mutual oaths and the sale is rescinded against the physical asset.
D) The seller's statement is taken unconditionally.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority restricts mutual oath after delivery to cases where the commodity is present, based on the explicit condition in the hadith, and does not extend it to perished goods.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Tahaluf After Destruction: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tahaluf after destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that mutual oath (tahaluf) does not apply after goods are destroyed, and contrast it with Imam Muhammad's view.

Core Idea
Tahaluf is only for extant goods; after destruction, the contract is dissolved and rescission is impossible.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi disagreement: does the mutual oath procedure (tahaluf) apply when the sold goods have perished after delivery? The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya expands on the positions of Imam Muhammad and the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), each with detailed proofs. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi methodology of textual restriction and analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
What is the setting? Buyer and seller disagree on the price after the goods have been destroyed following delivery. The goods no longer exist.
The dispute is about procedure, not about validity of the original sale.
2
State the positions
What does Imam Muhammad hold? He says they take mutual oaths and the sale is rescinded at the value of the destroyed goods. What do the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold? They say no tahaluf after destruction because it is contrary to analogy and the hadith conditions the goods being extant.
Imam Muhammad's view aligns with Shafi'i, Malik (in one narration), and Ahmad (in one narration).
3
Examine Muhammad's proof
How does he argue? Each party claims a different contract (one says sale for 1000, other for 2000). They are like two opposing claimants. Just as differing witnesses on price invalidates testimony, here each denies the other's claim, necessitating tahaluf. There is benefit: if one refuses to swear, the other's claim is upheld.
His argument is based on the idea that the difference in price amount creates two distinct contracts.
4
Examine the two's proof
How do they restrict the hadith? The hadith says 'and the commodity is extant' – this is a condition. The unrestricted command to return is carried upon the restricted. After destruction, the contract is dissolved by the goods' perishing, so rescission is impossible. Also, after delivery, the seller has no claim against the buyer; the buyer already owns the goods.
They also argue that the contract does not differ by difference in price amount from the same species, as seen when an agent sells for a different price.
5
Resolve the dispute
Which position is mufta bihi? The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: no tahaluf after destruction. The text indicates that their view is stronger because it adheres to the explicit condition in the hadith and avoids extending a ruling beyond its original context.
The practical consequence: if goods are destroyed, the parties do not take mutual oaths; instead, the seller's statement is taken with his oath (as per the general rule before tahaluf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب التحالف
وعند محمد له يتحالفان ، وينفسخ البيع على قيمة الهالك ؛ لأنَّ كلا منهما يدعي عقداً ويُنْكِرُهُ الآخر، فيتحالفان ، ولهما : أنَّ التَّحالف بعد قبض البيع على خلاف القياس ؛ فلا يَتَعَدَّى إلى حال هلاكِ السلعة.
[١]قوله : وعند محمد الله ؛ وهو قول الشافعي له ، وبه قال مالك له في رواية ، وأحمد له في رواية).
[٢] قوله : لأن كلا منهما... الخ ؛ تقريره : إنّ كلاً من المتبايعين يدعى عقداً غير العقد الذي يدعيه صاحبه، فإنَّ من الظاهر أنَّ البيع بألف غير البيع بألفين. ألا ترى أنَّ الشاهدين إذا اختلفا في قدرِ الثمن لا تقبل شهادتهما ؛ لعدم كمال النصاب في كل واحدٍ من البيعين، فصار كما لو ادَّعى أحدهما البيع والآخرُ الهبة، أو البيع مقايضة، فهلك أحد البدلين، أو اختلفا في جنس الثمن بعد هلاك السلعة ، بأن قال أحدهما : دراهم، والآخر: دنانير. فإن قيل : القياس فاسد ؛ لأنه حال قيام السلعة يفيد التراد، ولا فائدة له بعد هلاكها.
أجيب : بأنَّ في التحالف هاهنا أيضاً ،فائدة، وهو تسليم ما يدعيه البائع له على تقدير نكول المشتري ، أو سقوط الثمن كله عن المشتري على تقديـر عـدم نـــولـه فيتحالفان.
[٣] قوله : ولهما أن التحالف ... الخ ؛ تحريره : إنَّ التحالف بعد القبض على خلاف القياس ، لِمَا أَنه سَلّم للمشتري ما يدعيه، ولا يدعي المشتري على البائع شيئاً ينكره ؛ لأن المبيع مملولاً له، سلّم إليه، وقد ورد النص، وهو قوله : «إذا اختلف المتبايعان، والسلعة قائمة بعينها ، تحالفا وترادًا بالتحالف» (۲) في حال قيام السلعة، والتحالف فيه يفضي إلى الفسخ.
ولا كذلك بعد هلاكها ؛ لارتفاع العقدِ بهلاكها ، فلم يكن في معناه ؛ لأنَّ عند قيام السلعة يندفع الضرر عن كل واحد منهما بالتحالف، فإنّه ينفسخ العقد ويعود كل واحد منهما إلى رأس ماله بعينه، وبعد هلاكها لا يحصل ذلك، فالعقد بعد الهلاكِ لا يحتمل الفسخ بالإقالة، والردّ بالعيب، فكذا بالتحالف إذ الفسخُ لا يردُّ إلا على ما ورد عليه العقد.
وأما أنَّ النص قد ورد بالتحالف في حال قيام السلعة، فبيانه أن قوله : والسلعة قائمة» مذكورٌ على وجه الشرط، ولا يلزمُ إطلاق قوله : «إذا اختلف المتبايعان فالقول ما يقولُهُ البائع ويترادّان»، لأنَّ الأمر بالتراد دليل قيام السلعة، إذ هو تفاعل من الرد فيستدعي الردَّ من الجانبين، ولا ذلك إلا لقيام السلعة، وليس المراد به تراد العقد ؛ لأنه لا يتصور ذلك، مع أن المطلق والمقيد إذا وردا في حادثة واحدة في حكم واحد، فالمطلق يكون محمولاً على المقيد.
والجواب عن قوله : إنَّ كلَّ واحد منهما يدَّعي عقداً غير ما يدعيه الآخر : أنَّ العقد لا يختلف باختلاف قدرِ الثمن من جنس واحد، ألا ترى أنَّ الوكيل بالبيع بألف يبيعه بألفين، وأن البيع بألف يصير بألفين بالزيادة في الثمن، وبخمسمئة في الحط. وإنما لا يقبل شهادة الشاهدين إذا اختلفا في قدر الثمن مع اتحادِ الجنس ؛ لأنَّ المدعى أحدهما ، لا لاختلاف العقد ، بخلاف ما لو اختلفا في جنس الثمن ؛ لأنَّ المبيع لا يُسلّمُ للمشتري إلا بثمن، ولم يتفقا عليه، وهاهنا اتفقا على ألف، وهو تلقي الصحة. ألا ترى أنَّ المبيع لو كان جاريةٌ جاز وطؤها قبل الفسخ بعد التحالف، ولو كان مختلفاً لما صح كما لو ادَّعى أحدهما هبة، والآخرُ بيعاً؛ لاختلافهما حقيقة، وبخلاف بيع المقايضة ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ منهما مبيع ، فكان البيع قائماً ببقاء المعقود عليه ؛ ولذا لا يجوز الإقالة فيه، ويردُّ بالعيب.
والجواب عن قوله : إنه يفيد دفع زيادة الثمن ؛ إن فائدته ليست من موجباته، بل من موجبات نكول البائع وليست اليمين من موجبات العقد، حتى يكون النكولُ من موجباته، هكذا قالوا وهاهنا من المباحث النفيسة ما تركناها ؛ لغرابة المقام، إن شئت فارجع إلى مبسوطات الأعلام.
Book of Claims, Chapter of Mutual Oath. According to Muhammad, they take mutual oaths, and the sale is rescinded at the value of the destroyed [goods]; because each of them claims a contract and the other denies it, so they take mutual oaths. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): that mutual oath after taking possession of the sale is contrary to analogy, so it does not extend to the case of destruction of the commodity. [1] His statement: 'And according to Muhammad' — it is the saying of al-Shafi'i, and Malik said it in one narration, and Ahmad in one narration. [2] His statement: 'because each of them...' etc.; its explanation: that each of the two parties to a sale claims a contract different from the contract claimed by the other, for it is apparent that a sale for a thousand is not a sale for two thousand. Do you not see that if two witnesses differ on the amount of the price, their testimony is not accepted, due to the lack of complete evidence in each of the two sales. So it becomes as if one claimed sale and the other claimed gift, or sale by barter, then one of the two compensations perishes, or they differ on the species of the price after the destruction of the commodity, like one saying dirhams and the other dinars. If it is said: the analogy is flawed because at the time the commodity exists it leads to mutual return, and there is no benefit after its destruction, the answer is: that in mutual oath here also there is a benefit, which is the delivery of what the seller claims to him if the buyer refuses to take an oath, or the full dropping of the price from the buyer if he does not take an oath, so they take mutual oaths. [3] His statement: 'and for them that mutual oath...' etc.; its refinement: that mutual oath after taking possession is contrary to analogy, because the seller has already delivered to the buyer what he claims, and the buyer does not claim anything from the seller that he denies, because the sold item is owned by him and has been delivered to him. And the text has come, which is his saying: 'If the two parties to a sale differ and the commodity is still extant, they take mutual oaths and return [the goods] by mutual oath' in the case of the commodity being extant, and mutual oath therein leads to rescission. Not so after its destruction, because the contract is dissolved by its destruction, so it is not in its meaning. Because when the commodity is extant, harm from each of them is repelled by mutual oath, for the contract is rescinded and each returns to his original capital exactly. After its destruction that does not occur. So the contract after destruction does not admit rescission by mutual cancellation (iqala) or return due to defect, likewise by mutual oath, indeed rescission only applies to what the contract was upon. As for the text having come for mutual oath in the case of the commodity being extant, its clarification is that his statement 'and the commodity is extant' is mentioned as a condition, and the unrestricted statement 'If the two parties to a sale differ, then the statement is what the seller says and they return [the goods]' is not binding, because the command to return indicates the commodity is extant, for it is mutual return from the side of both, and that only happens if the commodity is extant. It is not intended thereby the return of the contract, because that is inconceivable. Moreover, when an unrestricted and a restricted [text] come on one incident in one ruling, the unrestricted is carried upon the restricted. The answer to his statement that each of them claims a contract different from what the other claims: that the contract does not differ by difference in the amount of the price from one species. Do you not see that an agent to sell for a thousand sells it for two thousand, and that a sale for a thousand becomes for two thousand by increase in price, and for five hundred by reduction. Testimony of two witnesses is not accepted when they differ on the amount of the price with the unity of species, because the claim is one, not due to difference of contract. Contrary to if they differ on the species of the price, because the sold item is not delivered to the buyer except for a price, and they have not agreed upon it. Here they have agreed on a thousand, which is accepted validity. Do you not see that if the sold item were a slave-girl, intercourse with her is permitted before rescission after mutual oath? If it were different, it would not be valid, as if one claimed a gift and the other a sale, due to their real difference. And contrary to a barter sale, because each of them is a sold item, so the sale remains by the survival of the subject matter, and therefore mutual cancellation is not permissible therein, and return due to defect applies. The answer to his statement that it benefits the repelling of increase of price: that its benefit is not from its requirements, but from the requirements of the seller's refusal to take an oath, and the oath is not from the requirements of the contract such that refusal would be from its requirements. Thus said they. And here there are many valuable investigations which we have left due to the oddity of the context; if you wish, refer to the detailed works of the scholars.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the application of mutual oath (tahaluf) when the subject matter of a sale has perished after delivery. The issue arises when the buyer and seller disagree on the price after the goods are destroyed. The immediate background is that the hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) prescribes mutual oath and rescission only when the goods are still extant. Imam Muhammad (and other schools) extend this ruling to the case of destruction, arguing that each party claims a different contract (one says a thousand, the other says two thousand), so they are like two claimants denying each other's claim, thus necessitating mutual oath. The rescission occurs at the value of the destroyed goods. On the other hand, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the 'two' — lahuma) hold that mutual oath after possession is contrary to analogy (qiyas) and is restricted by the hadith to the existence of the goods. Since the contract is already dissolved by destruction, rescission is not possible. They also argue that after delivery, the seller has given up possession and the buyer does not claim anything from the seller to deny. The commentary then provides the reasoning (taqrīr) and responds to objections. It explains that the difference in price amount does not change the contract itself, unlike difference in species. The text also uses examples from agency, barter, and slave-girl to illustrate points. The final position (mufta bihi) according to the text is that of the two, i.e., no tahaluf after destruction, as it is the relied-upon view. The passage is a typical expansion of the matn, showing the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oath between buyer and seller when they disagree on the price or goods, leading to rescission of the contract.
نكول (nukul): Refusal to take an oath after being required to do so; in this context, if one party refuses, the other's claim is upheld.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here, tahaluf after possession is said to be contrary to analogy because it extends a ruling beyond its original context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), why is mutual oath (tahaluf) not applied after the goods have been destroyed?
A) Because the hadith does not mention destruction.
B) Because the parties have already agreed on the price.
C) Because the contract is dissolved and rescission is impossible, and the hadith conditions the goods being extant.
D) Because the seller has already taken an oath.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, mutual oath (tahaluf) is only valid when the goods are still in existence; after destruction, the contract is dissolved and rescission is impossible.

The Necessity of the Commodity's Existence for Mutual Oath-Taking (Tahalf)
Responding to objections about the condition that the commodity must be extant for tahalf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition that the commodity must be extant for mutual oath-taking (tahalf) and evaluate the three major objections raised against it.

Core Idea
Mutual oath-taking requires the commodity's existence; rescission after destruction is impossible.

In this passage, the Hanafi scholar continues to defend the condition that the commodity must be present for mutual oath-taking (tahalf) between buyer and seller. He addresses three objections: the apparent absolute hadith, the claim of differing contracts, and the assertion that the condition merely prevents opportunistic pricing. The passage sharpens your ability to apply textual interpretation (haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad) and logical consistency in fiqh reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
State the Hanafi position: mutual oath-taking (tahalf) is only valid when the sold commodity is still in existence. If it is destroyed, rescission through tahalf is not possible.
This is the condition (shart) being defended.
2
Analyze the first objection and response
The opponent cites the absolute hadith 'they return' (yataraddan) without mention of existence. The author responds that the verb itself implies mutual return of an object, which requires the object to be present. Furthermore, the absolute text is restricted by the explicit condition in another hadith stating 'while the commodity is extant'.
This uses the usul principle of haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad.
3
Examine the second objection on contract unity
The opponent argues that each party claims a different contract (different price), so tahalf should not be treated as a single contract dispute. The author responds that price variation within the same currency type does not change the contract; the contract remains one. Examples: an agent selling for a thousand can sell for two thousand, and the contract is the same.
This distinguishes between price amount (within same type) and price type (e.g., dirhams vs dinars), where the latter would create different contracts.
4
Consider the third objection on purpose
The opponent claims the condition merely serves to prevent the buyer from claiming a higher price to reduce loss. The author refutes that this benefit is not a necessary consequence of the rule; the oath and its consequences are not part of the contract's necessary effects.
The ruling's basis is textual and logical, not utilitarian.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

One of them to his capital specifically, and after its destruction that is not attained. So the contract after destruction does not admit rescission by iqalah or return due to defect, likewise by mutual oath-taking since rescission only applies to what the contract has covered. As for the fact that the text has come regarding mutual oath-taking when the commodity is extant, its clarification is that his statement 'and the commodity is extant' is mentioned as a condition, and the absolute statement 'When the two buyers differ, the word is that of the seller and they return [the goods]' does not necessarily apply, because the command to return is evidence of the existence of the commodity, since it is a tafa'ul from 'radd' which requires return from both sides, and that is only possible if the commodity is extant. It does not refer to cancellation of the contract because that is inconceivable, moreover, when absolute and restricted [texts] appear on a single incident regarding one ruling, the absolute is carried upon the restricted. The answer to his statement that each of them claims a contract different from what the other claims: that the contract does not differ due to difference in the amount of price if of one type. No [witness] testimony is only rejected if they differ in the amount of price within the same type, because the subject of claim is one, not due to difference in the contract, contrary to if they differ in the type of price, because the sold item is only delivered to the buyer for a price, and they have not agreed upon it. Here they have agreed on a thousand, and it is accepted as valid. Do you not see that if the sold item was a slave girl, intercourse with her would be permitted before rescission after mutual oath-taking, and if it were different it would not be valid, as if one claimed a gift and the other a sale, due to their real difference, and contrary to barter because each one is a sold item, so the sale exists by the continued existence of the object of contract, hence iqalah is not permissible in it, and it may be returned due to defect. The answer to his statement that it benefits the rejection of the increase in price: that its benefit is not from its necessary consequences, but rather from the consequences of the seller's refusal [to take the oath], and the oath is not from the necessary consequences of the contract, such that the refusal would be from its necessary consequences. Thus they said, and here there are refined investigations which we have left due to the strangeness of the context; if you wish, refer to the comprehensive works of the distinguished scholars.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the discussion on the condition that the commodity must be extant for mutual oath-taking (tahalf) when buyer and seller differ on price. The author first reinforces the earlier argument: rescission (faskh) only applies to what the contract covered, and after destruction of the commodity, it is impossible to return it, so tahalf is not applicable. Then he addresses objections. First, the opponent says the hadith 'when the two disputing parties differ, the word is that of the seller and they return' is absolute and does not mention the commodity being extant. The author responds: the phrase 'and they return' implies the commodity exists because return requires giving back from both sides, which is only possible if the commodity is still there. Also, the absolute text is to be interpreted in light of the restricted text that explicitly says 'while the commodity is extant'. Second, the opponent argues that in a tahalf case each party claims a different contract (one claims sale, the other claims different price), so it should be treated as separate claims. The author refutes: the contract does not differ just because the price amount differs within the same type; the contract remains one. He gives examples: an agent authorized to sell for a thousand can sell for two thousand; the sale becomes for a different price but the contract is the same. Witness testimony may be rejected due to disagreement on price amount but that is because the claim is about one thing, not because the contract differs. Only if the price type differs (e.g., dirhams vs dinars) would the contract differ. Third, an objection that requiring the commodity to be extant only serves to prevent the buyer from claiming a higher price to reduce his loss. The author says that is not the purpose of the rule; the purpose is that the oath and its consequences (like buyer's refusal) are not part of the contract's necessary effects. The author ends by noting there are deeper discussions but leaves them here.

Key Terms
التحالف (al-tahalf): The mutual oath-taking between buyer and seller when they dispute the price, leading to rescission of the sale.
الإقالة (al-iqalah): Mutual rescission of a contract by agreement of both parties.
الرد بالعيب (al-radd bi al-'ayb): Returning the sold item due to a defect discovered after purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi arguments presented, why is mutual oath-taking (tahalf) not permissible after the destruction of the sold commodity?
A) Because the hadith explicitly forbids it.
B) Because rescission (faskh) can only apply to what the contract originally covered, and the destroyed item cannot be returned.
C) Because the buyer and seller cannot agree on the price after destruction.
D) Because the contract becomes invalid upon destruction of the item.

Key Takeaway
Mutual oath-taking (tahalf) requires the existence of the sold commodity; rescission is only possible when the object can be returned.

Mutual Oath-Taking After Partial Destruction: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tahalf after partial destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on tahalf after partial destruction and explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
When a sale item is partially destroyed and the price is disputed, tahalf is blocked unless the seller forfeits his share.

This lesson examines a specific procedural ruling in Hanafi sales law: what happens when part of a sold item perishes before payment is settled and the parties dispute the price? The core rule prohibits mutual oath-taking (tahalf) unless the seller waives his claim for the destroyed portion. However, within the Hanafi school, Abu Yusuf offers a different approach, leading to a notable intra-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
The case: two slaves sold in one transaction; both delivered; one perishes with the buyer; then the parties dispute the total price (seller claims 2000, buyer claims 1000).
The perished item is called 'halik', the surviving 'qa'im'.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa (the Imam) holds that tahalf is not permitted unless the seller agrees to forfeit his claim for the share of the perished item (i.e., take payment only for the living one).
This is the position recorded in al-Mabsut and al-Jami' al-Saghir, with a nuance: the buyer's word with oath is accepted if the seller does not choose to take the living item for free.
3
State Abu Yusuf's ruling
Abu Yusuf says tahalf is performed concerning the surviving item, the contract is rescinded for that part, and the buyer's word is taken regarding the value of the perished item.
This view appears in popular later works like Kamal al-Dirayah and al-Hidayah.
4
Analyze the commentator's critique
The commentator (of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya) notes a contradiction: in al-Hidayah, when the value of the perished item is disputed, the word is that of the seller (as the denier), not the buyer. So Abu Yusuf's assignment of the word to the buyer seems inconsistent. An interpretation is offered: after tahalf, the living item is returned to the seller, and the buyer's liability for its share falls away, which resolves the conflict.
This interpretation is from Nata'ij al-Afkar.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, the seller has the option to waive his claim for the destroyed item and keep the contract for the surviving part. Under Abu Yusuf, the contract is partially rescinded, and the buyer's claim on the value of the destroyed item is accepted.
The mufta bihi (operative position) in later Hanafi works generally follows Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And not after destruction of part of it unless the seller agrees to leave the share of the destroyed [item]. His statement: 'and not after destruction of part of it' means: there is no mutual oath-taking if they differ after destruction of part of the sale after delivery of all, just as if he sold two slaves in one transaction and one of them perished with the buyer after taking delivery of both, then they differed over the price, so the seller said: I sold them to you for two thousand dirhams, and the buyer said: I bought them from you for one thousand dirhams; then they do not perform mutual oath-taking according to the Imam [Abu Hanifa] unless the seller agrees to leave the share of the destroyed one. This is what al-Quduri explicitly stated, and the author of al-Nihayah considered it the wording of al-Mabsut. And it is stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir: The word is that of the buyer with his oath according to Abu Hanifa for him, unless the seller wishes to take the living one and nothing for it. End quote. And Qadi Khan said: And it is mentioned in al-Asl: unless the seller wishes to take the living one and does not take anything from the price of the dead one. And Abu Yusuf said: They perform mutual oath-taking regarding the surviving [item], and the contract is rescinded in it, and the word is that of the buyer regarding the value of the destroyed one. Thus in Kamal al-Dirayah, al-Hidayah, and other commonly used books. However, there is a type of deficiency in it, because if they differ in the value of the destroyed one, the word is that of the seller, not the buyer, as in al-Hidayah. For if they differ in the value of the destroyed one, the word is that of the seller because the price has been established by their agreement, then the buyer claims an increased reduction due to the decrease in value of the destroyed one, and the seller denies it, and the word is that of the denier. End quote to the extent necessary. And what is said here contradicts it, so there must be interpretation here, and that is what was mentioned in Nata'ij al-Afkar: that the meaning is that after the mutual oath-taking, he returns the living one to the seller, and the share of the living one falls from
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when buyer and seller dispute the price after part of the goods has been destroyed following delivery. The ruling from the matn is: there is no mutual oath-taking (tahalf) after destruction of part, unless the seller agrees to forego his share in the destroyed item. The commentator gives an example: two slaves sold in one transaction; one dies with the buyer after taking both; then they dispute the price. According to Abu Hanifa, they do not perform tahalf unless the seller agrees to leave the share of the dead slave (i.e., forfeit his claim for that portion). Al-Quduri and others report this. Interestingly, in al-Jami' al-Saghir, it is stated: 'The word is that of the buyer with his oath, unless the seller wishes to take the living one and nothing for it.' This seems to give the buyer the advantage. Abu Yusuf differs: he says they do perform tahalf regarding the surviving item, rescind the contract for that part, and then the buyer's word is taken regarding the value of the destroyed item. The commentator notes a problem with Abu Yusuf's view: if they dispute the value of the destroyed item, the ruling in al-Hidayah is that the seller's word is taken because the price is established by agreement, and the buyer is claiming a reduction. So what is said here (by Abu Yusuf) contradicts that. Therefore, an interpretation is needed: after tahalf, the living item is returned to the seller and the buyer's liability for the share of the living one falls. This harmonizes the positions.

Key Terms
حصة الهالك (hissat al-halik): The share of the destroyed portion of the sale item; the seller forfeiting his claim to that portion's price.
التحالف في القائم (al-tahalf fi al-qa'im): Mutual oath-taking regarding the surviving part of the sale (as per Abu Yusuf's view).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition under which mutual oath-taking is permitted after partial destruction of the sale item?
A) As long as both parties agree to oath-taking.
B) Only if the seller agrees to forfeit his share of the destroyed item.
C) It is always permitted if the value of the destroyed item is known.
D) It is never permitted because the contract is void.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, partial destruction of a sale item after delivery blocks mutual oath-taking unless the seller foregoes his claim for the destroyed portion.

12 min
Mutual Oaths When Part of Sold Goods Perishes
Explains the Hanafi dispute on oaths when part of sold goods perishes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three positions within the Hanafi school regarding mutual oaths (tahāluf) when part of a sold commodity is destroyed, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Tahāluf after partial destruction is limited to the living part only, unless the seller forfeits the price of the destroyed.

When a sold commodity is partially destroyed before delivery, the buyer and seller may disagree about the price. The Hanafi school applies tahāluf (mutual oaths) to resolve such disputes. However, the three founding imams differed on whether tahāluf applies to the destroyed part, the living part, or both. This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āyah explains their arguments and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the three positions
Recognize that the case is a sold commodity partially destroyed. State the three views: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad.
The debate is about the scope of tahāluf after partial destruction.
2
Examine the proof of each position
For each imam, identify the dalīl (proof) and the reasoning. Abu Hanifa uses qiyās khafī (subtle analogy) that tahāluf is contrary to qiyās and not extendable.
Notice the usul principle: mā thabata bi khilāf al-qiyās lā yata‘addā.
3
Analyze the responses to Muhammad and Abu Yusuf
The text refutes Muhammad because partial destruction requires estimation (ḥarz) which is unknown in the subject of oath. It refutes Abu Yusuf because division by estimation is impermissible without seller's consent.
The response highlights the importance of certainty in the subject of oath.
4
Interpret the exception in al-Jāmi‘ al-Ṣaghīr
The exception 'he takes the living and nothing for him' is debated whether it applies to the oath (tahāluf) or the buyer's oath. The majority hold it applies to tahāluf.
This interpretation supports Abu Hanifa's view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The majority of later scholars (ʿāmmah al-mashāyikh) adopt the view that tahāluf is restricted to the living part, and only occurs if the seller agrees to forgo the price of the destroyed. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
This is a fatwa of the muta’akhkhirūn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الحي والهالك، فقول المشتري إنّما يعتبر في حصة الهالك من الثمن الذي أقر به المشتري لا في قيمة الهالك. انتهى. وقال محمد : يتحالفان على الحي والهالك ، ويردُّ الحي وقيمة الهالك ؛ لأنَّ هلاك كل السلعة لا يمنع التحالف عنده ، فهلاك البعض أولى أن لا يمنع. ولأبي يوسف له : إِنَّ امتناع التحالف إنّما يكون لأجل الهلاك، فيتقدر بقدرِ الهالك ، فإنَّ الحكم لا يزيد على العلة. ولأبي حنيفة له : إنَّ التحالف بعد والظنّ، وذلك لا يجوز إلا أن يرضى البائع أن يترك حصة الهالك. فحينئذ يكون الثمنُ كلّه بمقابلة الحي، ويخرجُ الهالك عن العقد فيكون كأنَّ العقد وقع على هذا فيتحالفان، فإن حلفا فسخ العقد فيه وأخذه، ولا يأخذ من ثمن الهالك ولا من قيمته شيئاً، وأيهما نَكَل لزمه دعوى الآخر، كما هو الحكم في الاختلاف عند قيام السلعة. قوله : أي لا يأخذ... الخ ؛ يعني أنَّ البائع إذا رضي بترك حصة الهالك فلا يأخذ من ثمن الهالك شيئاً، ويجعله كأنه لم يكن ، والعقد كأنه وقع على القائم فقط فيكون الثمن كله بمقابلة القائم، فيتحالفان كما هو الحكم في الاختلاف عند قيام السلعة. وينظر: «تبيين الحقائق (٤ : ۳۰۸). هذا تخريج بعض المشايخ الله ، وينصرفُ الاستثناء عندهم إلى التحالف، وقالوا : إن المراد بقوله في «الجامع الصغير»: يأخذ الحي ولا شيء له : أي لا يأخذ من ثمن الهالك شيئاً أصلاً. وقال بعض المشايخ له : هذا تخريج ... الخ ؛ توضيح الكلام بحيث ينكشف به المرام : إنهم اختلفوا في انصراف هذا الاستثناء، فقال مشايخ بلخ : إن هذا الاستثناء ينصرف إلى يمين المشتري، ومعناه : إنَّ البائع يأخذ الحي منهما صُلحاً عما يدعيه المشتري من الزيادة ، ويُجْعَلُ صلحهما على هذا العبد كصلحها على عبد آخر. وصار تقدير ما في «الكتاب» على قول هؤلاء المشايخ : لا يتحالفان عند الإمام الله، ويكون القول قول المشتري مع يمينه إلا أن يأخذ البائع الحي ولا يأخذ شيئا آخر فحينئذ لا يحلف المشتري. قال شيخ الإسلام خُواهر زَادَه : هذا لا يقوى ؛ لأنَّ الأخذ معلق بمشيئة البائع، ولو كان أخذ الحي بطريق الصلح لكان معلقاً بمشيئتها ، وقال الزيلعي : ليس في هذا الأخذ فائدة [له] أيضاً ولا يحتملها ، فلا يصار إليه ؛ لأنَّ ترك حصة الهالك من الثمن من غير بدل يقابله ليس من الحكمة. انتهى. وذهب عامة المشايخ الله إلى أنَّ هذا الاستثناء ينصرف إلى التحالف، فصار تقدير ما في الكتاب على قول هؤلاء يتحالفان عند الإمام الله إلا أن يشاء البائع أن يأخذ الحي، ولا يأخذ من ثمن الميت شيئاً ، فحينئذ يتحالفان ؛ لأنَّ المذكور قبل الاستثناء هو التحالف دون اليمين، فكان صرفُ الاستثناء إلى المذكور أولى. وقال بعضهم: إنَّ هذا الاستثناء ينصرفُ إلى يمين المشتري ؛ لأنَّ البائع إذا رضي أن يأخذ الحي ولا يأخذ من ثمن الميت شيئاً ، فحينئذ لا يحلف المشتري ؛ لأن البائع إذا عرض عن دعواه فلا معنى لتحليف المشتري، قال الزيلعي : وهذا مثل الأول في تبيين الحقائق (٤ : ٣٠٩).
That is, he does not take anything from the price of the destroyed item at all, and he considers the destroyed item as if it never existed and as if the contract was only upon the existing item, so they take oaths concerning that price, and the buyer is liable for the share of the destroyed item from the price that the buyer admitted after dividing the price according to the value of the living and the destroyed. So the buyer's statement is only considered regarding the share of the destroyed from the price he admitted, not regarding the value of the destroyed. End. Muhammad said: They take oaths concerning the living and the destroyed, and he returns the living and the value of the destroyed; because the destruction of the entire commodity does not prevent the oath according to him, so the destruction of part is even more appropriate not to prevent. And for Abu Yusuf: The prevention of the oath is only due to destruction, so it is limited to the extent of the destroyed, because the ruling does not exceed the cause. And for Abu Hanifa: The oath after taking possession is established by the text contrary to analogy in the case of the commodity existing, and the commodity is the name of all its parts, so the commodity is not specific after the loss of a part, and what is established contrary to analogy is not extended. The response to Muhammad's statement: The destruction of part necessitates knowing the value by estimation, and that is unknown regarding the subject of the oath, so it is not permissible. The response to Abu Yusuf's statement: The oath regarding the existing cannot be except by considering its share of the price, so it is necessary to divide the price according to value, and it is known by estimation and conjecture, and that is not permissible unless the seller agrees to relinquish the share of the destroyed. Then the entire price is in exchange for the living, and the destroyed exits the contract, so as if the contract occurred on this, and they take oaths; if they both swear, the contract is rescinded regarding it and he takes it, and he does not take anything from the price of the destroyed nor its value, and whichever of them refuses (nukal) is bound by the claim of the other, as is the ruling in the dispute when the commodity exists. His statement: 'That is, he does not take...' means that if the seller agrees to relinquish the share of the destroyed, he does not take anything from the price of the destroyed, and he makes it as if it never were, and the contract as if it occurred only on the existing, so the entire price is in exchange for the living, and they take oaths as is the ruling in the dispute when the commodity exists. See: 'Tabyin al-Haqa'iq (4:308). This is a derivation (takhrij) of some scholars, and the exception according to them applies to the oath, and they said: The meaning of his statement in 'Al-Jami' al-Saghir': 'He takes the living and nothing for him' is that he does not take anything from the price of the destroyed at all. And some scholars said: This is a derivation... clarification so that the intended meaning is revealed: They differed regarding the application of this exception. The scholars of Balkh said: This exception applies to the buyer's oath, and its meaning is that the seller takes the living from them as a settlement regarding the increase claimed by the buyer, and their settlement regarding this slave is like their settlement regarding another slave. And the estimation of what is in 'Al-Kitab' according to these scholars is: They do not take oaths according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and the statement is the buyer's with his oath unless the seller takes the living and does not take anything else; then the buyer does not swear. Shaykh al-Islam Khwahar Zadah said: This is not strong because the taking is dependent on the seller's will, and if the taking of the living were by way of settlement, it would be dependent on their will. And al-Zayla'i said: In this taking there is no benefit for him either, and it is not plausible, so it is not to be adopted because giving up the share of the destroyed from the price without a corresponding consideration is not wise. End. And the majority of scholars held that this exception applies to the oath, so the estimation of what is in 'Al-Kitab' according to these scholars is: They take oaths according to Imam Abu Hanifa unless the seller wishes to take the living and not take anything from the price of the dead; then they take oaths because what is mentioned before the exception is the oath, not the taking of an oath, so applying the exception to what is mentioned is better. And some said: This exception applies to the buyer's oath because if the seller agrees to take the living and not take anything from the price of the dead, then the buyer does not swear because if the seller withdraws from his claim, there is no point in administering the oath to the buyer. Al-Zayla'i said: This is like the first in Tabyin al-Haqa'iq (4:309).
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on mutual oaths (tahaluf) when part of a sold commodity is destroyed before delivery. The dispute centers on whether the oaths apply only to the remaining (living) part or also to the destroyed part, and under what conditions. The text begins by stating a ruling: if the seller agrees to forgo the share of the destroyed from the price, then the oaths proceed only on the living part, and the destroyed is treated as if it never existed in the contract. Muhammad al-Shaybani argues that oaths apply to both the living and the destroyed, because total destruction does not prevent oaths, so partial destruction a fortiori should not prevent them. Abu Yusuf argues that prevention of oaths is due to destruction, so it should be limited to the extent of the destruction; the ruling does not exceed its cause. Abu Hanifa's position is that oaths after taking possession are established by textual proof (nass) contrary to analogy (qiyas) while the commodity is intact. Since the commodity is the name of all its parts, it is no longer the same after part is lost, and what is established contrary to analogy cannot be extended to new cases. The text then gives responses to Muhammad and Abu Yusuf. To Muhammad: partial destruction requires estimation of value, which is unknown in the subject of oath, so it is impermissible. To Abu Yusuf: the oath on the living can only be based on its share of the price, which requires division by value estimated by conjecture, which is not permissible unless the seller agrees to relinquish the share of the destroyed. The passage then discusses the interpretation of an exception in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'He takes the living and nothing for him.' The scholars of Balkh held that this exception applies to the buyer's oath, meaning the buyer does not swear if the seller takes the living. Khwahar Zadah and al-Zayla'i criticize this view. The majority hold that the exception applies to the oath (tahaluf) itself, so the default is oaths unless the seller chooses to take the living and forgo the price of the dead. Another view applies it to the buyer's oath as well but with different reasoning. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the exception relates to the oath, as the majority say. The authority weight is from the mutakkhirin (later scholars) commentary.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oaths between buyer and seller when they disagree about the price or condition of a sold item, leading to rescission of the contract if both swear.
هالك (hālik): The part of the sold commodity that is destroyed or perished before delivery.
قائم (qā'im): The remaining (living) part of the commodity that still exists.
استثناء (istithnā'): An exception clause in the text of al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'except that he takes the living and nothing for him'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the response to Muhammad's argument that partial destruction does not prevent oaths?
A) Partial destruction requires estimation of value, which is unknown in the subject of oath, so it is impermissible.
B) Muhammad's argument is correct and oaths apply to both parts.
C) The response is that oaths are limited to the extent of destruction.
D) The response is that the oaths are only on the living part.

Key Takeaway
When part of a sold commodity perishes, Abu Hanifa's position is that tahāluf only applies to the living part if the seller relinquishes the price of the destroyed part; this is the mufta bihi.

Dispute on the Exception: Tahalf or Buyer's Oath?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on interpretation of exception in sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two competing interpretations of the exception in the case of two slaves sold where one dies, and evaluate which view is more apparent (أظهر) according to the later Hanafi scholars.

Core Idea
An exception in a legal text should refer to the immediately preceding ruling unless context dictates otherwise.

This passage delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute about an exception clause in al-Jami' al-Saghir. The scenario: a buyer purchases two slaves; one dies before delivery, and the seller claims a higher price. The text says 'he takes the live one and nothing for him' – but does this exception eliminate the need for mutual oaths (tahalf) or only the buyer's oath? The scholars of Balkh and al-Kaysani argued one way, the majority another. The commentator weighs the arguments and gives preference to the mutual oath interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario and the exception
Understand the case: two slaves bought, one dies. The seller claims a higher price for the live one. The exception in al-Jami' al-Saghir is 'he takes the live one and nothing for him' – what does 'nothing' refer to?
The exception is from the general procedure of oath-taking.
2
State the view of the Balkh scholars (exception applies to buyer's oath)
According to Masayikh Balkh, the exception means that if the seller takes the live slave and gives up any claim on the deceased's price, then the buyer does not need to swear. This is based on the idea that the seller's abandonment renders the buyer's oath unnecessary.
They interpret 'nothing' as nothing from the increase claimed.
3
State the view of the majority (exception applies to mutual oath)
The majority of later scholars hold that the exception refers to the mutual oath (tahalf). Since the text immediately before the exception mentions tahalf, the exception should be applied to that. Also, once the deceased is removed from the sale, only the price of the live slave remains disputed, which requires tahalf.
This view is considered أظهر (more apparent) by the commentator.
4
Analyze the criticism of the Balkh view
Khwahrizada and al-Zayla'i criticize the Balkh view: if taking the live slave is a settlement, it should be dependent on the seller's will, and there is no benefit in taking the slave without receiving the deceased's price. The objection argues that the interpretation lacks wisdom and textual support.
Al-Kaysani offers a refinement, but the majority view remains preferred.
5
Explain the tarjih (preference) for the mutual oath view
The commentator states that the mutual oath view is أظهر because the exception's context (mention of tahalf) and the removal of the impediment (dead slave) make mutual oath the natural procedure. The disagreement is resolved by preferring the majority view.
This is an example of tarjih within the Hanafi school using textual and rational arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا تخريج بعض المشايخ، وينصرفُ الاستثناء عندهم إلى التحالف، وقالوا: إن المراد بقوله في «الجامع الصغير»: يأخذ الحي ولا شيء له: أي لا يأخذ من ثمن الهالك شيئاً أصلاً.
وقال بعض المشايخ: توضيح الكلام بحيث ينكشف به المرام: إنهم اختلفوا في انصراف هذا الاستثناء، فقال مشايخ بلخ: إن هذا الاستثناء ينصرف إلى يمين المشتري، ومعناه: إنَّ البائع يأخذ الحي منهما صُلحاً عما يدعيه المشتري من الزيادة، ويُجْعَلُ صلحهما على هذا العبد كصلحها على عبد آخر.
وصار تقدير ما في «الكتاب» على قول هؤلاء المشايخ: لا يتحالفان عند الإمام، ويكون القول قول المشتري مع يمينه إلا أن يأخذ البائع الحي ولا يأخذ شيئاً آخر فحينئذ لا يحلف المشتري.
قال شيخ الإسلام خُواهر زَادَه: هذا لا يقوى؛ لأنَّ الأخذ معلق بمشيئة البائع، ولو كان أخذ الحي بطريق الصلح لكان معلقاً بمشيئتها، وقال الزيلعي: ليس في هذا الأخذ فائدةٌ [له] أيضاً ولا يحتملها، فلا يصار إليه؛ لأنَّ ترك حصة الهالك من الثمن من غير بدل يقابله ليس من الحكمة. انتهى.
وذهب عامة المشايخ إلى أنَّ هذا الاستثناء ينصرف إلى التحالف، فصار تقدير ما في الكتاب على قول هؤلاء يتحالفان عند الإمام إلا أن يشاء البائع أن يأخذ الحي، ولا يأخذ من ثمن الميت شيئاً، فحينئذ يتحالفان؛ لأنَّ المذكور قبل الاستثناء هو التحالف دون اليمين، فكان صرفُ الاستثناء إلى المذكور أولى.
وقال بعضهم: إنَّ هذا الاستثناء ينصرفُ إلى يمين المشتري؛ لأنَّ البائع إذا رضي أن يأخذ الحي ولا يأخذ من ثمن الميت شيئاً، فحينئذ لا يحلف المشتري؛ لأن البائع إذا عرض عن دعواه فلا معنى لتحليف المشتري، قال الزيلعي: وهذا مثل الأول في عدم الفائدة. انتهى.
وقال الإمامُ الكَيْسَانِيُّ: يأخذ البائع في حق الهالك من المشتري ما يقر به المشتري، فحينئذ لا يحلف؛ لأنَّ الاستحلاف إنما شرع في حق المشتري إذا كان ينكر ما يدعيه البائع من الزيادة، فإذا ترك البائع دعوى الزيادة وأخذ الحي ورضي به المشتري فلا حاجة إلى استحلاف المشتري، والصحيح هو ترك دعوى الزيادة في الثمن لا ترك عن الهالك؛ لأنَّ البائع لا يتركُ من ثمن الهالك كله، وإنما يترك الزائد على ما يقرُّ به المشتري.
ومعنى قوله: لا يأخذ شيئاً؛ أي لا يأخذ من الزيادة التي يدعيها شيئاً، وعلى هذا التقدير ينصرفُ الاستثناء إلى يمين المشتري، ومن أصحابنا من قال: ينصرفُ الاستثناء إلى التحالف، وهو أظهر؛ لأنَّ المانع من التحالف وهو الهالك قد زال بخروجه من أن يكون مبيعاً، فصار كأنَّ المبيع هو الحي وحده، أو يرضاه بما أقر به المشتري من ثمن الهالك، فلم يبق الاختلاف بينهما إلا في ثمن الحي فيتحالفان، فأيهما نكل لزمه دعوى الآخر.
(١) قوله: يأخذ من ثمن الهالك؛ يعني إنَّ البائع يأخذ في حصة الهالك من المشتري ما يقر به المشتري، ويترك حصة الهالكِ على حسب دعواه، فلا شيء له من حصة الهالك إلا ما قال المشتري. 
(٢) قوله: فالاستثناء ... الخ؛ تفريع على ما تقدَّمَ من قول بعض المشايخ.
This is a derivation of some scholars, and according to them the exception applies to mutual swear (tahalf). They said: what is meant by his statement in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'He takes the live one and nothing for him' means: he does not take anything from the price of the deceased at all. And some scholars said: Clarification of the discourse so that the intended meaning is disclosed: They differed about the application of this exception. The scholars of Balkh said: This exception applies to the buyer's oath. Its meaning is: the seller takes the live one of them as a settlement for what the buyer claims of the increase, and their settlement regarding this slave is like a settlement regarding another slave. The estimation of what is in the book according to these scholars becomes: They do not mutually swear before Imam (Abu Hanifa), and the buyer's word is accepted with his oath, except if the seller takes the live one and does not take anything else, then the buyer does not take an oath. Shaykh al-Islam Khwahrizada said: This is not strong, because taking is dependent on the seller's will, and if taking the live one was by way of settlement, it would be dependent on his will. Al-Zayla'i said: There is no benefit in this taking for him also, and it is not plausible, so it is not resorted to, because leaving the deceased's share of the price without a corresponding consideration is not wise. End. The majority of scholars went to the view that this exception applies to mutual oath. So the estimation of what is in the book according to these scholars becomes: They mutually swear before Imam (Abu Hanifa) unless the seller wishes to take the live one and does not take anything from the price of the deceased, then they mutually swear, because what is mentioned before the exception is mutual oath, not the oath, so applying the exception to what is mentioned is more appropriate. And some said: This exception applies to the buyer's oath, because if the seller agrees to take the live one and does not take anything from the price of the deceased, then the buyer does not take an oath, because when the seller abandons his claim, there is no meaning in making the buyer swear. Al-Zayla'i said: This is like the first view in lack of benefit. End. Imam al-Kaysani said: The seller takes from the buyer regarding the deceased what the buyer confesses, then he does not swear, because the oath was legislated for the buyer only when he denies what the seller claims of the increase. So if the seller abandons the claim of increase and takes the live one and the buyer agrees, there is no need to make the buyer swear. The correct view is that he abandons the claim of increase in the price, not the claim about the deceased, because the seller does not leave all of the price of the deceased; rather he leaves the excess over what the buyer confesses. The meaning of his statement: 'he does not take anything' means he does not take anything from the increase he claims. According to this estimation, the exception applies to the buyer's oath. Some of our companions said: The exception applies to mutual oath, and it is more apparent, because the impediment to mutual oath, namely the deceased, has been removed by its exclusion from being sold, so it is as if the sold item is only the live one, or [if] he is satisfied with what the buyer confessed of the price of the deceased, then there remains no difference between them except regarding the price of the live one, so they mutually swear, and whichever of them refuses, he is bound by the other's claim. (1) His statement: 'he takes from the price of the deceased' means that the seller takes from the buyer regarding the deceased's share what the buyer confesses, and leaves the deceased's share according to his claim, so he has nothing from the deceased's share except what the buyer said. (2) His statement: 'so the exception... etc.' is a branch on what preceded from the statement of some scholars.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the interpretation of an exception (istithna') in a case where a buyer purchases two slaves and one dies before delivery. The dispute concerns whether the exception refers to the mutual oath (tahalf) between buyer and seller or to the buyer's oath alone. The scholars of Balkh held that the exception applies to the buyer's oath: the seller may take the live slave as a settlement for the increase claimed by the buyer, and then the buyer does not swear. Shaykh al-Islam Khwahrizada and al-Zayla'i criticized this view for lack of benefit and logic. The majority of later scholars (عامة المشايخ) held that the exception applies to mutual oath: they still perform tahalf because the reference before the exception is tahalf. Some held it applies to the buyer's oath again but al-Zayla'i dismissed it. Al-Kaysani refined the view: the seller only abandons the claim of increase, not the entire price of the deceased, so the exception applies to the buyer's oath. However, the text concludes that the more apparent (أظهر) view is that the exception refers to mutual oath, because the deceased is no longer part of the sale, so the dispute is only over the live slave's price, warranting tahalf. This shows the rich internal debate within the Hanafi school and the process of tarjih (preferring one view).

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithnā'): An exception clause in a legal ruling or contract that excludes a specific case from the general rule.
التحالف (al-taḥāluf): Mutual oath-taking between two litigants when there is a dispute over the price or object of sale and no clear evidence.
يمين المشتري (yamīn al-mushtarī): The oath of the buyer, which when combined with the seller's abandonment of claim, resolves the dispute without mutual oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the more apparent view (أظهر) in this passage, what does the exception (الاستثناء) refer to in the case of two slaves sold where one dies?
A) The buyer's oath (يمين المشتري)
B) The seller's oath (يمين البائع)
C) Mutual oath (التحالف)
D) There is no oath at all

Key Takeaway
The exception in a legal text should be referred to the immediately preceding ruling; here, the preceding discussion of tahalf makes it the more apparent interpretation.

The Exception in Spoiled Goods Disputes: Buyer's Oath or Mutual Tahaluf?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the interpretation of an exception in spoiled goods sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi interpretations of the exception (istithna) in spoiled goods disputes and identify which one is considered more apparent (azhar) with its reasoning.

Core Idea
The exception shifts from buyer's oath to mutual tahaluf when the spoiled goods are removed from the dispute.

In sale disputes involving spoiled goods, the Hanafi school has a specific ruling: the seller takes only what the buyer acknowledges from the price of the spoiled goods. But an exception clause in the text causes a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars: does the exception refer to the buyer's oath (i.e., he need not swear) or to the mutual oath-taking procedure (tahaluf) itself? This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each view and why the latter is considered more sound.

Guided Path
1
State the baseline ruling
Identify the rule: in spoiled goods cases, the seller takes only the amount the buyer acknowledges. The seller does not take the increase he claims.
2
Identify the exception clause
Locate the exception: 'unless the seller is satisfied to take the living goods and does not contest regarding the spoiled.'
This triggers the dispute.
3
State Imam al-Kaysani's view
He holds the exception refers to the buyer's oath: when the seller drops the claim, the buyer does not need to swear.
The buyer's oath is only needed when he denies the seller's claim.
4
State the opposing view (some companions)
They hold the exception refers to mutual tahaluf: because the spoiled goods are no longer part of the sale, the obstacle to tahaluf is removed, so they should take oaths regarding the living goods.
This is considered 'azhar' (more apparent).
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the first view, only the buyer swears; under the second, both parties swear. The latter is preferred.
