يأخذ من ثمن الهالك بقدر ما أقرَّ به المشتري ، ولا يأخذ الزيادة ، فالاستثناء يَنْصَرِفُ إلى يمين المشتري لا إلى التحالف، يعني أنهما لا يتحالفان ، ويكون القولُ قول المشتري مع يمينه إلا أن يرضى البائع أن يأخذ الحي، ولا يخاصمه في الهالك ، فحينئذ لا يحلف المشتري ؛ لأنَّه إنَّما يحلف إذا كان منكراً ما يدعيه البائع، فإذا أخذ صُلْحاً عن جميع ما ادعاه على المشتري ، فلا حاجة إلى تحليف المشتري. عدم الفائدة. انتهى. وقال الإمامُ الكَيْسَانِيُّ : يأخذ البائع في حق الهالك من المشتري ما يقر به المشتري، فحينئذ لا يحلف ؛ لأنَّ الاستحلاف إنما شرع في حق المشتري إذا كان ينكر ما يدعيه البائع من الزيادة، فإذا ترك البائع دعوى الزيادة وأخذ الحي ورضي به المشتري فلا حاجة إلى استحلاف المشتري ، والصحيح هو ترك دعوى الزيادة في الثمن لا ترك عن الهالك ؛ لأنَّ البائع لا يتركُ من ثمن الهالك كله ، وإنما يترك الزائد على ما يقرُّ به المشتري. ومعنى قوله : لا يأخذ شيئاً ؛ أي لا يأخذ من الزيادة التي يدعيها شيئاً، وعلى هذا التقدير ينصرفُ الاستثناء إلى يمين المشتري، ومن أصحابنا مـن قـال : ينصرفُ الاستثناء إلى التحالف، وهو أظهر ؛ لأنَّ المانع من التحالف وهو الهالك قد زال بخروجه من أن يكون مبيعاً، فصار كأنَّ المبيع هو الحي وحده ، أو يرضاه بما أقر به المشتري من ثمن الهالك، فلم يبق الاختلاف بينهما إلا في ثمن الحي فيتحالفان ، فأيهما نكل لزمه دعوى الآخر). [١) قوله : يأخذ من ثمن الهالك ؛ يعني إنَّ البائع يأخذ في حصة الهالك من المشتري ما يقر به المشتري، ويترك حصة الهالكِ على حسب دعواه ، فلا شيء له من حصة الهالك إلا ما قال المشتري. [٢) قوله : فالاستثناء ...الخ ؛ تفريع على ما تقدَّمَ من قول بعض المشايخ
He takes from the price of the spoiled goods to the extent that the buyer acknowledged, and he does not take the increase. So the exception refers to the buyer's oath, not to mutual oath-taking, meaning they do not take mutual oaths, and the saying is the buyer's saying with his oath, unless the seller is satisfied that he takes the living [goods] and does not contest regarding the spoiled goods. In that case the buyer does not take an oath, because he only takes an oath if he is denying what the seller claims. When he takes a settlement for all that he claimed from the buyer, there is no need to make the buyer take an oath. No use. End. And Imam al-Kaysani said: The seller takes from the spoiled goods' share from the buyer what the buyer acknowledges, so then he does not take an oath, because the oath is only legislated for the buyer when he denies the increase that the seller claims. When the seller abandons the claim of the increase, takes the living [goods], and the buyer is satisfied with that, there is no need to make the buyer take an oath. The correct view is that he abandons the claim of increase in the price, not the claim regarding the spoiled goods, because the seller does not abandon all of the price of the spoiled goods, only the increase over what the buyer acknowledges. And the meaning of his saying 'he does not take anything' is that he does not take anything from the increase he claims. On this basis, the exception refers to the buyer's oath. And some of our companions said: The exception refers to mutual oath-taking, and it is more apparent, because the obstacle to mutual oath-taking—namely the spoiled goods—has been removed by its exit from being a sale, so it is as if the only sold item is the living [goods], or he is satisfied with what the buyer acknowledged of the price of the spoiled goods. So the disagreement between them remains only regarding the price of the living [goods], so they take mutual oaths, and whichever of them fails to take the oath is bound by the other's claim.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage deals with a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the interpretation of an exception (istithna) in a case where some goods are spoiled (halik) and the buyer acknowledges a certain price. The initial ruling states that the seller takes from the price of spoiled goods only what the buyer acknowledges, not the increase he claims. The exception in the text ('except that the seller is satisfied...') is said by some to refer to the buyer's oath (i.e., the buyer does not need to swear if the seller drops the claim), while others say it refers to the mutual oath-taking (tahaluf) itself—that they do not need tahaluf. Imam al-Kaysani's view is that the exception relates to the buyer's oath. However, some of our companions (masa'ikh) argue that it refers to tahaluf, and this is considered more apparent (azhar). Their reasoning: once the spoiled goods are removed from the sale (because the seller accepts the buyer's acknowledgment), the only remaining dispute is about the living goods, so the normal rule of tahaluf applies. The passage thus presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with a preference for the tahaluf interpretation.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna): The exception clause in the legal ruling, specifically whether it modifies the buyer's oath or the mutual oath-taking.
التحالف (al-tahaluf): The mutual oath-taking procedure in dispute resolution where each party swears to their claim.
الهالك (al-halik): The spoiled or destroyed goods that are no longer in existence as a sale item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do some Hanafi scholars hold that the exception refers to mutual tahaluf (instead of the buyer's oath)?
A) Because the spoiled goods are no longer part of the sale, so the normal rule of tahaluf applies.
B) Because the buyer has already acknowledged the price, so no oath is needed.
C) Because the seller has abandoned his claim entirely.
D) Because the buyer is considered truthful by default.

Key Takeaway
In spoiled goods disputes, once the seller accepts the buyer's acknowledgment and takes the living goods, the more apparent Hanafi view applies mutual tahaluf rather than a single buyer's oath.

Tahaluf after Iqala: Salam vs Sale
Rulings on tahaluf after iqala in salam and sale contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why iqala in salam does not lead to tahaluf while iqala in sale does, and apply the rule to determine the legal outcome of a dispute after iqala.

Core Idea
Iqala in salam is a final dropping of debt; iqala in sale is a revocable reversal.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary clarifies a critical distinction between the effect of mutual rescission (iqala) in salam contracts versus ordinary sale contracts. When the parties later dispute the amount of the price or capital, the legal consequence differs fundamentally: in salam, no mutual oath-taking (tahaluf) occurs, and the contract does not revive; in sale, tahaluf takes place and the original sale returns. Understanding the rationale—based on the nature of iqala as a dropping of debt (isqat) versus a rescission (naqd)—is essential for correct application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage addresses two distinct situations: (1) iqala of a salam contract followed by a dispute over the capital; (2) iqala of a sale contract followed by a dispute over the price. Note that the ruling is different in each case.
2
Understand the rationale for no tahaluf in salam
The key legal reason (ta'lil) is that iqala in salam constitutes a dropping (isqat) of the debt (the obligation to deliver the goods). Since a dropped debt cannot be revived, tahaluf—which would require reinstating the contract—is impermissible. The muslam ilayhi (seller) is believed with his oath.
This is based on istihsan, not strict analogy.
3
Compare with iqala in sale
In a regular sale, iqala is not a dropping but a reversal (faskh) that can be undone. Therefore, if the parties later dispute the price, they perform tahaluf, and the sale returns to its original state, with each party regaining their rights.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is that the goods and price are restored to their original owners.
4
Analyze the example given by al-Zayla'i
Al-Zayla'i provides a concrete example: a slave girl is bought and delivered, then the parties mutually cancel. Afterward, they disagree on the price—buyer says 1000, seller says 500. With no evidence, they perform tahaluf, and the sale is revived, meaning the seller has claim to the price and the buyer to the slave. No restitution is required.
This illustrates the rule for sale iqala.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا في رأس المالِ بعد إقالته ، وصُدِّقَ المسلّمُ إليه إن حَلَفَ، ولا يعودُ السَّلم) : أي أقالا عقد السلم، فوقع الاختلاف في رأس المال، فالقول قول المسلم إليه، ولا تحالف ؛ لأنَّه إن تحالفا تنفسخُ الإقالة ويعودُ السَّلَم، وذا لا يجوز؛ لأنَّ إقالة السلم إسقاط الدين، والساقط لا يعود. (ولو اختلفا في قدر الثَّمن بعد إقالة البيع تحالفا ، وعاد البيع) الزيلعي [١] قوله : بعد إقالته ؛ قيّد به ؛ لأنهما لو اختلفا قبلها في قدره تحالفا ؛ كالاختلاف في جنسه ونوعِهِ وصفتِهِ كالاختلاف في المسلّم فيه في الوجوه الأربعة. كذا في «البحر (۲) [٢]قوله : لأن إقالة... الخ ؛ حاصله : إنّ الإقالة في باب السلم لا تحتمل النقض ؛ لأنه إسقاط فلا يعود بخلاف البيع. [٣] قوله : ولو اختلفا... الخ ؛ صورته أنه اشترى جاريةٌ وقبضها ثم تقايلا ، ثمَّ اختلفا في الثمن بأن قال المشتري : كان الثمن ألفاً، فعليك أن ترد الألف، وقال البائع : كان خمسمئة فعليَّ ردُّ خمسمئة ، ولا بيّنة لهما ، فإنّهما يتحالفان ويعود البيعُ الأول، حتى يكون حق البائع في الثمن، وحق المشتري في المبيع ، كما كان قبل الإقالة، فلا يجب على كل واحد منهما أن يرد على صاحبه شيئاً. (٤) قوله : تحالفا ؛ لو كان كلّ من المبيع والثمن مقبوضاً، ولم يرده المشتري إلى
And not in the capital after its iqala, and the muslam ilayhi is believed if he swears, and the salam does not return. Meaning: They cancelled the salam contract, then they disputed over the capital, so the statement is of the muslam ilayhi, and there is no tahaluf, because if they were to perform tahaluf, the iqala would be rescinded and the salam would return, and that is not permissible because the iqala of salam is a dropping of a debt, and what is dropped does not return. (And if they dispute over the amount of the price after the iqala of sale, they perform tahaluf and the sale returns). Al-Zayla'i: [1] His saying: 'after its iqala' – he restricted it because if they dispute before it over its amount, they perform tahaluf, just as in the dispute over its type, kind, and description, like the dispute over the subject matter of salam in four ways. As in al-Bahr. [2] His saying: 'because iqala...' – the summary: that iqala in salam does not admit of revocation because it is a dropping, so it does not return, unlike sale. [3] His saying: 'and if they dispute...' – its example: He bought a slave girl and took possession, then they cancelled, then they disputed over the price, e.g., the buyer said: the price was one thousand, so you must return one thousand; and the seller said: it was five hundred, so I must return five hundred. Neither has evidence. Then they perform tahaluf and the first sale returns, so that the seller has the right to the price and the buyer has the right to the goods, as before the iqala. Neither is obliged to return anything to the other. [4] His saying: 'they perform tahaluf' – if both the goods and the price had been delivered, and the buyer did not return...
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage deals with two separate scenarios involving iqala (mutual rescission). First, in a salam contract (advance payment for future delivery), if the parties cancel the contract and then dispute the amount of the capital (the paid sum), the ruling is that no tahaluf occurs; the muslam ilayhi (the seller who received the payment) is believed with his oath, and the salam contract does not revive. The reason is that iqala in salam is a dropping of a debt (the obligation to deliver the goods), and what is dropped cannot be reinstated. If they were to perform tahaluf and one party's oath caused the iqala to be rescinded, the salam would return, which is not allowed. Second, in a regular sale, if the parties cancel the sale (iqala) and then dispute the price, they do perform tahaluf, and the original sale returns. The example clarifies: after buying a slave girl, both parties cancel, then disagree about the price; they both take oaths, and the sale revives, meaning each party retains their original rights (seller to price, buyer to goods). The notes from al-Zayla'i emphasize that if the dispute occurs before the iqala, they would perform tahaluf in both cases, but after iqala, the rules differ due to the nature of the contracts.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqala): Mutual rescission or cancellation of a contract by both parties. In salam, it is treated as a dropping of debt; in sale, it is a revocation.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital or principal amount paid in a salam contract.
المسلم إليه (al-muslam ilayhi): The seller who receives the advance payment in a salam contract.
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance and delivery of the goods is deferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract that has been cancelled (iqala), what happens if the parties later dispute the amount of the capital?
A) They perform tahaluf and the salam is revived.
B) They perform tahaluf but the salam does not revive.
C) The muslam ilayhi is believed with his oath, and no tahaluf occurs.
D) The buyer is believed with his oath, and no tahaluf occurs.

Key Takeaway
Iqala in salam is a final dropping of debt, so no tahaluf after; iqala in sale is a reversible rescission, so tahaluf occurs and the sale returns.

Dispute after Iqalah of Salam: Seller's Word on Capital
Ruling on capital dispute after salam iqalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when parties dispute the capital after iqalah of salam and explain why the salam does not return.

Core Idea
Iqalah of salam drops the debt — seller's word trusted, salam cannot revive.

After both parties agree to cancel a salam contract, what happens if they disagree about the price paid? The Hanafi school has a clear rule that protects the finality of the cancellation. In this lesson, we explore the reasoning behind this ruling and the critical distinction between iqalah in sale and iqalah in salam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The parties have agreed to iqalah (mutual rescission) of a salam contract. Then they dispute the amount of capital (the price paid by the buyer).
The dispute is about the capital, not the subject matter.
2
State the ruling
The seller (musallam ilayh) is believed if he swears. The salam contract does not return.
No mutual oath (tahalluf) is required.
3
Understand the reason
Iqalah of salam is a dropping of debt (isqat al-dayn). What is dropped cannot be restored. Allowing mutual oaths would imply the iqalah was invalid and the debt would revive, which is impermissible.
Contrast with iqalah of a regular sale, which is a rescission that can be undone.
4
Contrast with dispute before iqalah
If the dispute occurs before iqalah, then mutual oaths are taken, as in a dispute over species, type, description, etc. of the salam subject matter.
The phrase 'after its iqalah' is a key restriction.
5
Note the distinction from sale
In a regular sale, iqalah is a rescission of the contract and can be reversed. In salam, iqalah is an absolute dropping of the debt, so it is final.
This is a unique Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱] ولا في رأس المالِ بعد إقالته ، وصُدِّقَ المسلّمُ إليه إن حَلَفَ، ولا يعودُ السَّلم) : أي أقالا عقد السلم، فوقع الاختلاف في رأس المال، فالقول قول المسلم إليه، ولا تحالف ؛ لأنَّه إن تحالفا تنفسخُ الإقالة ويعودُ السَّلَم، وذا لا يجوز؛ لأنَّ إقالة السلم إسقاط الدين، والساقط لا يعود.

الزيلعي [۱] قوله : بعد إقالته ؛ قيّد به ؛ لأنهما لو اختلفا قبلها في قدره تحالفا ؛ كالاختلاف في جنسه ونوعِهِ وصفتِهِ كالاختلاف في المسلّم فيه في الوجوه الأربعة. كذا في «البحر (۲)

[٢]قوله : لأن إقالة... الخ ; حاصله : إنّ الإقالة في باب السلم لا تحتمل النقض ; لأنه إسقاط فلا يعود بخلاف البيع.
And not in the capital after its iqalah, and the seller (musallam ilayh) is believed if he swears, and the salam does not return. (Explanation:) They cancelled a salam contract, and dispute occurred about the capital, so the word is the word of the seller, and there is no mutual oath; because if they take mutual oaths, the iqalah would be rescinded and the salam would return, and that is not permissible because the iqalah of salam is a dropping of debt, and what is dropped does not return.

Al-Zayla'i: 'After its iqalah' – he restricted it because if they disputed before it about its amount, they would take mutual oaths, like the dispute about its species, type, and description, like the dispute about the subject matter in the four aspects. Similarly in 'al-Bahr'.

'Because iqalah... the summary is that iqalah in the chapter of salam does not admit rescission because it is a dropping, so it does not return, unlike sale.'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage discusses what happens when a salam contract is mutually cancelled (iqalah) and the parties later dispute the amount of capital (the price paid by the buyer). The ruling is that the seller (musallam ilayh) is believed with his oath, and importantly, the salam contract does not revert to being valid. The commentary explains the reasoning: iqalah of salam is a dropping (isqat) of the debt (the obligation to deliver the goods), and a dropped obligation cannot be restored. If mutual oaths were taken, it would imply the iqalah was invalid, which is not allowed. The restriction 'after its iqalah' indicates that if the dispute occurred before the cancellation, they would take mutual oaths, as in the case of dispute over the species, type, or description of the salam subject matter. Thus, iqalah in salam is a final cancellation, unlike iqalah in a regular sale which can be rescinded.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqalah): Mutual rescission of a contract, resulting in the return of the subject matter and price.
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where the buyer pays the price immediately for future delivery of specified goods.
المسلّم إليه (al-musallam ilayh): The seller in a salam contract; the recipient of the capital (price).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The seller is believed with his oath and the salam does not return.
C) The buyer is believed with his oath and the salam returns.
D) They each produce evidence and the one with stronger evidence prevails.

Key Takeaway
The seller's word is accepted with oath after iqalah of salam, and the contract cannot be revived because iqalah here is a dropping of debt.

Mutual Oaths After Iqalah: Internal Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mutual oaths after sale iqalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling on mutual oaths after iqalah of a sale and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf from that of Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Mutual oaths after iqalah are not absolute; possession determines extension.

When a sale is mutually rescinded (iqalah), the parties may later dispute the original price. The default ruling in Hanafi fiqh is that they take mutual oaths (tahalluf) and the sale returns. However, if the buyer has already returned the sold item to the seller, an important internal ikhtilaf arises. In this lesson, we examine the reasoning behind the different views and their practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default scenario
Recognize the scenario: a sale is cancelled via iqalah, then the parties dispute the price. There is no evidence. The default ruling is mutual oaths and return of sale.
This is the starting point before considering possession.
2
Locate the ikhtilaf condition
Note that the ikhtilaf concerns whether the sold item has been returned to the seller by virtue of the iqalah. If yes, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no mutual oaths; Muhammad says mutual oaths still apply.
Possession is the key variable.
3
Analyze the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They argue that the ruling of mutual oaths in sale is established by the Sunnah for the original sale only. Iqalah is a rescission (faskh), and after possession, the analogy to sale is dropped.
They limit qiyas: before possession it operates, after possession it does not.
4
Analyze Muhammad's reasoning
Muhammad holds that the 'illa (cause) for mutual oaths is the denial of the other's claim, which exists regardless of possession. Therefore, the ruling should extend to iqalah even after possession.
He treats the 'illa as operative in all cases.
5
Determine the practical difference
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if the item is returned, the iqalah stands and no mutual oaths are taken. According to Muhammad, mutual oaths occur, reviving the original sale.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولو اختلفا في قدر الثمن بعد إقالة البيع تحالفا، وعاد البيع) وإذا انعدم التحالف أوجب اعتبار الدعوى والإنكار، فيكون القول للمنكر، وهو العبد، وإن أقام أحدهما بيّنةً تقبل، ويعملُ بها ; لأنه نور دعواه، وإن أقاما البينة فكانت بينة المولى أولى; لأنها تثبتُ الزيادة إلا أنه إذا أدَّى قدر ما أقامَ البيِّنة عليه يعتق ; لأنه أثبت الحريَّةَ لنفسه عند أداء هذا القدر، فوجب قبول بينتِهِ على ذلك. صرَّحَ به، وغيره.

[٣] قوله : ولو اختلفا... الخ ; صورته أنه اشترى جاريةٌ وقبضها ثم تقايلا ، ثمَّ اختلفا في الثمن بأن قال المشتري : كان الثمن ألفاً، فعليك أن ترد الألف، وقال البائع : كان خمسمئة فعليَّ ردُّ خمسمئة ، ولا بيّنة لهما ، فإنّهما يتحالفان ويعود البيعُ الأول، حتى يكون حق البائع في الثمن، وحق المشتري في المبيع ، كما كان قبل الإقالة، فلا يجب على كل واحد منهما أن يرد على صاحبه شيئاً. (٤) قوله : تحالفا ; لو كان كلّ من المبيع والثمن مقبوضاً، ولم يرده المشتري إلى بائعه بحكم الإقالة لا تحالف عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف . خلافاً لمحمد ; لأنه يرى النص معلولاً بعد القبض أيضاً ; لوجود الإنكار من كل واحدٍ من المتبايعين لما يدعيه الآخر، وهذه العلة لا تتفاوت بين كون المبيع مقبوضاً أو غير مقبوض ، ولما كان النص عنده معلولاً تعدى حكمه من البيع إلى الإقالة، وإن كان بعد قبض البائع المبيع بعد الإقالة. وهما قالا : كان ينبغي أن لا تحالف مطلقاً ; لأنه إنما ثبت في المبيع المطلق بالسنة ، والإقالة فسخ في حقهما إلا أنه قبل القبض على وفق القياس، فوجب القياس كما قسنا الإجارة على البيع قبل القبض، والوارث على العاقد، والقيمة على العين فيما إذا استهلكه في يد البائع غير المشتري.
(And if they differ about the amount of the price after the iqalah of the sale, they take mutual oaths, and the sale returns.) And if the mutual oath is absent, then the claim and denial are considered, so the word is of the denier, who is the slave. If one of them produces evidence, it is accepted and acted upon. If both produce evidence, the evidence of the master is given precedence because it establishes the increase, except that if he pays the amount for which he established evidence, the slave becomes free, because he has established freedom for himself upon payment of this amount, so his evidence must be accepted. He stated this, and others.

[3] His statement: 'And if they differ...' etc.; its scenario is that he bought a female slave and took possession, then they mutually cancelled, then they disputed about the price. The buyer said: 'The price was one thousand, so you must return one thousand.' The seller said: 'It was five hundred, so I must return five hundred.' And there is no evidence for either. Then they take mutual oaths and the original sale returns, so that the seller's right is in the price and the buyer's right is in the sold item, as before the iqalah. So neither is required to return anything to the other. (4) His statement: 'They take mutual oaths' - if both the sold item and the price were already in possession, and the buyer did not return it to its seller by virtue of the iqalah, then there is no mutual oath according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Contrary to Muhammad, because he considers the ruling to be effective after possession as well, due to the existence of denial by each of the two parties of what the other claims, and this cause does not differ between the sold item being in possession or not. Since he considers the ruling effective, he extends it from sale to iqalah, even after the seller has taken possession of the sold item after iqalah. The two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) said: 'It should not be mutual oath at all, because it is established only for an absolute sale by the Sunnah, and iqalah is a rescission in their case, except that before possession it is in accordance with qiyas, so it is necessary to analogize as we analogized ijarah to sale before possession, and the heir to the contractor, and the value to the specific asset when it is consumed in the hands of the seller without the buyer.'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling when a sale is mutually cancelled (iqalah) and the parties dispute the original price. The default rule: they take mutual oaths (tahalluf) and the sale reverts. The commentary provides an example: a slave buys a female slave, then they cancel the sale and dispute the price; they take mutual oaths and the sale returns, so each party retains their original rights. Then an important intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: if the buyer has already returned the purchased item to the seller (through the iqalah), then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, no mutual oaths are taken; the iqalah stands and the dispute is resolved through evidence or the denier's word. Muhammad disagrees, arguing that the cause ('illa) for mutual oaths is the denial of the other's claim, which exists regardless of possession. Therefore, according to Muhammad, mutual oaths are still required. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf respond that the ruling of mutual oaths in sale is established by the Sunnah only for an absolute sale (original contract), not for iqalah. Iqalah is a rescission (faskh) which, before possession, is analogized to sale (hence mutual oaths), but after possession, the ruling is not extended, adhering to the Sunnah's limitation. They cite analogies: ijarah before taking possession, and the case of an heir versus the original contractor.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahalluf): Mutual oath-taking by both parties in a contract dispute when there is no evidence; each swears to their claim, leading to rescission of the contract.
إقالة (iqalah): Mutual rescission of a contract, cancelling the sale and returning the parties to pre-contract status.
علة ('illa): The effective cause or reason underlying a legal ruling, used for qiyas (analogy).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, when is mutual oath (tahalluf) not required after iqalah of a sale?
A) Always, because iqalah is a rescission.
B) When the buyer has returned the sold item to the seller by virtue of the iqalah.
C) When the parties agree on the price.
D) Only when the sold item is a slave.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on mutual oaths after iqalah is not absolute; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf limit it to cases where the sold item has not yet been returned to the seller.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Two Claimants with Equal Evidence: The Hanafi Rule of Equal Division with Option
Rules when two claimants present evidence for ownership or marriage with equal or unequal dates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling when two claimants present equal evidence for purchase from a possessor and explain the option each claimant has to take half or leave.

Core Idea
When two claimants present equal evidence, each gets half with an option—because the contract's condition has changed.

In the Hanafi school, when two outsiders each prove they purchased the same item from a possessor, and their evidence is of equal strength (neither predates), the judge cannot give the whole to either. Instead, the law divides the item between them, giving each half for half the price. But because neither buyer originally consented to half, each has the option to walk away entirely. This ruling arises from a sophisticated understanding of contract consent and is defended against other schools that propose lot-casting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine the parties: a possessor (dhu yad) has an item, and two outsiders (kharijan) each present evidence that they purchased it from him. The evidence must be valid and equal in strength—either both undated or both with the same date.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The judge gives each claimant half of the item for half the price. Each claimant then has the option (takhyir): either accept the half for half the price, or reject it entirely and claim the full price from the seller.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Understand the rationale
Each buyer consented to the contract only for the whole item. Splitting the transaction changes the condition of the contract, giving rise to an option. This is analogous to two unauthorized agents (fuduli) selling the same item and the owner ratifying both sales.
The Hanafi school argues that both sales can be valid if the owner appointed two agents who sold simultaneously.
4
Compare with opposing views
Al-Shafi'i (in one opinion) and Ahmad hold that lots should be cast. Another Shafi'i opinion drops both evidences and reverts to the seller. The Hanafi school responds that contradicting evidence does not necessarily imply lying; simultaneous sales are possible.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Hanafi each gets half with option; under others either lots determine a winner or evidence is dropped.
5
Apply the rule to marriage and precedence
The same principle applies to marriage claims: an outsider claiming a woman married to a possessor must prove priority. For purchase, if one evidence is dated earlier, that claimant takes the whole. The commentary notes that mere claim of precedence is sufficient, but some Hanafi scholars require specification of time.

وبه قال مالك له في رواية والشافعي الله في قول، وقال في قول آخر : يقرع، وبه قال أحمد ، وعن الشافعي الله أيضاً : تسقط البينات، ويرجع إلى البائع، فإن صدق أحدهما سلّم ذلك الشيء له.
(وبترك أحدهما بعدما قَضَى لهما لم يأخذ الآخر كله".
وهو للسابق إن أرخا")
ولنا : أن كل واحدٍ ما رضي بالعقد إلا ليُسَلَّمَ له كل المبيع، فإذا لم يُسَلِّم له اختل رضاه بتفريق الصفقة عليه، فيتخير كما قلنا.
فإن قيل : ينبغي أن تسقط البينتان عندنا أيضاً ؛ لتيقن القاضي بكذب أحدِ الفريقين ؛ لعدم تصور البيعين في شيء واحدٍ بكماله من كل واحد من رجلين في وقت واحد.
أجيب : بأنّ كل فريق شهد بنفس البيع لا بصحته، ويتصوّر وقوع البيعين في وقت واحد بأن وكل المالك رجلين كل واحد على الإنفراد، فباعا معاً، ذكره الشمني، وغيره.
قوله : بعد ما قضى ؛ قيَّد بقوله : ما بعد القضاء ؛ لأنه قبل القضاء لا يأخذ كله ؛ لإثبات أنه اشتراء الكل بلا مزاحم للقضاء.
قوله : لم يأخذ الآخر كلّه ؛ لأنه صار في النصف مقضيّاً عليه، فانفسخ البيع فيه، وإنما قلنا ذلك ؛ لأنه ببينته استحق جميعه، وكان يُسَلّم له لولا بينةُ صاحبه، ولما قضى القاضي بينهما صار مستحقاً عليه، وانفسخ البيع في النصف، فلا يكون له أن يأخذه بعد الانفساخ. بخلاف ما لو ترك أحدهما قبل القضاء به بينهما حيث يكون للآخر أن يأخذ جميعه ؛ لأنه أثبت ببينة أنه اشترى الكلّ، وإنّما يرجع إلى النصف بالمزاحمة ضرورةً القضاء به بينهما ولم يوجد.
فصار نظير تسليم أحد الشفيعين فإنّه إن كان قبل القضاء فللآخر أن يأخذ كل المبيع، وإن كان بعده فليس له أن يأخذ إلا النصف ؛ لأنه يستحق بالسبب كله ، والانقسام للمزاحمة ضرورة القضاء به بينهما.
قوله : وهو للسابق إن أرخا ؛ لأنهما لَمَّا ادَّعيا الشراء من ذي اليد اتفقا على الشراء لكن اختلفا في الوقت، فإذا أرخ أحدهما شهراً والآخر ساعة كما تقدم، فالساعة أولى عند القهستاني. انتهى.
Book of Claims/Chapter of the claim of two men. Except when precedence is established, just as no judgment is given in favor of an outsider against a possessor whose marriage is apparent unless he establishes precedence. If two outsiders provide evidence of purchasing something from a possessor, then each has half for half the price, or he may leave it unless precedence is established. This is like the case where no judgment is given for the outsider's proof against a possessor whose marriage is apparent unless he proves precedence. That is: if a woman is in the hand of a man and his marriage is apparent, and an outsider claims that she is his wife and produces evidence, no judgment is given for him unless he proves that his marriage is earlier. (Then if the two outsiders provide evidence of purchasing something from a possessor, each has half for half the price, or he may leave it): that is, each of them has the option: if he wishes, he takes half of that thing for half the price, and if he wishes, he leaves it. This indicates that mere claim of precedence suffices, as some shaykhs said; others hold that it is necessary to specify, e.g., that the first was in Rajab and the second in Sha'ban. The complete discussion is in al-Fusul al-Imadiyyah. Al-Qahistani mentioned, quoting from al-Khazanah: if one specified a month and the other an hour, the hour takes precedence. End. His statement: 'except when precedence is established' means precedence of his marriage over the marriage of the possessor by evidence, then judgment is given for him because express statement is above indication, so it is not considered with it. His statement: 'Then if they provide evidence' means the two outsiders regarding purchase of something from a possessor, and they did not date or they dated equally. Then each has half: i.e., each of them has half of that thing for half the price if he wishes, and he can claim from the seller half of its price. Or he may leave it: i.e., he may leave the half if he wishes. Because when they are equal in the cause, the judge must judge between them, since it is impossible to give the whole to one. So each has an option because the condition of his contract has changed; perhaps he desires ownership of the whole, so he returns it and takes the whole price. This is like two unauthorized agents each selling to a man and the owner ratifies both sales: each buyer has an option because the condition of his contract has changed; perhaps he desires ownership of the whole. This is the view of Malik in one narration, and al-Shafi'i in one opinion. In another opinion, he said: lots should be cast, and Ahmad held that. Also from al-Shafi'i: the evidences are dropped and the matter reverts to the seller; if he affirms one of them, that thing is given to him. (And if one leaves after judgment has been given for them, the other does not take the whole. And it is for the earlier if they dated.) Our evidence: each one only consented to the contract in order to be given the entire sold item; when he is not given the entire item, his consent is disturbed by the splitting of the transaction, so he has an option as we said. If it is said: the two evidences should be dropped according to us as well, because the judge is certain that one of the two parties is lying, since it is impossible to conceive of two sales of one complete thing from two men at one time. The answer: each party testifies to the sale itself, not to its validity, and it is possible for two sales to occur at one time by the owner appointing two agents each independently, and they sold simultaneously. This was mentioned by al-Shumnuni and others. His statement: 'after judgment' restricts with 'after judgment' because before judgment he does not take the whole, because he proved that he bought the whole without any competing claim for judgment. His statement: 'the other does not take the whole' because he has been adjudged against regarding half, so the sale is rescinded in that half. And we only say that because by his evidence he was entitled to the whole, and it would have been given to him were it not for the evidence of his companion; when the judge judged between them, he became subject to a claim, and the sale was rescinded in half, so he cannot take it after rescission. Contrast with if one leaves before judgment between them, then the other may take the whole because he proved by evidence that he bought the whole, and he only returned to half due to competition out of necessity of judgment between them, which did not occur. This becomes analogous to one of two pre-emptors: if he relinquishes before judgment, the other may take the entire sold property; if after judgment, he may only take half because he is entitled to the whole by cause, and division is due to competition out of necessity of judgment between them. His statement: 'and it is for the earlier if they dated' because when they both claimed purchase from the possessor, they agreed on the purchase but differed on the time. So if one specified a month and the other an hour as mentioned, then the hour takes precedence according to al-Qahistani.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of claims, specifically when two claimants each present evidence of purchase (or marriage) from a person in possession. The Hanafi position is that if neither evidence has a date, or both have the same date, the judge gives each half of the claimed item for half the price. Each claimant has the option to take the half or to leave it entirely; if he leaves, he can claim the full price from the seller. The reasoning is that each contract was made with the expectation of receiving the whole item, and when that becomes impossible, the condition of the contract is altered, giving the buyer an option. This is analogized to two unauthorized agents both selling the same item and the owner ratifying both sales. The Hanafi school rejects the views of Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad who propose lot-casting or dropping of evidence. The text also addresses the scenario where one claimant relinquishes after judgment: then the other does not take the whole because the sale is deemed rescinded in the half he lost. However, if relinquishment occurs before judgment, the other may take the whole. This is analogized to the rule of pre-emption between two pre-emptors. Finally, if the evidences are dated, the one with the earlier date takes the whole, unless the dates are equal (then half each). The commentary elaborates that mere claim of precedence is sufficient to decide, but some Hanafi scholars require specification of time.

Key Terms
ذو يد (dhu yad): The person in possession of the claimed item or marriage, against whom the outsiders bring their claims.
البيّنة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, usually two male witnesses, presented by a claimant to prove his case.
السبق (al-sabq): Precedence in time; if one claimant's evidence shows an earlier date, he wins the whole.
تخيير (takhyir): The option given to each claimant to take half for half price or to leave it entirely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling when two claimants present evidence of buying from a possessor with equal dates?
A) Both evidences are dropped and the seller keeps the item.
B) Lots are cast to decide who gets the item.
C) Each claimant gets half of the item for half the price, with the option to take or leave.
D) The item is given to the one with the strongest evidence.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, equal evidence from two claimants leads to equal division with an option to withdraw, based on contract principles and the possibility of concurrent agency sales.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Resolving Conflicting Purchase Claims and Testimony Conditions
Commentary on ruling for two claimants with purchase evidence from different persons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on conflicting purchase claims and state the conditions required for accepting testimony of purchase.

Core Idea
Equal dates from different sellers means equal shares; priority only when same seller and earlier date.

When two claimants each bring evidence of purchasing the same item from different sellers, how does a judge decide? The Hanafi school provides a clear rule based on the date of the evidence and whether the seller is the same. Additionally, testimony of purchase is not accepted lightly—specific conditions must be met to ensure the seller owned the property. This lesson unpacks both the substantive ruling and the evidentiary requirements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimants and their evidence
Determine whether the two claimants bring evidence from the same seller or different sellers, and whether their evidence includes a date.
The scenario involves two outsiders (kharj) each claiming ownership via purchase from distinct sellers or the same seller.
2
Apply the rule for equal dates
If both have evidence with the same date and from different sellers, they are equal, and the item is divided between them. This is because each establishes ownership for his seller, and the sellers' claims are equal.
Contrast with the case where both claim from the same seller and only one dates—then the one with the date is preferred.
3
Consider possession and priority
If one claimant has physical possession of the item, he is preferred, unless the other has an earlier date and proves it. Possession is a strong indicator of ownership.
This is based on the maxim 'al-yad al-mutlaqatu amāratu al-milk' (possession is a sign of ownership).
4
Validate the testimony conditions
Testimony of purchase is not accepted unless witnesses state that the seller owned the item at the time of sale, or that the seller delivered it to the buyer. If the item is in the seller's hand, less detail is needed.
Reason: a person might sell what he does not own (as agent or trespasser), so the buyer may not acquire ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن بَرْهَنا على شراء شيء متفق تاريخهما من آخر، أو وقت أحدهما فقط استويا وإن بَرْهَنا على شراء شيء متفق تاريخهما من آخر) : أي قال أحدهما : اشتريته من زيد، وقال الآخر : اشتريته من عمرو ، وذكرا تاريخاً، أو وقت أحدهما فقط استويا)، فالحاصلُ : أنه إذا وقت أحدهما فقط، وتلقيا من واحد، فصاحب الوقت أحق ، وإن تلقيا من اثنتين فهما سواء.
وأما الصورة الثانية ؛ فلأنهما لما ادَّعيا من شخص واحدٍ فقد اتفقا على أنَّ الملك له، فمن أثبت منهما التلقي من جهته في زمان لا يزاحمه فيه أحد كان أولى، ولو لم يؤرخا فهما سواء، وإن أرخت إحداهما دون الأخرى، فالمؤرخ أولى، وإن كان مع أحدهما قبض كان هو أولى، ولو أرّخ الآخرُ ما لم يُثبت أنه أقدم تاريخاً. كذا في «رمز الحقائق للعلامة العيني. قوله : وإن برهنا...الخ ؛ يعني إن برهن أحدهما على الشراء من زيد، والآخر عليه من بكر، واتفق تاريخهما ، فهما على سواء ، حتى يكون المبيع بينهما نصفين ؛ لأنَّ كل واحدٍ منهما أثبت الملك لبائعه، وملك بائعه مطلق بلا تاريخ ، فصار كما إذا حضر البائع فادعى الملك المطلق، فيكون بين الخارجين ؛ لاستواء تاريخهما. وكذا لو وقت أحدهما فقط ؛ لأنَّ توقيت أحدهما لا يدلُّ على تقدم الملك ؛ لجواز أن يكون الآخر أقدم ، بخلاف ما إذا كان البائع واحد ؛ لأنهما لو اتفقا على الملك لا يتلقى إلا من جهته، فإذا أثبت أحدهما تاريخاً يحكم به، حتى يتبين أنه تقدمه لشراء غيره، ثم اعلم أن البينة على الشراء لا تقبل حتى يشهدوا أنه اشتراها من فلان، وهو يملكها. كذا في «البحر» معزيا إلى «خزانة الأكمل».
·
وفي «السراج الوهاج»: لا تقبل الشهادة على الشراء من فلان حتى يشهدوا أنه باعها منه ، وهو يومئذ يملكها ، أو شهدوا أنها لهذا المدعي اشتراها من فلان بكذا، ونقد الثمن وسلّمها إليه ؛ لأنَّ الإنسان قد يبيع ما لا يملك، لجواز أن يكون وكيلاً أو متعدياً ، فلا يستحق المشتري الملك بذلك ، فلا بد من ذكر ملك البائع أو ما يدلُّ عليه. انتهى. وفي «البزازيّة»: إن كان المبيع في يد البائع تقبل من غير ذكر ملك البائع، وإن كان في يد غيره والمدعي يدعيه لنفسه إن ذكر المدعي وشهوده أنَّ البائع يملكها، أو قالوا : أسلمها إليه، وقال: سلّمها إلي، أو قال : قُبِضَت، وقالوا : قبض، أو قال : ملكي اشتريتها منه وهي لي تقبل، فإن شهدوا على الشراء والنقد ولم يذكروا القبض ولا التسليم ولا ملك البائع ولا ملك المشتري لا تقبل الدعوى ولا الشهادة، ولو شهدوا باليد للبائع دون الملك اختلفوا. انتهى.
And if they bring evidence of purchase of a thing, their dates agreeing from another, or only one of them dated, they are equal. And if they bring evidence of purchase of a thing, their dates agreeing from another: i.e., one said 'I bought it from Zayd,' and the other said 'I bought it from Amr,' and they mentioned a date, or only one of them dated, they are equal. The conclusion: if only one of them dated, and they received from one, the one with the time is more entitled; if they received from two, they are equal. As for the second scenario: because when they claimed from a single person, they agreed that ownership is for him; so whoever of them proves receipt from him at a time when no one else rivals him is more entitled. If they did not date, they are equal. If one of them dated and the other did not, the one who dated is more entitled. If one of them had possession, he is more entitled, unless the other dated and did not prove that his date is earlier. Thus in 'Ramz al-Haqa'iq' by al-Ayni. His saying: 'And if they bring evidence...' etc.; meaning if one brings evidence of purchase from Zayd, and the other from Bakr, and their dates agree, they are equal, so the sold item is divided between them equally; because each one established ownership for his seller, and the ownership of his seller is absolute without a date, so it becomes as if the seller appeared and claimed absolute ownership, so it is between the outsiders due to equality of their dates. Likewise if only one dated; because the dating of one does not indicate priority of ownership, since it is possible that the other is earlier, unlike the case when the seller is one; because if they agree on ownership, it is only received from him; so if one proves a date, it is judged by it, until it becomes clear that the other preceded him in purchase of something else. Then know that testimony of purchase is not accepted unless they testify that he bought it from so-and-so and that he owned it. Thus in 'al-Bahr' citing 'Khizanat al-Akmal'. And in 'al-Siraj al-Wahhaj': Testimony of purchase from so-and-so is not accepted unless they testify that he sold it to him and at that time he owned it, or they testify that this claimant bought it from so-and-so for such amount, paid the price, and it was delivered to him; because a person may sell what he does not own, as he might be an agent or a transgressor, so the buyer does not acquire ownership by that; thus mention of the seller's ownership or what indicates it is necessary. End. And in 'al-Bazzaziyya': If the sold item is in the hand of the seller, it is accepted without mention of the seller's ownership; if it is in the hand of another and the claimant claims it for himself, if the claimant and his witnesses mention that the seller owns it, or they said: he delivered it to him and said 'Deliver it to me,' or said 'It was taken' and they said 'He took it,' or said 'My property, I bought it from him and it is mine,' it is accepted. If they testified to purchase and payment but did not mention delivery, nor seller's ownership, nor buyer's ownership, the claim and testimony are not accepted. If they testified to the hand of the seller without ownership, they differed. End.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the legal ruling regarding two claimants who each produce evidence of purchasing a specific item from two different sellers. The commentator explains that if both have evidence with the same date, they are considered equal and the item is divided between them. This is because each claimant establishes ownership for his seller, and the seller's ownership is absolute and undated, so it is as if the sellers themselves claimed ownership—neither is preferred. If only one claimant dates his purchase, and both claim from the same seller, the one with the date is preferred; but if they claim from different sellers, they remain equal because the dating does not prove priority over the other's seller. The passage then shifts to important conditions for accepting testimony of purchase: witnesses must explicitly state that the seller owned the property at the time of sale, otherwise the testimony is insufficient because a person might sell what he does not own (e.g., as an agent). If the item is in the seller's hand, testimony is accepted without such detail. If the item is in another's hand, the claimant must have witnesses testify to the seller's ownership or to delivery of possession. The commentary cites 'al-Siraj al-Wahhaj' and 'al-Bazzaziyya' for these nuanced conditions.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically testimony of two male witnesses or one man and two women, capable of establishing a claim in court.
خارج (kharj): A person who is not in possession of the disputed item; an outsider claiming ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two claimants both present evidence of purchasing the same item from different sellers. The dates on both testimonies are identical. How should the judge rule?
A) The judge awards the item to the claimant whose seller is of higher moral character.
B) The judge divides the item equally between the two claimants.
C) The judge awards the item to the claimant who can produce additional witnesses.
D) The judge rejects both claims and leaves the item with the current possessor.

Key Takeaway
Equal evidence of purchase from different sellers with equal dates leads to equal shares; testimony must include seller's ownership unless item is in seller's hand.

The Possessor's Hand Preponderates in Non-Recurring Ownership Disputes
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidence for non-recurring ownership causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi majority view on conflicting evidence for non-recurring causes of ownership and evaluate Isa ibn Aban's alternative position.

Core Idea
When both parties present evidence on a non-recurring cause, the possessor's hand tips the balance.

This passage tackles a nuanced scenario in judicial evidence: when both an outsider and a possessor bring proof on a cause that cannot logically occur for both—like a camel being born to each. The commentator explains why the majority holds that the possessor wins by decree, while Isa ibn Aban argues the evidence cancels out. Understanding this reveals how Hanafi jurisprudence balances textual proof with physical possession (yad).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Dispute
The dispute is about a non-recurring cause of ownership (e.g., birth of an animal, making cheese). Both parties produce evidence that the item originated with them. Since the cause cannot be true for both, how should the judge decide?
Non-recurring means it cannot repeat; e.g., a single animal cannot have two mothers.
2
State the Majority Hanafi Position
The majority holds that the possessor (dhu al-yad) is given judgment because his evidence is preponderated by his physical possession. Even though the evidence is on a cause not indicated by the hand, the hand tips the scale.
The ruling applies whether the possessor's evidence comes before or after judgment for the outsider.
3
Analyze Isa ibn Aban's Objection
Isa ibn Aban argues that since the judge is certain one party is lying (the cause cannot be true for both), the two evidences cancel out (tahaatur al-bayyinatan). The item remains with the possessor only by default, not by judicial decree.
The practical difference: under Isa's view, the case is left undecided; under majority, a decree is issued.
4
Examine the Hanafi Response to the Objection
The response is that testimony on birth is not based on witnessing actual parturition but on seeing the young following the mother. Both parties may have relied on an apparent cause (sabab zahir), so the testimonies are not necessarily contradictory. Hence, the judge can accept both as plausible and then use possession to prefer the possessor.
This reasoning keeps the testimonies valid rather than dismissing them as irreconcilable.
5
Identify the Practical Outcome (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
The majority view results in a judicial decree in favor of the possessor. Isa ibn Aban's view leaves the item with the possessor but without a decree (like a default retention). The commentator endorses the majority as the correct (sahih) view.
This affects the certainty of ownership and the availability of future claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن بَرْهَنَ خارج على الملك، وذو اليد على الشراء منه أسلمها إليه، وقال: سلّمها إلي، أو قال : قُبِضَت، وقالوا : قبض، أو قال : ملكي اشتريتها منه وهي لي تقبل، فإن شهدوا على الشراء والنقد ولم يذكروا القبض ولا التسليم ولا ملك البائع ولا ملك المشتري لا تقبل الدعوى ولا الشهادة، ولو شهدوا باليد للبائع دون الملك اختلفوا. انتهى.
قوله : فإن برهن... الخ ؛ يعني لو أقام الخارج البينة على أنه ملك هذا الشيء وأقام ذو اليد على أنه اشتراه من الخارج، فصاحب اليد أولى بأن يقضى له ؛ لأنَّ الخارج وإن كان يثبت أوّليّة الملك فذو اليد يتلقى الملك منه ، ولا تنافي فيه، فصار كما إذا أقر بالملك ثم ادعى الشراء منه.
وكذا لو أقام الخارج ذو اليد بينة على سبب ملك لا يتكرر، كما إذا برهنا كلّ واحد منهما على أنّ هذه الدّابة نتجت وولدت عنده ، أو على أن اللبن له وملكه ، حلب في يده من شاته، أو على أنَّ هذا الجبن له صنعه في ملكه، أو على أنّ هذا اللبدله صنعه في ملكه، أو على أنه قطع هذا الجز من غنمه ، فصاحب اليد أولى، سواء أقام صاحب اليد بينةً على دعواه قبل القضاء بها للخارج أو بعده، أما قبله فظاهر. وأما بعده ؛ فلأنّ ذا اليد لم يعتبر مقضيّاً عليه ؛ لأنَّ بينته في نفس الأمر دافعةً بينةَ الخارج ؛ لأن الكلام في سبب لا يتكرّر ، فإذا ظهرت بينة دافعة تبين أن الحكم لم يكن مستنداً إلى حجّة فلا يكون معتبراً، وإنما كان صاحب اليد أولى ؛ لأنَّ البينة قامت على ما لا تدل عليه اليد، وهو أوليّة الملك بالنتاج ، فاستوت البينتان، وترجحت بينةُ ذي اليد باليد، فيقضى له، وهذا هو الصحيح.
وقال عيسى بن أبان : إنه يتهاتر البينتان، ويترك المتنازع فيه في يد صاحب اليد، لا على طريق القضاء ؛ لأنَّ القاضي تَيَقّنَ بكذب أحد الفريقين ؛ لأنَّ نتاج دابة من دابتين غير متصور، فصار كأنهما لم يقيما بينة ، ولو لم يقيما بينة يقضى لصاحب اليد قضاء ترك.
والجواب : أن الشهادة على النتاج ليست بمعاينة الانفصال من الأم بـل بـرؤية الفصيل يتبع الناقة، وكلُّ واحدٍ من الفريقين اعتمد سبباً ظاهراً لأداء الشهادة، فيجب
If an outsider brings evidence of ownership, and the possessor brings evidence of purchase from him, he delivered it to him, and said: 'Deliver it to me,' or said: 'It was taken,' and they said: 'He took it,' or said: 'My property, I bought it from him and it is mine,' it is accepted. If they testified to purchase and payment but did not mention delivery, nor seller's ownership, nor buyer's ownership, the claim and testimony are not accepted. If they testified to the hand of the seller without ownership, they differed. End.
His saying: 'If he brings evidence...' etc.; meaning if the outsider establishes by evidence that he owns this thing, and the possessor establishes that he bought it from the outsider, then the possessor is more entitled to have judgment for him; because even though the outsider proves primary ownership, the possessor receives ownership from him, and there is no contradiction, so it is as if he acknowledged ownership then claimed purchase from him.
Likewise, if the outsider and the possessor both bring evidence on a non-recurring cause of ownership, as if each of them brings evidence that this camel was born and gave birth in his possession, or that the milk is his property and was milked in his hand from his goat, or that this cheese was made by him in his property, or that this felt was made by him in his property, or that he cut this portion from his sheep, then the possessor is more entitled, whether the possessor brought evidence on his claim before judgment for the outsider or after. As for before, it is obvious. As for after, because the possessor is not considered a party against whom judgment was passed; because his evidence is in fact negating the outsider's evidence; since the discussion concerns a non-recurring cause, when rebutting evidence appears, it becomes clear that the judgment was not based on a proof, so it is not considered. And the possessor was more entitled because the evidence was on what the hand does not indicate, i.e., primary ownership by birth; so the two evidences are equal, and the evidence of the possessor is preponderated by the hand, so judgment is given for him, and this is the correct view.
Isa ibn Aban said: The two evidences cancel each other out, and the disputed thing is left in the hand of the possessor, not by way of judgment; because the judge is certain that one of the two groups is lying, since the birth of a single animal from two mothers is inconceivable, so it is as if they did not produce evidence. If they had not produced evidence, judgment would be given for the possessor as a judgment of leaving.
The answer: Testimony on birth is not based on seeing the separation from the mother, but rather on seeing the young one following the she-camel. Each of the two groups relied on an apparent cause for giving testimony, so it is necessary...
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where an outsider brings evidence that he owns a certain item, and the person currently in possession of that item brings evidence that he purchased it from that outsider. The commentator first clarifies that in this situation, the possessor is given preference because his evidence shows that he derived ownership from the outsider, which is not contradictory to the outsider's claim—just as if the outsider had confessed to ownership and then claimed the possessor bought it from him. The passage then discusses cases where the cause of ownership is non-recurring, meaning it cannot logically be true for both parties. Examples include a camel's offspring, milk, cheese, felt, or a portion of sheep. In such cases, both parties present evidence that the item originated with them (e.g., the camel was born in their possession). Since the cause cannot be true for both, the evidences are considered equal, but the possessor's hand gives preponderance to his evidence, so he wins. This applies whether the possessor's evidence is presented before or after a judgment for the outsider. The commentator then presents the opposing view of Isa ibn Aban, who argues that the two testimonies cancel each other out because the judge knows one party is lying—since one animal cannot have two mothers. Therefore, the item remains with the possessor not by judicial judgment but by default (like a judgment of leaving). The commentator responds by explaining that testimony about birth is not based on direct observation of parturition but on seeing the young following the mother, which provides a plausible external basis for each party's testimony. This response supports the earlier ruling that the possessor's evidence is preferred by his possession.

Key Terms
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The person who has physical possession of the disputed item; the possessor.
نتاج (nitaj): Offspring; the birth of an animal. In this context, a non-recurring cause of ownership that cannot be true for two claimants.
تهاتر البينتان (tahaatur al-bayyinatan): The two pieces of evidence cancel each other out, so neither is given weight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over a camel's offspring, both the outsider and the possessor bring evidence that the camel was born in their possession. What is the ruling according to the preferred (sahih) view?
A) The outsider wins because he presented evidence first.
B) The possessor wins because his evidence is supported by his possession (yad).
C) The judge rejects both testimonies and the camel is left in the possession of the current holder without a judgment.
D) The camel is divided equally between the two claimants.

Key Takeaway
When evidence on a non-recurring cause is equal, the possessor's hand provides the decisive preponderance, giving him the item by judicial decree.

The Wall Belongs to the Owner of the Beams
Disputed wall ownership rule with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for disputed walls based on beams, explain the Hanafi proof using custom and analogy, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions.

Core Idea
The wall belongs to the owner of the beams because usage (tasarruf) outweighs mere attachment (yad).

When two parties dispute ownership of a wall, one having placed beams on it and the other only planks or a screen, which one prevails? The Hanafi school relies on the sign of actual use: beams are placed for the wall's purpose, not planks. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi proof based on custom and analogy, contrasts it with the Shafi'i objection, and explores the nuanced rules when beam counts differ or when one party connects their own wall.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claimed rights
One party has beams (jidha') on the wall; the other has planks (maradi), a screen (sutrah), or a physical connection (ittisal). Determine who has tasarruf (active use) and who has yad (mere possession).
Beams are functional for supporting a roof; planks are merely placed on top.
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its basis
The wall goes to the owner of the beams. The proof is custom (urf): buildings are built for beams, not for planks. Also analogies: household goods suitability and the animal with load vs. hanging jug.
This is istihsan overriding qiyas.
3
Examine the Shafi'i objection
Al-Shafi'i argues that beams could be via loan or usurpation, so they are not conclusive evidence. Only probability exists.
Shafi'i requires stronger proof (bayyinah).
4
Trace the Hanafi response and analogy
The owner of beams is the musta'mil (user); the planks owner is muta'alliq (attacher). Usage indicates ownership strongly. Custom supports that walls serve beams.
Analogy with dispute over animal: load (himl) vs. hanging jug (kuz).
5
Apply the rule to sub-scenarios
If one has 3+ beams and the other has fewer, the wall is for the owner of 3 (istihsan). If both have 3, it's half. If beams vs. connection, tasarruf (beams) prevails over yad (connection).
Intra-Hanafi development: Abu Hanifa initially held qiyas (equal shares for less than 3) then retracted to istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحائط لمن جذوعُهُ عليه حكماً، ولو كبر وادعى الحريَّة لا يقبل قولُه ، فلا تزول يدُ مَن هو في يده إلا بدليل ؛ لأنه ظهر عليه الرق في حال صغره، فلا ينقض ذلك إلا بالبينة، كما صرحوا به). [1]قوله : لا يجوز أن يشهد ... الخ؛ فيه نظر؛ لأنه قد صرّح في (كتاب الشهادة) في هذا الكتاب، وفي جميع الكتب بأنَّ الرجلَ إذا رأى صبياً لا يعبر عن نفسه جاز له أن يشهد أنه له، على أنه لا بد له على نفسه بحسب الشرع، فيكون لصاحب اليد، فتأمل فيه.
[٢] قوله : والحائط... الخ ؛ صورة المسألة أنَّ الحائط لرجل عليه جذوع، أو هو متصل ببنائه، وللآخر عليه خشبات توضع على الجذوع ، فذلك الحائط يكون لصاحب الجذوع، والاتصال دون المرادي، وقال الشافعي : لا يرجح بوضع الجذوع ؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يكون ملكه أو عارية أو غصباً ، فلا يكون حجة مع الاحتمال.
ولنا : إن صاحب الجذوع هو المستعمل ، وصاحب المرادي صاحب تعلق ، والبناء يبنى للجذوع عادة، لا لوضع المرادي، فوضع الجذوع علامة ملكه، ومثل هذا يصلح أن يكون علامة كما إذا اختلف الزوجان في متاع البيت حتى جعل الترجيح بالصلاحية كما تقدم ذكره، فصار نظير دابَّةٍ تنازعا فيها ؛ ولأحدهما حمل عليها، وللآخر كوز معلق بها، حيث تكون الدابةُ لصاحب الحمل لا لصاحب التعلّق كما عرفت.
وإن كان لأحدهما عليه جذوع ، وللآخر سترة أو حائط فالمتنازع فيه وهو الأسفل لصاحب الجذوع، والسترة لصاحب السترة بمنزلة سفل لرجل عليه علو لآخر، ولا يؤمر صاحب السترة برفع السترة إلا أن يثبت مدعي الحائط استحقاق الحائط بالبينة، فحينئذ يؤمر صاحب السترة برفعها.
وإن كان لأحدهما على الحائط المتنازع فيه جذوع ، وللآخر اتصال بهذا الحائط من جانب واحد، فعندنا : صاحب الجذوع أولى. كذا في فتاوى قاضي خان»، وفيه أيضاً : لو كان لأحدهما جذع أو جذعان دون الثلاث، وللآخر عليه ثلاثة أجذاع، أو أكثر.
ذكر في «النوازل»: إنَّ الحائط يكون لصاحب الثلاث، ولصاحب ما دون الثلاث موضع جذعه، قال: وهذا استحسان، وهو قول أبي حنيفة له وأبي يوسف له آخراً، وقال أبو يوسف : القياس أن يكون الحائط بينهما نصفين، وبه كان أبو حنيفة له يقول أولاً ثم رجع إلى الاستحسان. انتهى. وإن شئت زيادة التفصيل فارجع إليه، فإنَّ فيه شفاء لكل عليل). ولو كان لكل واحد منهما عليه جذوع ثلاثة ، فهو بينهما؛ لاستوائهما، ولا معتبر بالأكثر منها بعد الثلاثة، ولو كان لأحدهما جذوع، وللآخر اتصال، فالأول أولى، ويروى الثاني أولى.
أقوى.
وجه الأول أن لصاحب الجذع التصرف، ولصاحب الاتصال اليد، والتصرف أقوى.
ووجه الثاني : إنَّ الحائطين بالاتصال يصيران كبناء واحد، ومن ضرورة القضاء له ببعضه القضاء بكله. كذا في «الهداية».
And the wall is for the one whose beams are upon it as a ruling. And if he grows up and claims freedom, his statement is not accepted, so the hand of the one in whose hand he is does not cease except by evidence; because slavery appeared upon him in his childhood, so that is not invalidated except by proof, as they have explicitly stated. [1] His statement: 'It is not permissible to testify... etc.'; there is consideration in it; because he has explicitly stated in (the Book of Testimony) in this book, and in all books, that if a man sees a child who cannot express himself, it is permissible for him to testify that he is his, on the basis that he is necessarily subject to him according to the law, so he belongs to the possessor of the hand, so ponder. [2] His statement: 'And the wall... etc.'; the description of the issue is that the wall is for a man upon whom are beams, or it is connected to his building, and another has wooden planks placed on the beams; so that wall is for the owner of the beams, and the connection is beneath the planks. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not given precedence by the placement of the beams because it could be his property or a loan or usurpation, so it is not evidence with probability. And for us: The owner of the beams is the user, and the owner of the planks is the possessor of attachment, and the building is customarily built for beams, not for the placement of planks, so the placement of beams is a sign of his ownership. And such a thing is suitable to be a sign, just as when the spouses differ over the household goods, preference is given by suitability as previously mentioned. So it became similar to an animal about which there is dispute; one has a load on it, and the other has a jug hanging on it, where the animal belongs to the owner of the load, not to the owner of the hanging, as you know. And if one has beams on it, and the other has a screen or wall, then the object of dispute, which is the lower part, is for the owner of the beams, and the screen is for the owner of the screen, like a lower floor for a man with an upper floor for another. And the owner of the screen is not ordered to remove the screen unless the claimant of the wall proves his entitlement to the wall by evidence; then the owner of the screen is ordered to remove it. And if one has beams on the disputed wall, and the other has a connection to this wall from one side, then according to us, the owner of the beams is more entitled. Thus in Fatawa Qadi Khan. And in it also: If one has one or two beams, less than three, and the other has three beams or more, it is mentioned in al-Nawazil: The wall is for the owner of three, and the owner of less than three has the place of his beam. He said: This is istihsan, and it is the saying of Abu Hanifa for him, and of Abu Yusuf for him later. And Abu Yusuf said: By analogy, the wall should be between them half and half, and that was the saying of Abu Hanifa initially, then he retracted to istihsan. End. And if you want further detail, refer to it, for indeed it contains healing for every ill. And if each of them has three beams on it, then it is between them equally because they are equal, and consideration is not given to more than three. And if one has beams and the other has connection, the first is more entitled, and it is narrated that the second is more entitled. The first is stronger. The reasoning for the first is that the owner of the beam has disposal (tasarruf), and the owner of connection has possession (yad), and disposal is stronger. And the reasoning for the second is that the two walls by connection become like one building, and it is necessary that judgment for part of it entails judgment for all of it. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah, discussing the rule that a disputed wall belongs to the owner of the beams placed upon it. The commentator begins by affirming the ruling: the wall is for the owner of the beams, and if a child who was a slave grows up and claims freedom, his claim is not accepted without evidence because the initial presumption of slavery from childhood is not overturned without proof. The commentator then addresses a remark about testimony: while the source text said it is not permissible to testify based on hand alone, the commentator notes that in the Book of Testimony it is stated that one can testify that a mute child belongs to the possessor of the hand, because legally the child is under his control. Thus, the hand on a child does indicate ownership in this context.

The core issue is the wall: a wall with beams from one person and planks on those beams from another—the wall belongs to the owner of the beams. Imam al-Shafi'i objects, saying the beams could be from ownership, loan, or usurpation, so they are not conclusive evidence. The Hanafi response ("Lana") is that the owner of the beams is the actual user (musta'mil), while the owner of the planks has mere attachment (ta'liq). The building is customarily built to support beams, not for attaching planks, so the beams are a sign of ownership. The commentator draws an analogy: just as in a dispute between spouses over household goods, preference is given to the one for whom the item is suitable, and just as a disputed animal with a load (himl) belongs to the owner of the load rather than someone who hung a jug (kuz) on it, here the beams are like the load. The principle is that actual use (tasarruf) indicates ownership more than mere attachment (yad).

The passage then covers additional scenarios: if one has beams and the other has a screen (sutrah) or a separate wall above, the lower part (the main wall) is for the owner of the beams, and the screen belongs to its owner. The screen owner is not forced to remove it unless the wall claimant proves his right. Then the intra-Hanafi dispute: if one has less than three beams and the other has three or more, according to al-Nawazil, the wall goes to the owner of three beams, and the other only retains the spot of his beams. This is istihsan, reported from Abu Hanifa ultimately and from Abu Yusuf later. Abu Yusuf had said qiyas demands equal shares, but Abu Hanifa initially agreed then retracted to istihsan. If each has exactly three beams, the wall is shared equally, and more than three does not matter. Finally, if one has beams and the other has a connection (ittisal) from one side, there are two narrations: the first (beams owner) is stronger because the beams owner has tasarruf (active use) while the other only has yad (passive possession), and tasarruf is stronger; the second narration (connection owner) is based on the idea that connected walls become like one building, so giving part implies giving all—this latter is from al-Hidayah.

Throughout, the commentator uses rational proofs (istihsan, qiyas) and references to earlier Hanafi authorities (Fatawa Qadi Khan, al-Nawazil, al-Hidayah).

Key Terms
جذوع (judhu' (pl. jidha')): Large beams or rafters placed on a wall to support a roof or structure.
مرادي (maradi): Planks or wooden slabs placed on top of beams, typically used for roofing or flooring.
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference deviating from qiyas due to custom, necessity, or equity.
اتصال (ittisal): Physical connection of two walls by being attached side by side, making them function as one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over a wall, if one person has three beams on it and another has only one beam, what is the Hanafi ruling according to istihsan?
A) The wall is divided equally between them.
B) The wall goes to the owner of the three beams, and the other only gets the spot of his beam.
C) The wall goes to the owner of the single beam because he is the first user.
D) The wall belongs to neither until a judge decides.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule for disputed walls: the owner of the beams prevails because custom indicates that walls are built for beams, and active use (tasarruf) outweighs mere attachment (yad).
