Three Cases of Birth Timing After Sale and Lineage Claim
Commentary on three scenarios of birth relative to sale and their legal effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three time frames for birth relative to sale, identify which cases establish lineage and which do not, and explain why the third case is treated as a claim of emancipation.

Core Idea
A seller's paternity claim is accepted only if birth occurs within two years of sale; beyond that, it becomes a void claim of emancipation.

When a slave woman gives birth after being sold, the timing of the birth determines whether the seller's claim of paternity is accepted. This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya' explains three possible time intervals and their legal effects. Understanding these intervals is crucial for determining the status of the child and the mother, and for distinguishing between a valid claim of lineage and a mere claim of emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three time intervals
The commentary divides birth relative to sale into three periods: less than half a year (6 months), between half a year and two years, and more than two years.
Half a year (6 months) is the minimum human pregnancy duration according to Hanafi school.
2
Determine the default ruling for each interval
For birth within 6 months and between 6 months and 2 years, the seller's claim is accepted, the sale is void, and the child is free. For birth after 2 years, the claim is rejected unless the buyer affirms.
The acceptance in the first two intervals depends on the possibility of conception occurring before the sale.
3
Analyze the reasoning for the third case
Birth after 2 years cannot be attributed to the seller's ownership because the maximum pregnancy is 2 years. Hence, the claim is not one of lineage but of emancipation (da'wat tahrir).
Only the owner can emancipate; the seller is no longer owner.
4
Understand the effect of buyer's affirmation in the third case
If the buyer affirms, lineage is established but the child remains the buyer's slave and the mother does not become umm walad, because conception is deemed to occur after sale.
Affirmation creates lineage but does not retroactively make the mother an umm walad.
5
Apply the rules to a new scenario
Given a specific time of birth, walk through the steps: identify interval, determine acceptance, deduce consequences for child and mother.
Practice consolidates the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى النسب
وهي أُم وَلَدِهِ نكاحاً
القسمُ الأَوَّلُ: ما إذا ولدت لأقل من نصف حول من زمان البيع. والثاني : ما إذا وَلَدَتْ لأكثر من نصف حول وأقل من سنتين.
والثالث : ما إذا وَلَدَتْ لأكثر من سنتين .
ففي القسم الثاني : يثبت نسبهُ وأُمِّيتها ، ويفسخ البيع، ويردُّ الثَّمن كما في القسم الأول، (وهي أُم وَلَدِهِ نكاحاً) : أي أم الولد نكاحاً : هي أمةٌ وَلَدَتْ من زوجها فملكها الزوج ، أو أمةٌ ملكها زوجها فولَدَتْ فادعى الولد، وهاهنا يُحْمَلُ
على هذا.
فلم يوجد المصحح فيه بيقين ، فلم تصح إلا بتصديق المشتري ، فإن صدقه ثبت النسب لتصادقهما فيه، ويبطل البيعُ لاستنادِ العلوقِ إلى ما قبل البيع ؛ لإمكانه، فيتبين أنه باع أم ولده ويكون الولدُ حُراً ، والأم أم ولد له تبعاً للولد.
ولو ولدت لأكثر من سنتين من وقت البيع ردت دعوة البائع إلا إذا صدقه المشتري، فيثبت النسب منه ، ويحملُ على أن البائع استولدها بحكم النكاح حملاً لأمره على الصلاح، ويبقى الولد عبداً للمشتري ، ولا تصير الأمة أم ولد للبائع ؛ لأنَّ العلوق حادث بعد البيع، ولا يستند إلى ما قبل البيع حتى يبطل، فكانت هذه الدعوة دعوة تحرير، فلا ينفذ إلا في الملك، إذ لا يقدر غير المالك على التحرير. [١] قوله : وهي أم ولده على المعنى اللغوي أي أنها كانت زوجته، وأنت منه بولد، وليست أم ولد له بالمعنى الاصطلاحي، وهي من استولدها في ملكه لما تقدم من تيقن أنها في غير ملكه نكاحاً : أي إنّ المشتري أنكحهـا لـه بعـد أن دخلت في ملكه واستولدها، ويعطي الولد حكم ولد أمة الغير المنكوحة، فيكون للمشتري والنسب ثابت من البائع، ويبقى الولد عبداً ، فهو كالأجنبي إذا ادّعاه ؛ لأنه بتصادقهما أنَّ الولد من البائع لا يثبتُ كون العلوق في ملكه ؛ لأنَّ البائع لا يدعي ذلك.
وكيف يدعي والولد لا يبقى في البطن أكثر من سنتين، فكان حادثاً بعد زوال ملك البائع، وإذا لم يثبت العلوق في ملك البائع لا يثبت حقيقةُ العتق للولد، ولا حقَّ العتق للأمة، ولا يظهر بطلان البيع، ودعوى البائع هاهنا دعوى تحرير وغير المالك
ليس بأهل لها كما ذكرناه مفصلاً.
Book of Claims, Chapter of Claiming Lineage. And she is his 'umm walad' by marriage. The first section: when she gives birth at less than half a year from the time of sale. The second: when she gives birth at more than half a year and less than two years. The third: when she gives birth at more than two years. In the second section: his lineage and her status as umm walad are established, the sale is annulled, and the price is returned as in the first section. ('And she is his umm walad by marriage'): i.e., umm walad by marriage: she is a slave woman who gave birth from her husband and then the husband owned her, or a slave woman whom her husband owned and she gave birth and he claimed the child, and here it is interpreted in this way. So the corrective element is not found with certainty, so it is not valid except with the buyer's affirmation. If he affirms him, lineage is established due to their mutual affirmation, and the sale is void because the conception is attributed to before the sale due to its possibility, so it becomes clear that he sold his umm walad and the child is free, and the mother becomes his umm walad following the child. If she gives birth more than two years from the time of sale, the seller's claim is rejected unless the buyer affirms him; then lineage is established from him, and it is interpreted that the seller impregnated her by marriage, interpreting his matter favorably, but the child remains a slave of the buyer, and the slave woman does not become umm walad for the seller because conception occurred after the sale and is not attributed to before the sale to annul it. So this claim is a claim of emancipation, which is only effective when one owns, since only an owner can emancipate. [1] His saying: 'And she is his umm walad' in the linguistic sense means that she was his wife and you have a child from him, but she is not his umm walad in the technical sense, which is one who gives birth in his ownership due to the previous certainty that she was not in his ownership. 'By marriage' means that the buyer married her to him after she entered his ownership and he impregnated her, and the child is given the ruling of a child of another's slave woman who is married; so the child belongs to the buyer and lineage is established from the seller, but the child remains a slave. It is like a stranger if he claims him, because their mutual affirmation that the child is from the seller does not establish that conception occurred in his ownership, as the seller does not claim that. How can he claim when the child does not stay in the womb more than two years, so it occurred after the seller's ownership ended. If conception is not established in the seller's ownership, then neither the actual freedom of the child nor the right of freedom for the mother is established, nor does the invalidity of the sale become apparent. The seller's claim here is a claim of emancipation, and a non-owner is not qualified for it, as we have explained in detail.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling regarding the claim of lineage when a seller sells a slave woman who later gives birth. The text presents three possible time frames for birth relative to the sale: less than half a year, between half a year and two years, and more than two years. In the first two cases, the seller's claim of paternity is accepted if the conception could have occurred before the sale. The sale is annulled, the child is free, and the mother becomes an umm walad (mother of the child) following the child. In the third case, if the birth occurs more than two years after the sale, the claim is automatically rejected because the minimum pregnancy duration according to the Hanafi school is two years; if the buyer confirms the claim, lineage is established but the child remains a slave and the mother does not become umm walad because the conception is deemed to have occurred after the sale. The commentary further explains that when the buyer affirms the claim, it is treated as a claim of emancipation (tahrir), which only the owner (the buyer) can effect. The key distinction is whether the conception is attributed to the seller's ownership or not.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): In this passage, 'umm walad' refers to a slave woman who gives birth to her master's child, either by marriage or by concubinage. The technical sense requires the conception to have occurred while she was in his ownership.
علوق (ʻuluq): Conception or impregnation – the point at which the child is conceived. The timing of ʻuluq determines whether the child is attributed to the seller or buyer.
دعوة تحرير (daʻwat tahrīr): A claim that is essentially a declaration of emancipation – i.e., the claimant asserts that the person is free because of some prior event, but this claim is only effective if the claimant is the owner at the time the claim relates to.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi madhhab, if a seller sells a slave woman and she gives birth more than two years after the sale, and the buyer affirms the seller's paternity claim, what is the status of the child?
A) The child is free and the mother becomes umm walad for the seller.
B) The child remains a slave of the buyer and the mother does not become umm walad.
C) The sale is annulled and the seller gets the child.
D) The child is free and belongs to the seller.

Key Takeaway
A seller's claim of paternity after selling a slave woman is accepted only if birth occurs within two years of sale; beyond that, it is treated as a non-owner's claim of emancipation.

Seller's Paternity Claim and Annulment of Transactions
Ruling on seller's paternity claim after subsequent sale and similar transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a seller's paternity claim after selling a child born in his possession and distinguish which transactions are annulled and which are not.

Core Idea
A seller's paternity claim annuls revocable transactions but not irrevocable ones like emancipation.

This lesson examines a scenario where a seller claims paternity of a child sold earlier. The Hanafi school rules that the claim is valid and certain transactions are nullified. Understanding the distinction between revocable and irrevocable transactions is key to applying this ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the seller's claim of paternity after selling a child who was born in his possession. The ruling is that the claim is accepted (sahih) and the sale is annulled.
The passage states: 'ولو باع من وُلِدَ عنده ... صح نسبه وَرُدَّ بيعه' – 'if one sells a person who was born in his possession ... his lineage is valid and his sale is annulled.'
2
Recognize the essential condition
The claim is only valid if the original conception occurred while the child was in the seller's ownership. If conception was in another's ownership, the claim becomes a claim of emancipation (tahrir), not lineage.
The commentary specifies: 'أما إذا كان العلوقُ عند غيره والوضع عنده فهي دعوة تحرير' – 'if the conception was with another and the birth with him, it is a claim of emancipation.'
3
List revocable transactions that are annulled
The seller's claim also annuls other revocable transactions: kitabah (contract of manumission), rahn (pawn), ijarah (lease), and tazwij (marriage) of the slave woman. All these can be nullified because they are 'revocable' (تحتمل النقض).
The passage includes: 'وكذا لو كاتب الولد أو الأم، أو رَهَن، أو أجر، أو زوجها' – 'Likewise, if he executed kitabah on the child or the mother, or pawned, or leased, or married her.'
4
Identify irrevocable transactions not annulled
Emancipation (itq) and tadbir (promise of freedom after death) are irrevocable and cannot be annulled by the paternity claim. The claim does not override these because they are not subject to annulment (لا يحتملان النقض).
The passage states: 'بخلاف الإعتاق والتدبير، فإنّهما لا يحتملان النقض' – 'unlike emancipation and tadbir, for they cannot be annulled.'
5
Apply the rule if the buyer claims first
If the buyer claims paternity first during his ownership, that lineage becomes established and cannot be annulled. It takes precedence over a later claim by the seller, similar to how emancipation overrides the seller's right.
The passage notes: 'بخلاف ما إذا ادّعاه المشتري أولاً ثم ادعاه البائع حيث لا يثبتُ النسب من البائع' – 'unlike the case where the buyer first claims him and then the seller claims him, because the lineage established from the buyer cannot be annulled.'

And if one sells a person who was born in his possession, then claims him after the sale by his buyer, his lineage is valid and his sale is annulled. Likewise, if he executed kitabah (contract of manumission) on the child or the mother, or pawned, or leased, or married her, then claimed him, the claim is valid regarding both the mother and the child, and these transactions are nullified, and the female slave is returned to the seller. Know that the wording of Al-Hidayah is likewise: 'And whoever sells a slave who was born in his possession, and the buyer sells him to another, then the first seller claims him, he is his son and the sale is void because the sale can be annulled, and his right of claim cannot be annulled, so the sale is annulled for its sake. Similarly, if he executed kitabah on the child, or pawned him, or leased him, or executed kitabah on the mother, or pawned her, or married her, then the claim was made.' His saying: 'If one sells...' means if a man sells a slave who was born in his possession, and the original conception was in his ownership, and the buyer sells him to another, then the first seller claims him, he is his son because the conception was in his ownership; it is like evidence testifying for his claim. But if the conception was with another and the birth with him, it is a claim of emancipation, as they clarified. His saying: 'And his sale is annulled' because the sale can be annulled and his right of claim cannot be annulled, so the sale is annulled for its sake. His saying: 'And he nullifies these transactions' from kitabah, pawn, lease, and marriage; the secret in it is that this compensation can be annulled, so all that is annulled, and the claim of the seller is valid, unlike emancipation (itq) and tadbir (contract of manumission after death), for they cannot be annulled. Also unlike the case where the buyer first claims him and then the seller claims him, because the lineage established from the buyer cannot be annulled, so it becomes like his emancipation, which takes precedence over the seller's right. His saying: 'Know...' the purpose of quoting Al-Hidayah is to raise the issue regarding the reference of the pronoun in his saying 'he executed kitabah' and repelling it by different references. His saying: 'born in his possession' means the original conception was in his ownership. (1) Al-Hidayah (3:177).
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a specific scenario: when a seller sells a person who was born in his possession (i.e., the conception occurred while the child was his property), and then after a subsequent sale (the buyer resells), the original seller claims paternity. The claim is accepted, the lineage is established, and the sale is annulled because the right of claim cannot be annulled while the sale can be. The same applies if the buyer had engaged in other revocable transactions like kitabah (contract of manumission), pawning (rahn), leasing (ijarah), or marriage (tazwij) of the slave woman. All these transactions are nullified because they are revocable. The commentary contrasts this with irrevocable transactions: emancipation (itq) and tadbir (promising freedom after death) cannot be annulled. Also, if the buyer claims the child first during his ownership, that lineage is established and takes precedence over the seller's later claim. The passage includes a quote from Al-Hidayah to illustrate the ruling.

Key Terms
كتابة (kitābah): A contract of manumission between a master and a slave, where the slave agrees to pay a sum in installments to earn freedom.
تدبير (tadbīr): A contract where the master declares that the slave will be free upon the master's death. This is an irrevocable transaction.
يحتمل النقض (yaḥtamil al-naqḍ): Capable of being annulled or rescinded. In this context, certain transactions like sale, kitabah, pawn, lease, and marriage can be nullified by a subsequent paternity claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The marriage remains valid because it is a separate contract.
B) The marriage is annulled because it is a revocable transaction.
C) The marriage becomes valid only if the buyer agrees.
D) The marriage is irrevocable and remains valid.

Key Takeaway
A seller's paternity claim is valid only when conception occurred in his ownership, and it annuls all revocable transactions but not irrevocable ones.

Effect of Paternity Claim on Prior Slave Transactions
Ruling on rescinding transactions due to subsequent paternity claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a paternity claim rescinds rescindable transactions (sale, kitabah, rahn, ijarah, tazwij) but not irrevocable ones (itq, tadbir), and explain the grammatical resolution of the ambiguous pronoun in the key phrase.

Core Idea
A paternity claim rescinds any rescindable transaction, but not irrevocable acts.

This lesson examines a Hanafi ruling on how a subsequent claim of paternity over a slave born in one's possession affects prior transactions. The core principle is that the right of paternity is non-rescindable, so it overrides transactions that are rescindable. However, the text also contains a grammatical ambiguity that the commentator resolves, highlighting the precision required in legal texts.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its scope
Identify the main ruling: if a person sells a slave born in his possession and later claims paternity, the sale is rescinded. This applies to other rescindable transactions (kitabah, rahn, ijarah, tazwij) but not to emancipation (itq) or tadbir.
The basis is that the paternity right is stronger and does not admit rescission.
2
Understand the rationale
Why are some transactions rescinded and others not? Because sale, kitabah, etc., are inherently rescindable (yahtamil al-naqd), while itq and tadbir are irrevocable (la yahtamilan al-naqd).
This is a key distinction in Hanafi law.
3
Identify the grammatical ambiguity
The phrase 'او كاتب الولد او الام' contains a pronoun 'he' that could refer to the buyer (al-mushtari) or the original seller (man ba'a). The commentator shows that both interpretations lead to problems, and resolves it by assigning different references: for the child, the pronoun refers to the buyer; for the mother, it refers to the original seller.
This is an expansion probe: the commentator clarifies the text.
4
Apply the resolution to the overall ruling
Once the pronouns are correctly assigned, the ruling becomes consistent: if the buyer does kitabah for the child, or the seller does kitabah for the mother, both are rescinded upon paternity claim. This maintains the principle that rescindable transactions are overturned.
The commentary also offers an alternative that both pronouns refer to the buyer, with the mother's kitabah being a separate case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اعلم أن عبارة «الهداية كذلك : ومَن باع عبداً وُلِدَ عنده ، وباعَهُ المشتري من آخر ، ثُمَّ ادعاه البائع الأول ، فهو ابنه ، وبطل البيع ؛ لأنَّ البيع يحتمل النقض، وماله من حقِّ الدّعوة لا يحتمله، فينتقض البيع لأجله، وكذلك إذا كاتب الولد، أو رَهَنَه ، أو أجره ، أو كاتب الأم، أو رَهَنَها ، أو زَوْجَهَا، ثُمَّ كانت الدعوة لأنَّ هذه العوارض تحتمل النقض، فينتقض ذلـك كلـه وتـصـح الـدعـوة بخـلاف الاعتاق والتدبير على ما مر .
أقول : ضمير الفاعل في : كاتب ؛ إن كان راجعاً إلى المشتري، وكذا في قوله : أو كاتب الأُم يصير تقدير :الكلام ومَن باعَ عبداً وُلِدَ : عنده ، أو كاتب المشتري الأم، وهذا غير صحيح؛ لأنَّ المعطوف عليه بيع الولد لا بيع الأم، فكيف يصح قوله، أو كاتب المشتري الأم، وإن كان راجعا إلى من في قوله : ومَن باغ عبداً ؛ فالمسألة أن رجلاً كاتب مَن وُلِدَ عنده ، أو رَهَنَه ، أو أجره ، ثُمَّ كانت الدعوة، وحينئذ لا يحسن قولُهُ بخلافِ الإعتاق ؛ لأن مسألة الإعتاق التي مرت ما إذا أعتق المشتري الولد ؛ لأنَّ الفرق الصحيح أن يكون بين إعتاق المشتري وكتابته ، لا بين إعتاق المشتري وكتابة البائع. إذا عرفت هذا فمرجعُ الضمير في كاتب الولد هو المشتري، وفي كاتب الأم من في قوله : من باع .
قوله : أقول : حاصله : إِنَّ ضمير الفاعل في قوله : كاتب الولد وكاتب الأم لا يخلو : إما أن يكون راجعاً إلى المشتري ، أو إلى من في قوله : من باع ، وعلى كلا التقديرين يفسد المعنى، فلا بُدَّ من إرجاعه، بحيث يصح المعنى، وهو أن يرجع الضمير في قوله كاتب الولد إلى المشتري، وفي قوله : كاتب الأم إلى من في قوله : من باع. فحينئذ يكون المعنى سالماً عن الفساد وهاهنا كلام نفيس، وهو أنه يحتمل أن يكون قوله : أو كاتب الأم ؛ إشارة إلى مسألة أخرى ، صدرها بمحذوف لانفهامه من السياق، وهو أنه باع أم مَن وُلِدَ عنده ، وكاتب المشتري فلم يتجه الإيراد باختيار الشق الأول، كما لا يخفى على المتفطن.
ويمكن أن يقال : إنَّ المرجع فيهما المشتري ، وقوله : لأنَّ المعطوف عليه بيع الولد لا بيع الأم، مدفوع بأنَّ المتبادر بيعه مع أمه بقرينة سوق الكلام، ودليل كراهة التفريق بحديث سيد الأنام عليه وعلى آله التحية والسلام.

And if he sells one who was born in his possession, then claims him after the sale by the buyer, his lineage is established, and his sale is revoked. Likewise, if he executes a kitabah contract for the child or the mother, or pawns, or leases, or marries her, then claims him, the claim is valid with respect to both the mother and the child, and these transactions are rescinded, and the slave girl is returned to the seller.
Know that the expression of al-Hidayah is: 'Whoever sells a slave who was born in his possession, and the buyer sells him to another, then the first seller claims him, he is his son, and the sale is invalidated; because the sale is susceptible to rescission, while his right of claim is not susceptible, so the sale is rescinded for its sake. Similarly, if he executes kitabah for the child, or pawns him, or leases him, or executes kitabah for the mother, or pawns her, or marries her, then the claim is made, because these transactions are susceptible to rescission, so all of them are rescinded and the claim is valid, unlike emancipation and tadbir as previously mentioned.'
I say: The subject pronoun in 'kaataba' if it refers to the buyer, and likewise in 'or he executes kitabah for the mother,' the estimation would be: 'and whoever sells a slave born in his possession, or the buyer executes kitabah for the mother' – this is incorrect because the conjunction is with the sale of the child, not the sale of the mother. So how can his statement 'or the buyer executes kitabah for the mother' be correct? And if it refers to the subject in 'whoever sells a slave,' then the case is that a man executes kitabah for one born in his possession, or pawns him, or leases him, then the claim is made – and then his statement 'unlike emancipation' is not proper, because the case of emancipation that passed was when the buyer emancipated the child; for the correct distinction is between the buyer's emancipation and his kitabah, not between the buyer's emancipation and the seller's kitabah. Once you understand this, the reference of the pronoun in 'kaataba the child' is the buyer, and in 'kaataba the mother' is the subject in 'whoever sells.'
His statement: 'I say: its summary: The subject pronoun in 'kaataba the child' and 'kaataba the mother' is not free: either it refers to the buyer, or to the subject in 'whoever sells,' and on both assumptions the meaning is corrupted, so it must be referred in a way that corrects the meaning, namely that the pronoun in 'kaataba the child' refers to the buyer, and in 'kaataba the mother' refers to the subject in 'whoever sells.' Then the meaning is free from corruption. And here there is a fine point: it is possible that his statement 'or he executes kitabah for the mother' refers to another case, whose beginning is omitted because it is understood from context, namely that he sold the mother of one born in his possession, and the buyer executed kitabah – so the objection does not arise by choosing the first option, as is not hidden from the perceptive.
And it can be said that the reference in both is the buyer, and his statement 'because the conjunction is with the sale of the child, not the sale of the mother' is refuted by the fact that what is apparent is the sale of him with his mother, by the context of the speech and the evidence of the prohibition of separation by the hadith of the master of mankind, peace and blessings be upon him and his family.
Yes; the apparent meaning of the expression of al-Wiqayah is to say: considering his statement 'after the sale'...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding the effect of a subsequent paternity claim on prior transactions involving a slave. The ruling states that if a person sells a slave who was born in his possession (meaning the conception occurred while the slave was in his ownership), and later claims the slave as his son, the sale is invalidated. The same applies to other transactions such as kitabah (contract of manumission), rahn (pawn), ijarah (lease), and tazwij (marriage) – all are rescinded upon the establishment of the paternity claim. This is because these transactions are inherently rescindable, whereas the right of paternity is not. However, emancipation (itq) and tadbir (postponed emancipation) are not rescinded because they are irrevocable. The commentary then delves into a textual problem in the base text: the pronoun in 'or he executes kitabah (kaataba) the child or the mother' is ambiguous. The commentator resolves this by clarifying that for the child, the pronoun refers to the buyer (i.e., the one who purchased the slave), while for the mother, it refers to the original seller (the one who sold the slave). This resolution is necessary to make sense of the text and to maintain consistency with other rulings. The commentary also discusses an alternative interpretation that both pronouns refer to the buyer, supported by the principle of avoiding separation of a mother and child (as per a hadith). Ultimately, the relied-upon position is that the transactions are rescinded except for emancipation and tadbir, and the pronoun reference is resolved as above.

Key Terms
دعوى النسب (da'wa al-nasab): The legal claim of paternity; asserting that a slave is one's biological son.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract between master and slave whereby the slave is to be emancipated upon payment of a sum of money by installments.
نقض (naqd): Rescission or invalidation of a transaction due to a stronger competing right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following transactions is NOT rescinded by a subsequent paternity claim over a slave born in one's possession?
A) Sale of the slave
B) Kitabah (manumission contract)
C) Emancipation (itq)
D) Lease (ijarah)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on paternity claims over slaves born in one's possession rescinds all rescindable prior transactions but not irrevocable acts like emancipation.

Child of a Reclaimed Slave-Girl: Hanafi Ruling
Ruling statement with commentary expansion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a purchased slave-girl is reclaimed after giving birth to the buyer's child, and apply it to similar scenarios by identifying the key conditions and consequences.

Core Idea
The child of a reclaimed slave-girl is born free; the father must pay its value to the rightful owner.

In this passage, the Hanafi school addresses a unique scenario: a man buys a slave-girl, she bears his child, but later the slave-girl is reclaimed by her true owner. Does the child become a slave? The answer reveals a key principle: the child's freedom is not affected by the mother's defective title, but the father must compensate for the value. Let's dissect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their rights
Clearly identify the buyer (father), the slave-girl (mother), the child, and the reclaimant (true owner). Understand that the buyer possessed the slave-girl under a defective title (invalid sale).
The right of the reclaimant is to recover the slave-girl and claim compensation for any loss, including the child's value.
2
Determine the child's legal status
According to the text, the child is declared free (hurr). This is because the child was conceived while the father believed the mother to be his property, and the child's origin is freedom.
The child does not become a slave even though the mother is reclaimed.
3
Determine the father's liability
The father must pay the value of the child at the time of the lawsuit (yawm yukhāṣam). This compensates the reclaimant for the loss of the child's potential service (as a slave).
The father pays from his own property; if he dies, the value is taken from his estate.
4
Expand to analogous cases
The commentary extends the ruling: it applies whether the slave-girl was acquired by purchase, gift, or any other cause. Also, it applies if the man married a woman thinking she was free but she was actually a slave and gave birth.
The underlying principle is that the child's freedom is protected regardless of how the father acquired the mother.
5
Note the inheritance consequence
If the father dies before paying, the value is taken from the children's estate (i.e., the father's overall inheritance), but not from the child's own property, because the child is originally free.
This reinforces that the child is not liable for the value—the debt is on the father.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ولدت أمة مشرية، وادعى المشتري الولد، واستحقت غرم الأب قيمة الولد يوم يخاصم، وهو حر. قوله : ولو ولدت.... الخ؛ صورة المسألة : إنَّ رجلاً اشترى أمةً فولدت ولداً من ماء المشتري عنده ثم استحقها مستحق ضَمِنَ المشتري قيمة الولد، والولد حر، وكذا إذا ملكها لسبب آخر غير الشراء : أي سبب كان، وكذا إذا تزوجها على أنها حرّة فولدت له ثم استحقت ، صرح به الزَّيْلَعِي ، وغيره ، فعلى هذا لو قال : ولو ملك أمةً بأي سبب كان لكان أشمل. قوله : غرم الأب قيمة الولد ; ولا يغرم الولد ، حتى لو كان الأب ميتاً تؤخذ من تركةِ الأولادِ للمستحق عليه ؛ لأنه عَلِق حرّ الأصل، وإنما قدر الرق ضرورة القضاء بالقيمة، فلا تعدو محلها.
And if a purchased slave-girl gives birth, and the buyer claims the child, and she is reclaimed, the father is liable for the value of the child on the day of the lawsuit, and the child is free. The commentator says: 'And if she gives birth...' The picture of the issue: A man buys a slave-girl, she gives birth to a child from the buyer's semen while with him, then she is reclaimed by a rightful owner; the buyer guarantees the value of the child, and the child is free. Similarly, if he owned her by any other cause, and similarly if he married her thinking she was free and she gave birth to him then she is reclaimed, as stated by al-Zayla'i and others. So if it were said: 'And if he owned a slave-girl by any cause' it would be more comprehensive. Commentary
This passage presents a ruling: when a man buys a slave-girl, she gives birth to a child who is the offspring of the buyer, and then the slave-girl is reclaimed by a rightful owner (i.e., she was unlawfully sold), the buyer must pay the value of the child to the reclaimant, and the child is declared free. The commentary expands that this applies regardless of whether the man acquired the slave-girl by purchase or any other means, and also if he married her thinking she was free. The child is free from birth (حر الأصل), and the liability for the value is on the father, not the child. If the father dies, the value is taken from the children's inheritance, but not from the child's own property. The reasoning is that slavery is imposed only to the extent necessary for the judgment of value, and does not affect the child's original freedom.

Key Terms
أمة مشرية (amatun mushriyyah): A slave-girl who has been purchased (by the defendant).
استحقاق (istihqāq): Reclamation of property (here the slave-girl) by the rightful owner, invalidating the buyer's possession.
قيمة الولد (qīmat al-walad): The monetary value of the child at the time of the lawsuit, to be paid by the buyer.

The Hanafi school protects the freedom of a child born to a wrongfully held slave-girl, imposing compensation on the father rather than enslaving the child.

Child of a Claimed Slave Girl: Free or Slave?
Ruling on child when purchased slave girl is claimed by rightful owner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the child born to a purchased slave girl later claimed by the rightful owner, explain the father's liability to pay the child's value, and apply the rule to determine when compensation is due.

Core Idea
The child is free but the father pays its value to the owner — compensation, not enslavement.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi sales law where a man buys a slave girl, she bears his child, and then a third party proves rightful ownership. The text presents the ruling that the child is free by origin, but the buyer must compensate the owner for the child's value at the time of litigation. We will explore the reasoning behind this ruling, including the principle of shubha (apparent right) and the use of qiyas with borrowed property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Scenario
Understand the facts: a man purchases a slave girl, she gives birth to his child, and then the original owner claims her. The buyer had a reasonable belief of ownership (shubha).
The scenario also includes marriage thinking she is free.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
The child is free; the father must pay the child's market value to the claimant. The value is assessed on the day of litigation, not at birth or purchase.
If the child dies before payment, no compensation is owed.
3
Locate the Evidence (Dalil)
The text cites a hadith of Umar (رضي الله عنه) and uses qiyas with the offspring of usurped property. Understand how these proofs support the ruling.
The hadith is interpreted to mean payment of value, not exchange of children.
4
Analyze the Reasoning (Ta'lil)
The father is obliged because he caused the child's existence under a valid shubha; the child is free by origin, so the owner's right shifts to value. The child's death ends liability because the child was never truly owned by the claimant.
The principle: the child is a trust (amana) like offspring of borrowed property.
5
Apply to Variations
The same rule applies if the man owned the slave girl by another means or married her thinking she was free. Also, if the father dies, the value is taken from his estate.

Source Text

And if a purchased slave girl gives birth, and the buyer claims the child, and then the slave girl is claimed by the rightful owner, the buyer must pay the value of the child on the day of litigation, and the child is free. (And the value of the child is considered on the day of litigation) because it is the day of prevention and transfer from the physical thing to the value; because when it was held as a slave in the right of the master, his right was in the physical child, and it is transferred to value by judgment, so its value at the time of transfer is considered.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal scenario where a man purchases a slave girl, she gives birth to his child, and then a third party proves rightful ownership of the slave girl (istihqaq). The ruling is that the child is free by origin, but the buyer (the father) must pay the value of the child to the rightful owner at the time of litigation. This is based on the principle that the child is the offspring of a deceived party (maghrur) — the buyer had a valid reason to believe the slave girl was his (purchase or marriage) and is therefore excused, but the rightful owner's right must be protected. The compensation is a valuation at the time of the lawsuit because that is when the owner's right is denied. If the child dies before payment, the father owes nothing, because the child was never truly owned by the claimant; the obligation to pay value was a substitute that ceases with the child's death. The passage also discusses that the same rule applies if the man owned the slave girl by any other means or married her thinking she was free. The proof includes istihsan and qiyas with the case of a usurper or borrowed property, as well as a hadith from Umar (رضي الله عنه) which is interpreted to mean payment of value, not specific exchange.

Key Terms
مغرور (maghrur): A person who has intercourse with a woman believing in his right (ownership or marriage) but later it is invalidated.
قيمة الولد (qimat al-walad): The market value of the child, determined at the time of litigation (yawm al-khusuma).
استحقاق (istihqaq): Claiming rightful ownership; here, a third party proves that the slave girl was rightfully theirs, invalidating the buyer's ownership.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the child of a purchased slave girl later claimed by the rightful owner is free, but the father must pay its market value at the time of litigation as compensation.

Father's Liability and Buyer's Recourse for Value of Claimed Child
Ruling and commentary on father's liability and buyer's recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the father's liability for the claimed child's value, explain the proof (hurr al-asl), and apply the buyer's right to recover the value but not the 'aqr from the seller.

Core Idea
The father's liability arises from preventing the substitute; the buyer recovers the value, not the 'aqr.

When a child born to a slave-girl is claimed by another man, complex liability questions arise. If the father kills the child or receives blood money after another kills him, he must pay the child's value to the claimant. Moreover, the buyer of the slave-girl can recover that value from the seller—but only the value, not the 'aqr (compensation for intercourse). This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The father pays the child's value to the claimant if he kills the child or if someone else kills and he actually receives the blood money. Prevention of the child triggers liability.
The child is considered free from the father's side; this establishes inheritance and liability.
2
Analyze the proof: hurr al-asl
The text states 'because he is originally free' – meaning the child is free in relation to the father, so the father inherits and is liable. The child is only deemed a slave for the claimant by necessity to allow taking value.
Legal fiction (ضرورة) is limited to its need.
3
Note the condition of actual receipt
If the father is awarded blood money but does not receive it, he is not liable because prevention has not occurred. Actual receipt is necessary for liability.
From al-Mabsut: prevention is realized only when the substitute reaches the hand.
4
Explain the buyer's recourse against the seller
The buyer can recover the value paid to the father from the seller because the sale includes the child, and the seller implicitly guarantees the sold item's safety from claims. The price is safe for seller, so the sold item must be safe for buyer.
The seller is like a guarantor for the child's freedom from claims.
5
Differentiate between value and 'aqr
The buyer cannot recover the 'aqr from the seller because the 'aqr is compensation for the benefit of intercourse, which is not part of the sold item. The seller does not guarantee that benefit.
The 'aqr is a substitute for the enjoyment of the vulva, not for the child.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى النسب
وتركته له ، فإن قتله أبوه أو غيرُهُ غُرِّمَ الأب قيمته ، ورجع بها كثمنها على بائعه لا بالعقر
( وتركته له ) ؛ لأنَّه حُرُّ الأصل، فإن قتله أبوه أو غيرُهُ غُرِّمَ الأب قيمته ، ورجَعَ بها كثمنها على بائعه لا بالعقر)، إن قتلَهُ الأب يضمن قيمته للمستحق، وكذا إن قتلَهُ غيرُه ، فأخذ الأب ديته ، فإن الدِّيةَ بدلّ له، فسلامة البدل للأب كسلامة الولد ، ثُمَّ مَنْعُ البدلِ من المستحق كمنع الولد، وفيه القيمة، ويرجع" بقيمته على البائع كما يرجع بثمنها ، ولا يرجعُ بالعقر الذي أَخَذَ منه المستحق ؛ لأنه بدلُ استيفاء منفعة البضع. (۱) قوله : لأنه حر الأصل ؛ يعني في حق أبيه فيرثه أبوه، لا يقال : إنّ هذا الولد كما هو حر في حق أبيه كذلك رقيق في حق مدعيه ، فَلِمَ لم يقسم تركته بينهما ؛ لأنا نقول: هو حر في حق مدعيه أيضاً، ألا ترى أن ولاءه ليس لمدعيه، وإنما جعلناه رقيقاً في حقه ضرورة القضاء له بالقيمة، وكلُّ ما ثبت بالضرورة يتقدر بقدرها.
[٢]قوله : فأخذ الأب ديته ؛ قيّد بالأخذ ، ذكر في «المبسوط» فإن قضى له بالدية فلم يقبضها لم يأخذ بالقيمة ؛ لأنَّ المنع لا يتحقق فيما لم يصل إلى ليدها من البدل، فإنَّ قبض من الدية قدر قيمة المقتول قضى عليه بالقيمة للمستحق؛ لأنَّ المنع يتحقق بوصول يده إلى البدل، فيكون منعُهُ قدر قيمة الولد، كمنعه للولد. كذا في «الكفاية»). [٣]قوله : ويرجع ... الخ ؛ يعني ويرجع المشتري على أبيه بقيمة الولد التي ضمنها ، لأنَّ البيع كما يرجع المشتري بثمن الجارية ؛ لأنه صار كفيلا بما شرط عليه من البدل ؛ مبني على مساواة البدلين في حكم الضمان، فلما كان الثمن من جانب المشتري سالماً للبائع وجب أن يكون المبيع سالماً للمشتري. وذلك بأن يجعل البائع كفيلاً بسبب تملك البدل، فصار كأنه قال للمشتري : إنَّ الحكم قد ثبت لك، فإن ضَمِنَك أحدٌ بدعوى باطل فأنا ضامن لك بما ضَمِنَك ؛ ولأنَّ البائع التزم سلامتها عن العيب إذ المعاوضة تقتضي ذلك ، ولا عيب فوق الاستحقاق، عليه.
(۱) (الكفاية (٧: ٢٩٤ - ٢٩٥).
Book of Claims, Chapter of Claim of Lineage. And his estate (i.e., the child's) is for him (the father), then if his father or someone else kills him, the father is liable for his value, and he recovers it like its price from his seller, not the 'aqr. (And his estate is for him) because he is originally free. If his father or someone else kills him, the father is liable for his value to the claimant. Similarly, if someone else kills him, and the father takes his blood money, then the blood money is a substitute for him, so the safety of the substitute for the father is like the safety of the child. Then preventing the substitute from the claimant is like preventing the child. In it is the value. And he recovers its value from the seller just as he recovers its price. And he does not recover the 'aqr which the claimant took from him because it is a substitute for the enjoyment of the vulva. (1) His statement: 'Because he is originally free' means in respect to his father, so his father inherits from him. Let it not be said: This child, as he is free in respect to his father, likewise he is a slave in respect to his claimant. So why is his estate not divided between them? Because we say: He is free also in respect to his claimant. Do you not see that his wala' is not for his claimant? Rather, we made him a slave in respect to him by necessity of adjudicating the value for him. And everything established by necessity is limited to its extent. [2] His statement: 'Then the father takes his blood money' is restricted to taking. It is mentioned in al-Mabsut: If he is adjudicated the blood money but does not take it, he does not take the value because prevention is not realized as long as the substitute has not reached his hand. If he takes from the blood money the amount of the value of the killed, he is adjudicated the value for the claimant because prevention is realized by the arrival of the substitute to his hand. So his prevention is an amount equal to the value of the child, like his prevention of the child. Thus in al-Kifaya. [3] His statement: 'And he recovers... etc.' means the buyer recovers from the father the value of the child which he (the buyer) guaranteed because the sale is like the buyer recovers the price of the slave-girl because he became a guarantor for what was stipulated for him as a substitute, based on the equality of the two substitutes in the ruling of guarantee. Since the price from the buyer's side is safe for the seller, it is necessary that the sold item be safe for the buyer. And that is by making the seller a guarantor due to his ownership of the substitute. So it is as if he (the seller) said to the buyer: 'The ruling has been established for you. If someone sues you with a false claim, then I am a guarantor for you for what he sued you.' And because the seller undertook its safety from defect since exchange necessitates that, and there is no defect above the claim. (1) (al-Kifaya 7:294-295).
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (talil) from the Hanafi school on the liability of the father when his child (born to a slave-girl) is claimed by another. The base text states that the child's estate belongs to the father because the child is originally free (حر الأصل) from the father's side. If the father kills the child, he must pay its value to the claimant; if someone else kills the child, the father takes the blood money, but if he receives it, he prevents the child from the claimant, so he must pay its value. The father can then recover the value from the seller of the slave-girl, just as he would recover the price if the slave-girl herself were claimed. However, he cannot recover the 'aqr (compensation for intercourse) that the claimant took from him, because the 'aqr is a substitute for the benefit of sexual intercourse, which is not part of the sold item—so the seller is not liable for its safety. The commentary elaborates: the child is free only in relation to the father—if he were a slave, the father would not inherit—but he is also free in relation to the claimant; he is deemed a slave only to allow the claimant to take his value. This is a legal fiction (ضرورة) limited to that purpose. Furthermore, if the father receives the blood money, he must pay the value only after actual receipt, because prevention of the child only occurs after the substitute reaches his hand. The buyer's recourse against the seller is based on the principle of guarantee in exchange: since the seller received the price safely, he must ensure the sold item is safe from encumbrances; thus, if a third party successfully claims the child, the seller compensates the buyer as if he guaranteed the safety.

Key Terms
حر الأصل (hurr al-asl): Free from the original perspective; the child is considered free in relation to the father.
عقر (ʿaqr): Compensation paid for intercourse with a slave-woman, usually to her owner.
مستحق (mustahaqq): The claimant who proves the child is his.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the father not recover the 'aqr from the seller?
A) Because the 'aqr is a substitute for the benefit of intercourse, which is not part of the sale.
B) Because the father is not a party to the sale.
C) Because the 'aqr is less than the value.
D) Because the seller is not liable for any claims.

Key Takeaway
The father's liability for the claimed child's value is contingent on actual receipt of the substitute; the buyer recovers only the value, not the 'aqr, from the seller.

Buyer's Recourse for 'Aqr and Gift Children: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Commentary contrasting Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on buyer's recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between commutative and gratuitous contracts in the buyer's recourse for 'aqr and children's value, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A buyer cannot recover 'aqr or children's value from a donor because 'aqr compensates intercourse and a donor is a benefactor not liable for mere deception.

When a purchased slave-girl is claimed by a third party, the buyer may lose not only the price but also the 'aqr (a fine for intercourse) and the value of any children born to her. The Shafi'i school allows the buyer to recover these from the seller or donor, but the Hanafi school restricts such recourse. This lesson unpacks the two issues: the 'aqr and the children's value from a donor, examining the legal reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Recognize that the passage addresses two separate issues: (1) recovery of the 'aqr from the seller, and (2) recovery of the value of children from a donor (gift, charity, bequest). Distinguish between them.
The 'aqr is a payment for intercourse; the children's value is a separate claim against the donor.
2
State the Hanafi position for each
The Hanafi view: the buyer cannot recover the 'aqr from the seller because it is compensation for a benefit not part of the sale. Regarding children, the buyer cannot recover value from the donor because the donor is a benefactor (muhsin) and mere deception (ghurur) does not create liability.
The principle 'not on the benefactor is any way' (Quran 9:91) is cited.
3
Identify the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the buyer can recover both: the 'aqr because the seller failed to ensure safety, and the children's value because the donor deceived the recipient by claiming ownership.
Shafi'i considers ghurur sufficient for recourse in these contexts.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
For the 'aqr, the Hanafi rebuttal uses a qiyas (analogy): if the buyer recovered the 'aqr, the intercourse would be free, which is impermissible. For the gift case, Hanafis argue that a donor is a benefactor and Qur'an exempts them; only commutative contracts (like sale) impose a guarantee of safety.
The text also distinguishes marriage from gift: marriage's purpose is procreation, so if that fails, recourse is allowed; a gift's purpose is generosity and ownership, which is achieved without children.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi law, the buyer bears the loss of the 'aqr and cannot reclaim the children's value from the donor, whereas under Shafi'i law the buyer can recover these amounts.
This affects the allocation of risk in property transactions involving slaves and gifts.

تناكحوا توالدوا تكثروا... الحديث.

Similarly, if it (the slave-girl) dies in the possession of the buyer, and the claimant makes him liable for her value and the value of the children, he (the buyer) does not recover from the seller the price or what he is liable for of the children's value. Nor does he recover from him the value of the slave-girl, because taking her value from him is like taking her actual self, and in that case he only recovers the price. So is this. Al-Ayni and al-Zaylai and others stated it clearly.
And the buyer does not recover the 'aqr which the claimant took from him, because it is a substitute for the enjoyment of the vulva, and it is not part of the sold item. So the seller is not liable for its safety, thus he does not recover it from the seller. According to al-Shafi'i: He does recover, because it is a liability that befell him due to the loss of safety.
The answer: The 'aqr is a compensation for what was enjoyed of the seller's benefits. So if he recovered it, the enjoyment would be free for him, and intercourse with another's property is not permissible to be rendered free. And he does not recover from the donor, the charity-giver, or the legator anything of the value of the children. According to al-Shafi'i: He does recover, because deception (ghurur) has been realized from him (the donor) by his making the ownership valid for him and his informing him that she was his property.
The answer: Mere deception is not sufficient for recourse. For if someone informs a person that this road is safe, and he takes it, and thieves take his property, he does not recover anything from the informer. Unlike a sale, for it is a commutative contract that necessitates safety or guarantee as we have explained. This is a gift, and he is a benefactor, and there is no way (of liability) against the benefactor. And unlike marriage, for it is founded on seeking children and procreation according to the hadith: 'Marry, beget, multiply...' So if what is intended (procreation) is not achieved, he recovers from the one who deceived him. But the purpose of a gift is to show generosity and munificence and to confirm ownership, and this purpose is achieved without procreation. Thus in al-Minah and al-Tabyin and others.

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute presenting two issues: (1) the buyer's recovery of the 'aqr from the seller, and (2) the buyer's recovery of children's value from a donor (gift, charity, bequest). The Hanafi position is clear: the buyer does not recover the 'aqr because it is compensation for the benefit of intercourse, not part of the sale. The seller is not liable for its safety. The Shafi'i view is that the buyer can recover it because the loss results from the seller's fault. The Hanafi response uses a qiyas: if the buyer could recover the 'aqr, the intercourse would be free, which is impermissible. Secondly, regarding gifts: the buyer cannot recover the value of children from the donor because the donor is a benefactor (muhsin) and not liable under the principle 'not on the benefactor is any way' (Quran 9:91). Mere deception (ghurur) is insufficient for recourse; only commutative contracts like sale impose a guarantee of safety. The text further distinguishes marriage from gift: in marriage, the purpose is procreation, so if that fails, the deceived spouse can claim compensation; but the purpose of a gift is generosity and ownership, which is achieved even without children, so no recourse lies.

Key Terms
غرور (ghurur): Deception; misleading someone into believing something false.
تبرع (tabarruʿ): Gratuitous contract; a gift or donation without exchange.
محسن (muhsin): Benefactor; one who does good without obligation.

B) Because the 'aqr is a fine that the state takes.
C) Because the buyer already recovered the price.
D) Because the seller is not liable for any claims.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a buyer cannot recover the 'aqr or children's value from a donor because these are not part of the sale or are protected by the principle of benefactor immunity.

Interpretation of Ambiguous Numbers in Iqrar: Qiyas vs. Istihsan
Hanafi ruling on interpreting 'one hundred and a dirham' etc.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for interpreting ambiguous numbers in iqrar and distinguish between measurable and non-measurable items.

Core Idea
Istihsan overrides qiyas: a dirham interprets one hundred; for non-measurables, qiyas remains.

When someone declares a debt with an ambiguous number followed by a specific item, how do we determine the exact obligation? The Hanafi school uses istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom to interpret the specific item as clarifying the ambiguous number for commonly traded measurables. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its analogical basis, and the distinction from non-measurable items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous number and specific item
Locate the ambiguous number (e.g., 'one hundred') and the specific item (e.g., 'a dirham') connected by 'wa' (and). This is the basic scenario.
The phrase 'one hundred and a dirham' is the classic example.
2
Classify the specific item as measurable or not
Determine if the item is from the muqaddarat (measured by weight or volume) like dirhams, wheat, or oil, or non-measurable like garments, sheep, or slaves.
Measurable items are those that are commonly established in the dhimma (liability) through frequent transactions.
3
Apply the Hanafi istihsan rule
If the item is measurable, by istihsan the specific item interprets the ambiguous number. The total becomes the number of that item (e.g., 100 dirhams) plus the additional single item (1 dirham). If non-measurable, the ambiguous number remains ambiguous and must be interpreted separately (qiyas).
The ruling for measurable items is from zahir al-riwayah; for non-measurables, the qiyas stands.
4
Handle two ambiguous numbers with a single specifier
If someone says 'one hundred and three garments', where there are two ambiguous numbers (100, 3) followed by a specifier without 'wa', the specifier interprets both numbers by consensus. This applies regardless of measurability.
This is because both numbers are equally ambiguous and custom uses the specifier for both.
5
Recognize the Shafi'i disagreement and the Hanafi response
Al-Shafi'i holds that the specific item never interprets the ambiguous number because conjunction ('wa') does not imply interpretation; the number remains ambiguous. The Hanafi school responds that custom (istihsan) overrides qiyas in frequent transactions.
The practical difference: Hanafi interprets '100 and a dirham' as 101 dirhams; Shafi'i leaves the 100 to be interpreted by the court.

Source Text

And one hundred and a dirham: all of it is dirhams. And in 'one hundred and a garment' and 'one hundred and two garments': the one hundred is interpreted (by the garment). And 'one hundred and three garments': all are garments. Know that in his statement: 'So-and-so owes me one hundred and a dirham' according to al-Shafi'i, the one hundred is interpreted as in 'I owe one hundred and a garment', and that is the analogical ruling. And likewise everything that is measured by volume or weight; so if he said: 'He owes me one hundred and a qafiz of wheat', he is liable for one hundred qafiz and a qafiz of wheat. And if he said: 'I owe one hundred and a garment' or he said: 'I owe one hundred and two garments', the interpretation of the one hundred is required, so he is liable for one garment in the first case, and two garments in the second case by agreement, because the one hundred is ambiguous, and the garment is conjoined to it, not an interpretation, because the conjoined item is not placed to interpret the conjoined-to item, and it is not of the type of sufficiency, as in 'one hundred and a dirham'. And if he said: 'He owes me one hundred and three garments', then all is garments, so he is liable for garments in all, because he mentioned two ambiguous numbers, and after them he mentioned a specifier without 'waw', so it applies to both, due to their equal need for interpretation for a single number by conjunction. [2] His statement: 'That is the qiyas'; its summary: The qiyas is that the interpretation of the one hundred refers to him, in his statement 'one hundred and a dirham' as in his statement 'one hundred and a garment' or 'one hundred and two garments', and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, because the conjunction of a specific upon an ambiguous in both cases, and the conjoined is not the conjoined-to, and it is not placed for clarification, so the one hundred remains ambiguous as in the conjunction of the garment upon it. [3] His statement: 'And according to us... etc.'; this is istihsan, and its explanation: The conjunction of a weighed or measured item upon an ambiguous number is a clarification of the ambiguous customarily, because people found it burdensome to repeat the interpretation due to frequent usage, and that is in what is dealt with in transactions, which is what is established in the dhimmah, namely the measured and weighed items, and they sufficed with its mention once due to frequency of its causes and its circulation in speech. Unlike garments and other non-measured items, because transactions with them are not frequent due to their not being established in the dhimmah in all transactions, so they did not find it burdensome to mention them due to the infrequency of their circulation in speech, and the sufficiency with the second due to frequency was not present, so it remained on qiyas. Unlike his statement 'one hundred and three garments', where the garments are also an interpretation of the one hundred, and it applies equally to measured and non-measured items, because he mentioned two ambiguous numbers and followed them with an interpretation, so it applies to both, so it is garments for both, and this is by consensus, because their custom is such. Do you not see that they say: 'twenty-one garments' and 'fifty-three dirhams', so the interpretation applies to both due to their equal need? And in 'al-Nihayah': Ibn Sam'ah narrated from Abu Yusuf, may Allah be pleased with him, regarding his statement 'one hundred and a garment': that all is garments, and likewise regarding 'one hundred and a sheep', and its reasoning: because garments and sheep are divided as a single division, unlike slaves, for they are not divided as a single division, and what is divided as a single division is realized in their homogeneous numbers, so it is possible to make the interpreted part an interpretation of the ambiguous. And this is not apparent, because according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) slaves are divided like sheep, and they are not divided according to Abu Hanifah. So it is in 'al-Tabyin' and others.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof concerning the interpretation of ambiguous numbers in declarations (iqrar). The author explains the Hanafi position on what happens when someone says 'I owe you one hundred and a dirham' or similar phrases. The key principle is based on istihsan (juristic preference) due to custom (urf). The passage first states the ruling: if someone says 'one hundred and a dirham,' all is dirhams—the dirham interprets the ambiguous hundred. This is by istihsan because people customarily use the specific item to explain the number. In qiyas (analogy), the hundred would remain ambiguous and be interpreted by the opponent, as al-Shafi'i holds. The Hanafi school distinguishes between measurable items (like weight and volume) and non-measurable items (like garments). For measurable items, the second mention interprets the first due to frequent use. For non-measurable items, the qiyas remains. However, when two ambiguous numbers are followed by a single specifier without 'wa' (as in 'one hundred and three garments'), the specifier interprets both numbers by consensus. The passage also includes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf (in one narration) holds that even for garments, the whole is garments, reasoning that garments are divisible like sheep, unlike slaves. This is not the mainstream view; Abu Hanifa and the majority consider the division of slaves differently. Overall, this lesson clarifies the Hanafi methodology of balancing qiyas with custom (istihsan) in contractual language.

Key Terms
مقدرات (muqaddarat): Items that are measured by volume or weight (e.g., dirhams, wheat) that are established in the dhimma (liability).
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas when necessary, here due to custom and necessity.
مبهم (mubham): An ambiguous number that requires interpretation (e.g., 'one hundred' without specification).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says: 'I owe you one hundred and a qafiz of wheat,' what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He owes 100 dirhams and a qafiz of wheat separately.
B) He owes 100 qafiz of wheat and one qafiz of wheat (total 101 qafiz).
C) The hundred is uninterpreted and must be clarified by court.
D) He owes 100 qafiz of wheat only.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, customary practice (istihsan) determines that a specific measured item following an ambiguous number interprets that number, overriding the default qiyas.

Interpreting Ambiguous Numbers in Confession: The Hanafi Rule
Explains how to interpret unspecified numbers when followed by a specific item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule for interpreting a single ambiguous number in a confession when followed by a measured vs. non-measured item, and distinguish the consensus rule for two ambiguous numbers.

Core Idea
A single ambiguous number is interpreted by the following term only if it is a measured item; two ambiguous numbers are always interpreted by the subsequent term.

This lesson examines how Hanafi jurisprudence resolves the ambiguity when someone confesses to a number followed by an item, such as 'a hundred and a garment'. The ruling depends on whether the item is a measured good (muqaddar) like dirhams or wheat, which are frequently transacted on credit, or a non-measured good like a garment. Additionally, when two numbers are mentioned (e.g., 'a hundred and three garments'), a consensus rule applies that the later term interprets both numbers. We will also explore an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the interpretation of 'a hundred and a garment' as narrated from Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ambiguity
In an iqrar (confession), when someone says 'a hundred and a dirham,' the number 'a hundred' is ambiguous because it is not linked to a type. The question is how to interpret it. Does the subsequent term 'dirham' clarify the hundred? Note the passage distinguishes between muqaddarat (measured items) and other items.
2
Apply the analogy (qiyas) for a single ambiguous number
If the item is a measured good like dirham or wheat, the number is interpreted as that good by analogy to cases where a number is followed by another number (e.g., 'a hundred and three garments'). The basis is that measured items are frequently used in credit transactions, so people avoid mentioning the number separately for each. If the item is not a measured good (e.g., a garment), the analogy does not apply, and the hundred remains unspecified.
The analogy is qiyas jaliy based on common commercial practice.
3
Apply the consensus (ijma) rule for two ambiguous numbers
When two ambiguous numbers are given, like 'a hundred and three garments,' the subsequent specific term (garments) interprets both numbers. This is by consensus because common usage shows such expressions, e.g., 'twenty-one garments.' The need is equal for both numbers.
Ijma is a binding proof; this rule applies regardless of whether the item is measured or not.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf (narrated by Ibn Sama'ah) held that in 'a hundred and a garment' or 'a hundred and a sheep,' the whole is of that type, reasoning that garments and sheep are divisible uniformly. However, the author notes this is not the apparent view; according to Abu Hanifah, slaves are not divisible like sheep, so the analogy fails. The relied-upon position is that the hundred remains unspecified unless both numbers are ambiguous.
The dispute hinges on whether the item is divisible (qismah wahidah) and the frequency of transaction.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Yusuf's view, one confessing to 'a hundred and a garment' would owe a hundred garments. Under the relied-upon view, he owes an unspecified hundred (to be determined by other evidence) plus one garment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما إذا قال : مئة ودرهم، ومئة وقفيز حنطة تكون المئة من جنس ذلك المقدر قياساً على ما إذا ذَكَرَ بعد لفظ العدد عدداً آخر نحو مئة وثلاثة أثواب، وإن لم يكن من المقدرات كالثوب مثلاً فحينئذ يفسر المئة. بخلاف الثياب وغيرها مما ليس من المقدّرات؛ لأنها لا يكثر التعامل بها لعدم ثبوتها في الذمة في جميع المعاملات، فلم يستثقلوا ذكرها؛ لقلة دورانها في الكلام، والاكتفاء بالثاني للكثرة، ولم توجد، فبقي على القياس. بخلاف قوله: مئة وثلاثة أثواب، حيث يكون الأثواب تفسيراً للمئة أيضاً، ويستوي فيه المقدّرات وغيرها؛ لأنه ذكر عددين مبهمين، فأعقبهما بتفسير، فينصرف إليهما، فيكون ثياباً لهما، وهذا بالإجماع؛ لأن عادتهم جرت بذلك. ألا ترى أنهم يقولون: أحد وعشرون ثوباً، وثلاثة وخمسون درهماً، فينصرف التفسير إليهما؛ لاستوائهما في الحاجة. وفي «النهاية»: روى ابن سماعة الله عن أبي يوسف له في قوله: مئة وثوب: إنّ الكل من الثياب، وكذا في قوله: مئة وشاة، ووجهه: إنّ الثياب والغنم تُقسم قسمة واحدة، بخلاف العبيد، فإنّهم لا يقسمون قسمة واحدة، وما يقسم قسمة واحدة تتحقق في أعدادها المجانسة، فيمكن أن يجعل المفسر منه تفسيراً للمبهم، وهذا ليس بظاهر، فإنّ عندهما يقسم العبيد كالغنم، ولا يقسمون عند أبي حنيفة. كذا في التبيين وغيره.
As when he says: 'a hundred and a dirham' and 'a hundred and a qafiz of wheat' – the hundred is of the same type as that measured thing, by analogy to when he mentions after the word of number another number, like 'a hundred and three garments', even if it is not from the measured things – for example, a garment – then in that case the hundred is interpreted (by that item). But unlike garments and other things that are not from the measured things, because transactions with them are not frequent due to them not being confirmed in the dhimmah (obligation) in all transactions, so they did not find it cumbersome to mention them (repeatedly), due to their infrequent usage in speech, and the sufficiency of the second (mention) due to frequency is not present, so it remains on analogy. But unlike his saying 'a hundred and three garments', where the garments become an interpretation also for the hundred, and it is equal in measured and non-measured things, because he mentioned two ambiguous numbers, and then followed them with an interpretation, so the interpretation applies to both, and they are garments for both. This is by consensus (ijma), because their custom has been so. Do you not see that they say 'twenty-one garments' and 'fifty-three dirhams', so the interpretation applies to both due to their equal need. In al-Nihayah: Ibn Sama'ah narrated from Abu Yusuf that in his saying 'a hundred and a garment', the whole is from garments, and similarly in his saying 'a hundred and a sheep' – the reason is that garments and sheep are divided in one division, unlike slaves, for they are not divided in one division. And whatever is divided in one division, its homogenous numbers are verified, so it is possible to make the attributed (item) an interpretation for the ambiguous. But this is not apparent, because according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) slaves are divided like sheep, but they are not divided according to Abu Hanifah. Thus in al-Tabyin and others.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on how to interpret ambiguous numbers in a confession (iqrar). The author begins by giving examples: if someone says "a hundred and a dirham," the hundred is interpreted as dirhams because the subsequent specific term clarifies the ambiguous number by analogy to cases where a number is followed by another number like "a hundred and three garments." However, if the item after the number is not a standard measure (like a garment), the hundred is interpreted by that item. The reasoning is based on common practice: for items that are typically subject to sale and loan (like weighed or measured goods), people avoid repeating the term frequently, so they suffice with mentioning it once. For garments, which are less frequently dealt with in such a way, the analogy does not apply. But when two ambiguous numbers are mentioned (e.g., "a hundred and three garments"), the subsequent specific term interprets both, by consensus. The author then reports a view from Abu Yusuf (narrated by Ibn Sama'ah) that in expressions like "a hundred and a garment" or "a hundred and a sheep," the whole is of that type, reasoning that garments and sheep are divisible uniformly (unlike slaves). However, the author notes that this view is not the apparent one, and that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider slaves divisible like sheep, while Abu Hanifah disagrees. Thus the relied-upon position is that the interpretation applies only when both numbers are ambiguous.

Key Terms
الذمة (al-dhimmah): Legal capacity for obligations; items that can be established as a debt in the dhimma are typically weighed or measured goods.
المقدّرات (al-muqaddarāt): Items that are measured by weight or volume, which can be owed as a debt in the dhimma.
الإجماع (al-ijmā'): Consensus of the scholars on the interpretation of two ambiguous numbers followed by a specific term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone confesses, 'I owe you a hundred and a sheep,' what is the ruling according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) He owes a hundred sheep and one sheep (total 101).
B) He owes a hundred unspecified items and one sheep.
C) He owes a hundred sheep only.
D) He owes a hundred dirhams and one sheep.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the interpretation of an ambiguous number in a confession depends on whether the following item is a measured good and whether there is one or two ambiguous numbers.

Acknowledgment of Debt from Price of a Specific Slave
Detailed ruling on acknowledgment conditional on delivery of a specific slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on acknowledgment of a debt tied to a specific slave and identify the conditions under which the debt is binding.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment of debt from a specific slave binds only upon delivery; if unspecified, 'not received' is futile.

This passage from the commentary on 'al-Wiqaya' deals with a common scenario in sales: a buyer acknowledges a debt from the price of a slave but claims non-receipt. The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between specifying the slave and leaving it general. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct fatwa, as it determines whether the debtor can avoid payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acknowledgment scenario
Determine whether the acknowledger specified a particular slave or not. The ruling hinges on this distinction.
Specification means mentioning a concrete, identifiable slave.
2
Check delivery condition
If the slave is specified, the debt is only binding if the acknowledgee delivers that slave to the acknowledger. If not delivered, the debt is not binding by consensus.
Delivery is the condition for obligation.
3
Apply the rule on 'ma qabadtuhu'
If the slave is not specified, the debtor's statement 'I have not received' is considered laghw (futile) according to Abu Hanifa. The debt remains binding regardless.
Laghw means the statement has no legal effect; it does not negate the acknowledgment.
4
Differentiate the four sub-scenarios
The text outlines four cases: (1) confirm and deliver – binding; (2) different cause but same entitlement – binding; (3) acknowledgee denies sale – not binding; (4) claim of different slave – oath-taking.
Each scenario flows from the key distinction between specification and non-specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فإن قال له ...الخ ; يعني فإن قال المقر : لفلان علي ألف درهم من ثمن عبد اشتريته منه ، ولم أقبضه ، فإن عينه بأن ذكر عبداً بعينه وصدقه المقر له في شرائه وعدم قبضه، ه، قيل للمُقَرِّ له : سَلّم العبد إلى المقرّ، وخذ ثمنه منه ، فإن سلم المقر له ذلك العبد المعين بأن يحضره بين يديه، لزم الألفُ على المقر بهذا القيد ; لأنه أقر له بألف على صفة، فيلزمه على الصفة التي أقرَّ بها ، وإن لم يسلّم العبد إلى المقر لا يلزمُهُ الألفُ إجماعاً، وهذه المسألة على وجوه : أحدها : ما ذكره المصنف ، وهو ما إذا صدَّقَه وسَلَّمه إليه، وحكمه ما تقدَّم من لزومِ الألف على المقر بالقيد المذكور ; لأنَّ ما ثبت بتصادقهما يكون كالثابت عياناً. والثاني : أن يقول المقرّ له : إن هذا العبد عبدك ما بعتكه، وإنما بعثك عبداً آخر، وسلّمتُه إليك، والحكم فيه كالأوَّل ; لأنهما اتفقا على ما أقرّ به من أنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ منهما يستحق ما أقرَّ به، غير أنهما اختلفا في سبب الاستحقاق، ولا يبالي باختلافها، ولا باختلاف السبب عند حصول المقصودِ واتحادِ الحكم ، فصار كما إذا أقر له بغصب ألف درهم، فقال المقر له : هي،قرض، فإنّه يؤمرُ بالدَّفع إليه لاتفاقهما على الاستحقاق. والثالث : أن يقول المقوله : إن هذا العبد عبدي ما بعتكه، وحكمه أن لا يلزم على المقر شيء ; لأنه لم يقر بالمال إلا عوضاً عن العبد، فلا يلزمه دونه.
