His statement: 'For he said...' etc.; meaning if the acknowledger says: I owe so-and-so one thousand dirhams from the price of a slave I bought from him and have not received him, and he specifies by mentioning a particular slave and the acknowledgee confirms the purchase and non-receipt, then it is said to the acknowledgee: Deliver the slave to the acknowledger and take his price from him. If the acknowledgee delivers that specified slave by presenting it before him, the thousand becomes binding upon the acknowledger due to this condition, because he acknowledged a thousand with a description, so it becomes binding as per the description he acknowledged. And if he does not deliver the slave to the acknowledger, the thousand is not binding by consensus. This issue has several aspects: First: what the author mentioned, which is when he confirms and delivers it, and its ruling is as previously mentioned of the binding of the thousand upon the acknowledger under the said condition, because what is established by their mutual confirmation is like that established by sight. Second: that the acknowledgee says: This slave is your slave, I did not sell it to you, but I sent you another slave and delivered it to you. The ruling here is like the first, because they agreed on what he acknowledged that each one deserves what he acknowledged, but they differed on the cause of entitlement. Difference does not matter, nor does difference in the cause when the objective is achieved and the ruling is unified. So it becomes like when he acknowledges to him the usurpation of one thousand dirhams, and the acknowledgee says: It is a loan, then he is ordered to hand it over due to their agreement on entitlement. Third: that the acknowledgee says: This slave is my slave, I did not sell it to you. The ruling is that nothing is binding on the acknowledger, because he only acknowledged the money as a substitute for the slave, so it is not binding without him. (1) 'What I have not received' and 'specify it', then if the acknowledgee delivers it, the thousand is binding, otherwise not; and if he does not specify, it is binding, and 'what I have not received' is futile. His statement: 'what I have not received' is a description of the slave, and his statement 'specify it' means specify the slave, and it is in the hand of the acknowledgee. Then if the acknowledgee delivers that to the acknowledger, the thousand is binding, otherwise not. (1) ('And if he does not specify, it is binding, and 'what I have not received' is futile): meaning his statement 'what I have not received' is futile according to Abu Hanifa, whether connected or separate, because denial of possession in non-specified items... And the fourth: that the acknowledgee says: I did not sell you this slave but I sold you another slave. The ruling is that they take an oath, because they differed in the sold item, which necessitates an oath. [1] His statement: 'what I have not received'; he said in al-Munh: 'what I have not received' meaning that slave, connected while 'what I have not received' is connected to his acknowledgment. This condition is explicitly stated in al-Hawi al-Qudsi where he said: If he acknowledges one thousand and then later says 'from the price of a slave I have not received', he is not believed unless he says it connected to his words, pointing to a particular slave. End. The author of al-Kanz and al-Wiqaya omitted this condition, so be mindful of it when giving fatwa. End of what is in al-Munh. I say: There is no need to mention this condition because it is known from what preceded at the beginning of the chapter of his statement: Whoever excludes some of what he acknowledged connectedly, the remainder is binding. Then he mentioned exception and its meanings in branches and did not mention connection, relying on that. Because it is the principle that comes to mind; how could one imagine the inclusion of the disconnected after understanding its non-consideration in the school? So without considering the explicit statement at the start of the chapter, there is no flaw, and the best speech is that which is brief yet indicative, so reflect. [2] His statement: 'And if he does not specify...' etc.; meaning if the acknowledger does not specify the slave, the thousand is binding on the acknowledger. And his statement 'what I have not received' is futile, so this statement is not accepted. (3) His statement: because denial of possession... etc.; its explanation: his statement 'what I have not received' is a retraction of the acknowledgment, for he acknowledged the obligation of the money based on the word 'upon', and denial of possession in non-specified items fundamentally negates the obligation; because ignorance of the sold item is like its destruction, due to inability to deliver the unknown, so it is impossible.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling statement from the commentary on the chapter of acknowledgment (iqrar) concerning a debtor who says, 'I owe him one thousand dirhams from the price of a slave that I bought from him but have not taken possession of.' The commentary explains the conditions and multiple scenarios. If the debtor specifies a particular slave and the creditor confirms, the creditor is ordered to deliver the slave to the debtor; then the debt becomes binding. If the creditor does not deliver, the debt is not binding by consensus. Four scenarios are detailed: (1) The creditor confirms and delivers – debt binding. (2) The creditor claims the slave is the debtor's and he sent another – still binding due to agreement on entitlement. (3) The creditor claims the slave is his own – debt not binding. (4) The creditor claims a different slave was sold – they take oaths. The text then discusses the phrase 'what I have not received' (ma qabadtuhu). According to Abu Hanifa, this phrase is considered futile (laghw) when the slave is not specified, meaning it does not affect the acknowledgment. However, if the slave is specified, the acknowledgment is conditional on delivery. The commentary also addresses the requirement of connection (ittisal) in making an exception, noting that some works omitted it but it is understood from earlier principles.

Key Terms
عبد معين ('abd mu'ayyan): A specific, identified slave; the acknowledgment is conditional on its delivery.
لغو (laghw): Irrelevant, futile; the statement 'what I have not received' has no legal effect in a non-specific acknowledgment.
قبض (qabd): Possession or delivery of the slave; the condition for the debt to become binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an acknowledgment of debt from a sale is conditional on delivery only if the item is specified; otherwise, denial of receipt is futile.

One Son's Confession of a Debt on the Father's Estate
Specific ruling on effect of one heir's confession on inheritance shares

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of iqrar (confession) in inheritance to determine the effect of one heir's confession on the distribution of a debt owed to the deceased.

Core Idea
An heir's confession binds only himself, not his co-heirs.

Confession (iqrar) is a powerful legal tool in Hanafi fiqh. When one heir confesses to having received part of a debt owed to the deceased, that confession affects only his own share. Your task today is to trace how this principle plays out in a simple two-son inheritance scenario, and to understand why the other heir's share remains untouched.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Confession
One of two sons states that his deceased father had a debt (a receivable) owed to him by a third party, and that he (the son) received half of that debt from his father during his lifetime.
The confession is about a specific debt, not the entire estate.
2
Determine the Legal Effect on the Confessor
Apply the Hanafi rule that a person's confession is binding against himself. The confessing son admits he already received half of that debt, so he forfeits his right to any further share of that debt.
Result: the confessing son gets nothing from that debt.
3
Determine the Effect on the Other Heir
The confession does not bind the other son because no one's confession affects another person's rights. The other son retains his full share of the debt (the remaining half) as an heir.
Result: the other son takes the other half.
4
Generalize the Principle
This ruling illustrates a broader Hanafi principle: iqrar (confession) is valid only against the confessor and does not prejudice the rights of others. In inheritance, one heir cannot unilaterally reduce another heir's share through confession.
This is consistent with the Qur'anic principle that no bearer of burdens bears another's burden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ولو أقر أحد ابني ميت له على آخر دين بقبض أبيه نصفه, فلا شيء له, والنصف للآخر )
(And if one of the two sons of a deceased person confesses that his father had a debt owed to another, and that he (the confessing son) received half of it from his father, then he gets nothing, and the other half is for the other [son].)
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This is a specific ruling about a confession made by one of two sons after the father's death. The son confesses that the father owed a debt to a third party, and that he (the son) received half of that debt from the father. The ruling: the confessing son gets nothing from that half (it is as if he admitted it belongs to the creditor), and the other son gets the other half (presumably the other half of the debt). The commentary likely explains that the confession is valid against the confessor himself but not against the other heir (the other son). The exact reasoning is that the confessor's admission that he received half of the debt means he acknowledges that half is not his, so he forfeits his share, but the other son's share is unaffected.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Confession of a debt or right; here a son confesses that his father had a debt and that he received part of it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Both sons share the debt equally
D) The confession is invalid because it harms the other son

Key Takeaway
One heir's confession of having received part of an asset from the deceased only affects his own share; co-heirs' rights remain intact.
